Second Argument: Creation from Nothing
A debate on the meaning of creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between 'creation from nothing' interpreted as temporal beginning versus as denial of pre-existing matter, and how this distinction deflects an argument for temporal creation.

Core Idea
Created from nothing does not automatically mean created in time.

This section presents a crucial semantic debate. Supporters of temporal creation argue that if the world is made from nothing, it must have begun to exist after non-existence — implying a start in time. The supporters of pre-eternity counter that 'from nothing' only denies pre-existing matter, not a lack of temporal beginning. This subtle distinction is essential for understanding the Islamic philosophical debate on the world's origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Observe the concise argumentative structure.
The Arabic uses parallel phrasing for the two positions.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the statement of the supporters of ḥudūth (temporal creation) and the response of the supporters of qidam (pre-eternity). Note how the response reinterprets the premise.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
Ask yourself: What does 'min shay'' (from something) vs. 'min al-'adam' (from nothing) actually mean? How does the response avoid implying a temporal beginning?
This distinction is the heart of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول أنصار الحدوث إذا كان العالم مصنوعاً من العدم، فهو موجود بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً . و يرد أنصار القدم : ليس القصد من المقدم أن العالم مصنوع بعد العدم ، بل إنه ليس مصنوعاً من شيء .
The supporters of ḥudūth say: If the world is made from nothing, then it exists after it did not exist. And the supporters of qidam respond: The point of the premise is not that the world is made after non-existence, but rather that it is not made from something.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a semantic distinction. The supporters of temporal creation interpret 'creation from nothing' as meaning that the world began to exist after a state of non-existence, implying a temporal beginning. The supporters of pre-eternity correct this: 'from nothing' (ex nihilo) does not indicate a temporal sequence; it simply means the world is not composed of pre-existing matter. They deny that 'after non-existence' entails a temporal gap. So the argument is deflected.

Key Terms
عدم ('adam)
مصنوع من العدم (maṣnū‘ min al-‘adam)
من شيء (min shay’)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'creation from nothing' necessarily means the world began to exist at a point in time.
Correction: In this passage, the supporters of qidam argue that it only means the world was not made from pre-existing matter, leaving open the possibility of an eternal world.

Checkpoint

A) They deny that the world is made from nothing.
B) They say that 'after non-existence' does not imply temporal priority.
C) They claim that 'from nothing' means the world has always existed.
D) They argue that non-existence is impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'created from nothing' does not necessarily mean a temporal beginning; it may simply mean not made from pre-existing matter.

The Createdness of Time: Responding to the Dilemma of Eternal Power
Ghazali presents an objection about God's eternal power and responds that time is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that time and duration are created, and how this resolves the philosophical objection about God's power before creation.

Core Idea
Time is not an eternal container; it is created by God, resolving the dilemma of pre-creation delay.

Ghazali presents a powerful objection that might be leveled against his own position: if God could have created the world from eternity but delayed, the duration of waiting must be either finite or infinite, both problematic. His response is to challenge the assumption that time is eternal. Instead, he asserts that time itself is created, so the dilemma dissolves. In this lesson, we examine this argument closely and see how Ghazali uses the createdness of time to defend orthodox Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: 'If God was able to create from eternity but waited, the duration of waiting must be finite or infinite. If finite, God's existence has a beginning; if infinite, an infinite number of possibilities have elapsed.' Note the two horns of the dilemma.
The objection targets the Ash'ari view that God's will is eternal but creation is temporal.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Ghazali asserts that 'duration and time are created.' Consider how this statement dissolves the dilemma. If time is created, there is no pre-creation timeline over which the duration of waiting could be measured.
This is a radical departure from the philosophers' view of time as eternal.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Ghazali mentions he will explain further in the refutation of their second proof. Notice how this response fits into his overall strategy of refuting the philosophers' claims about the eternity of the world.
The createdness of time is a key premise in Ghazali's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كماء البحر ينقسم بالجداول والأنهار ، ثم يعود إلى البحر ، فأما ما لا كمية له فكيف ينقسم ؟ والمقصود من هذا كله، أن نبين أنهم لم يعجزوا خصومهم عن معتقدهم في تعلق الإرادة القديمة بالأحداث إلا بدعوى الضرورة ، وأنهم لا ينفصلون عمن يدعي الضرورة عليهم، في هذه الأمور على خلاف معتقدهم، وهذا لامخرج عنه فإن قيل : هذا ينقلب عليكم فى أن الله قبل خلق العالم كان قادراً على الخلق بقدر سنة أو سنين ، ولا نهاية لقدرته ، فكأنه صبر ولم يخلق ، ثم خلق ، ومدة الترك متناه أو غير متناه ؟ فإن قلتم : متناه ، صار وجود البارى متناهى الأول ، وإن قلتم : غير متناه ، فقد انقضى مدة فيها إمكانيات لا نهاية لإعدادها قلنا : المدة والزمان مخلوقان عندنا ، وسنبين حقيقة الجواب عن هذا في الانفصال عن دليلهم الثانى
Like sea water, it divides into streams and rivers, then returns to the sea. As for what has no quantity, how can it divide? The purpose of all this is to show that they did not dislodge their opponents from their belief concerning the attachment of the eternal will to events except by claiming necessity, and that they are no different from one who claims necessity against them in these matters contrary to their belief, and there is no way out of this. For if it is said: This turns against you concerning that God before creating the world was able to create by a year or years, and there is no end to His power, so it is as if He waited and did not create, then created. Is the duration of abstention finite or infinite? If you say finite, then the existence of the Creator becomes finite in the beginning. If you say infinite, then a duration has elapsed containing possibilities infinite in number. We say: Duration and time are created according to us, and we will explain the true answer to this in the refutation of their second proof.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage begins with a metaphor comparing the philosophers' argument to sea water that divides into streams and returns to the sea, questioning how something without quantity can divide. Ghazali then states his purpose: to show that the philosophers only refute their opponents by appealing to necessity (al-darura), a method that can be turned against them. He then presents an objection that would be used against his own position: if God was able to create from eternity but delayed, the duration of His abstention must be either finite or infinite. If finite, God's existence would have a beginning; if infinite, an infinite sequence of possibilities would have elapsed. Ghazali's response is that both duration (al-mudda) and time (al-zaman) are created, so the objection does not apply. He promises to elaborate later in his discussion of their second proof.

Key Terms
المدة (al-mudda)
الزمان (al-zaman)
مخلوقان (makhluqan)
الضرورة (al-darura)
الإرادة القديمة (al-irada al-qadima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is eternal and independent of creation, providing a backdrop against which God's actions can be measured.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that time itself is created by God, so it cannot precede creation or serve as a framework for measuring divine delay.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the response to the objection about the duration of God's abstention from creating?
A) The duration is finite, so God's existence has a beginning.
B) The duration is infinite, so an infinite sequence has elapsed.
C) Duration and time are both created by God.
D) The objection is invalid because God's power is not subject to time.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that time and duration are created by God, thus the philosophical dilemma about God's power before creation is resolved because there is no pre-creation time line.

The Analogy Between Spatial and Temporal Void
Ghazali argues that temporal void is inconceivable by analogy with spatial void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that time, like space, is an accident of a finite subject, and therefore there is no infinite temporal void before the world.

Core Idea
Time is an accident of motion, just as space is an accident of body.

In this passage, Ghazali extends his critique of infinite regress from space to time. He first shows that imagining an infinite void beyond the world is flawed because extension is dependent on body. Then he applies the same logic to time, arguing that temporal extension depends on motion, which is finite. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its implications for the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Ghazali structures his argument: first spatial void, then temporal void.
Pay attention to the key terms: وهم (imagination), بعد (extension), تابع (consequent).
2
Understand the spatial void argument
Identify why Ghazali says spatial void is inconceivable. The key point: extension (al-bu'd) is an accident of body, not an independent reality. Since body is finite, its extension is finite.
The imagination (وهم) may still picture an infinite void, but reason rejects it.
3
Apply the same reasoning to time
See how Ghazali draws the analogy: temporal extension is an accident of motion. Motion is finite (has a beginning and end), so time is finite. Therefore, no infinite time before the world.
This refutes the philosophers' claim of an eternal past.
4
Reflect on the role of imagination
Note that Ghazali acknowledges the imagination still clings to the idea of infinite time, but rational proof overrides it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما جاز أن يكذب الوهم في تقديره فوق العالم خلاء هو بعد لا نهاية له بأن يقال له الخلاء ليس مفهوماً في نفسه أما البعد فهو تابع للجسم الذي تتباعد أقطاره فإذا كان الجسم متناهياً كان البعد الذي هو تابع له متناهياً فانقطع أن الخلاء والملاء غير مفهوم فثبت أنه ليس وراء العالم لا خلاء ولا ملاء وإن كان الوهم لا يذعن لقبوله وكذلك يقال كما أن البعد المكاني تابع للجسم فالبعد الزماني تابع للحركة فإنه امتداد الحركة كما أن ذلك امتداد لأقطار الجسم وكما أن قيام الدليل على تناهى أقطار الجسم منع من إثبات بعد مكانى وراءه فقيام الدليل على تناهى الحركة من طرفيه يمنع من تقدير بعد زمانى وراءه وإن كان الوهم متشبثاً بخياله
And just as it is possible for the imagination to be belied in its estimation of a void above the world, that is, an infinite extension, by saying to it: 'The void is not a concept in itself; rather, extension is consequent upon the body whose dimensions are separated; so if the body is finite, the extension which is consequent upon it is finite,' so it is cut off that both void and plenum are inconceivable, and thus it is established that there is neither void nor plenum beyond the world, even if the imagination does not assent to its acceptance. And likewise it is said: Just as spatial extension is consequent upon body, so temporal extension is consequent upon motion, for it is the extension of motion, just as that is the extension of the body's dimensions. And just as the establishment of the proof of the finitude of the body's dimensions prevented the postulation of a spatial extension beyond it, so the establishment of the proof of the finitude of motion at both its ends prevents the estimation of a temporal extension beyond it, even if the imagination clings to its fancy.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by analogy. Ghazali begins by noting that the imagination tries to conceive an infinite void beyond the finite world. He refutes this by explaining that the concept of 'void' is not independent; rather, extension (al-bu'd) is an accident that follows from the body. Since the body is finite, its extension is finite. Therefore, there is no void or plenum beyond the world, even if the imagination does not accept it. Next, he draws a parallel with time. Just as spatial extension is consequent upon body, temporal extension is consequent upon motion. Motion is finite (it has a beginning and end, as proven elsewhere in kalam), so time, being the measure of motion, is also finite. Hence, one cannot postulate an infinite time before the world. Ghazali concludes that the same reasoning that denies spatial void also denies temporal void, despite the imagination's resistance.

Key Terms
وهم
بعد مكاني
بعد زماني
تبعي
خيال
تناهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is an independent substance that could exist without motion.
Correction: Ghazali argues time is an accident of motion, like spatial extension is an accident of body. If motion is finite, time is finite.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, why is there no temporal void before the world?
A) Because time is an illusion.
B) Because time is an accident of motion, and motion is finite.
C) Because the imagination denies it.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Time is not a self-subsistent entity; it depends on motion, and because motion is finite, time cannot be infinite in the past.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your imagination may resist that your time is finite. What part of your life do you treat as if it stretches infinitely, ignoring that your days are bounded by the finitude of your actions? What will you change today to confront that reality?

?

Ghazali's Response to the Relativity Objection
Objection and answer: Ghazali shifts from above/below to inside/outside to defend the temporal analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends his analogy between temporal and spatial extension by replacing the relative terms 'above/below' with the absolute terms 'inside/outside'.

Core Idea
Before and after are as intrinsic to existence as inside and outside are to bodies.

The opponent challenges Ghazali's analogy by arguing that spatial directions like 'above' and 'below' are relative to the observer, while 'before' and 'after' are absolute properties of the world's existence. Ghazali responds by switching the analogy to 'inside' and 'outside', which, like 'before' and 'after', refer to inherent boundaries. This lesson traces the objection and Ghazali's clever reframing.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the opponent's claim that the spherical world has no fixed above/below, making the analogy flawed.
Notice the opponent uses spatial relativity to attack the parallel with time.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
The opponent says above/below depend on the observer's position, so they are not intrinsic. In contrast, before/after are fixed limits of existence. Understand why this seems to break the analogy.
3
See how Ghazali shifts the analogy
Ghazali replaces above/below with inside/outside. He argues that if 'outside' means the surface boundary, the world has an outside. Similarly, if 'before' means the start of existence, the world has a before. The limits are intrinsic.
Ghazali points out that inside/outside are not relative; they are fixed by the body's shape.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that before/after are intrinsic limits like inside/outside, not relative like above/below. Thus his original analogy holds, and the opponent's objection fails.
The passage ends by noting that the opponent's imagination, even if he believes in eternity, can still conceive of a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذه الموازنة معوجة، لأن العالم ليس له «فوق» ولا «تحت» لأنه كرى، وليس للكرة «فوق» ولا «تحت»، بل إن سميت جهة «فوقاً» فمن حيث إنها تلى رأسك، والأخرى «تحتاً» فمن حيث إنها تلى رجلك، فهو اسم تجدد له بالإضافة إليك، والجهة التى هى «تحت» بالإضافة إليك، هي «فوق» بالإضافة إلى غيرك، إذا قدرته على الجانب الآخر من كرة الأرض واقفاً يحاذى أخمص قدمه أخمص قدمك، بل الجهة التي تقدرها فوقك من أجزاء السماء نهاراً، هي بعينها تحت الأرض ليلا، وما هو تحت الأرض يعود إلى فوق الأرض بالدور؛ وأما الأول لوجود العالم فلا يتصور أن ينقلب آخراً، وهو كما لو قدرنا خشبة أحد طرفيها غليظ، والآخر دقيق، واصطلحنا على أن نسمى الجهة التي تلى الدقيق «فوقاً» إلى حيث ينتهى، والجانب الآخر تحتاً، لم يظهر بهذا اختلاف ذاتى فى أجزاء العالم، بل هي أسامى مختلفة، قيامها بهيئة هذه الخشبة حتى لو عكس وضعها لانعكس الاسم، والعالم لم يتبدل، فالفوق والتحت نسبة محضة إليك لا تختلف أجزاء العالم وسطوحه فيه؛ وأما العدم المتقدم على العالم، والنهاية الأولى لموجوده، فذاتى له لا يتصور أن يتبدل فيصير آخراً، ولا العدم المقدر عند فناء العالم، الذي هو عدم لاحق يتصور أن يصير سابقاً، فطرفا نهاية وجود العالم اللذان أحدهما أول، والثانى آخر، طرفان ذاتيان ثابتان، لا يتصور التبدل فيهما بتبدل الإضافات إليه البتة بخلاف «الفوق» و «التحت» فإذن أمكننا أن نقول: ليس للعالم «فوق» ولا «تحت»، ولا يمكنكم أن تقولوا: ليس لوجود العالم «قبل» ولا «بعد»، وإذا ثبت «القبل» و «البعد» فلا معنى للزمان سوى ما يعبر عنه «بالقبل» و «البعد». قلنا: لا فرق، فإنه لا غرض فى تعيين لفظ «الفوق» و «التحت»، بل نعدل إلى لفظ «الداخل» و «الخارج»، ونقول: للعالم داخل وخارج، فهل خارج العالم شيء من ملاء أو خلاء؟ فسيقولون: ليس وراء العالم لا خلاء ولا ملاء. وإن عنيتم بالخارج سطحه الأعلى فله خارج، وإن عنيتم غيره فلا خارج له، فكذلك إذا قيل لنا: هل لوجود العالم «قبل»؟، قلنا إن عنى به هل لوجود العالم بداية، أى طرف منه ابتدأ فله «قبل» على هذا، كما للعالم خارج على تأويل أنه الطرف المكشوف والمنقطع السطحى، وإن عنيتم «بقبل» شيئاً آخر فلا قبل للعالم، كما أنه إذا عنى بخارج العالم شيء سوى السطح، قيل لا خارج للعالم، فإن قلتم لا يعقل مبدأ وجود لا «قبل» له، فيقال ولا يعقل متناهى وجود من الجسم لا خارج له، فإن قلت «خارجه» سطحه الذي هو منقطعه لا غير، قلنا قبله بداية وجوده الذي هو طرفه لا غير بقى أن نقول: لله وجود ولا عالم معه، وهذا القول أيضاً لا يوجب إثبات شيء آخر، والذى يدل على أن هذا عمل الوهم، أنه مخصوص بالزمان والمكان فإن الخصم وإن اعتقدم قدم الجسم يذعن وهمه لتقدير حدوثه، ونحن و إن اعتقدنا
If it is said: This analogy is crooked, because the world has no 'above' and no 'below', since it is spherical, and the sphere has no 'above' and no 'below'. Rather, if a direction is called 'above', it is because it faces your head, and the other direction 'below' because it faces your foot. So it is a name that arises anew relative to you. The direction that is 'below' relative to you is 'above' relative to another, if you imagine him on the opposite side of the earth’s sphere standing so that the sole of his foot aligns with the sole of your foot. Indeed, the direction you estimate above you from parts of the sky by day is the very same as below the earth by night, and what is below the earth returns to above the earth by rotation. As for the first (precedence) of the world’s existence, it cannot be imagined to turn into the last. It is as if we imagined a board with one end thick and the other thin, and we agreed to call the direction facing the thin end 'above' to its limit, and the other side 'below'. Through this, no intrinsic difference in the parts of the world appears; rather, these are different names whose existence depends on the form of this board, such that if its position were reversed, the name would be reversed, while the world does not change. So 'above' and 'below' are pure relations to you; the parts of the world and its surfaces do not differ in them. As for the non-existence preceding the world, and the first limit of its existence, that is intrinsic to it and cannot be imagined to change so as to become the last. Nor can the non-existence estimated at the annihilation of the world, which is a subsequent non-existence, be imagined to become prior. Thus the two limits of the world’s existence – one being the first and the other the last – are two intrinsic, fixed limits. Their change with the change of relations to them is absolutely unimaginable, unlike 'above' and 'below'. Therefore we can say: The world has no 'above' and no 'below', but you cannot say that the world’s existence has no 'before' and no 'after'. And if 'before' and 'after' are established, then time is nothing but what is expressed by 'before' and 'after'. We say: There is no difference. For there is no purpose in fixing the terms 'above' and 'below'. Rather, we switch to the terms 'inside' and 'outside'. We say: The world has an inside and an outside. So is there anything outside the world, whether plenum or void? They will say: There is neither void nor plenum beyond the world. If you mean by 'outside' its upper surface, then it has an outside. But if you mean something else, then it has no outside. Similarly, if it is said to us: Does the world’s existence have a 'before'? We say: If you mean whether the world’s existence has a beginning, i.e., a limit at which it began, then it has a 'before' in this sense, just as the world has an 'outside' on the interpretation that it is the uncovered surface and the surface-end. But if you mean by 'before' something else, then the world has no 'before', just as if one meant by 'outside' of the world something other than the surface, it would be said: The world has no outside. For if you say: A beginning of existence without a 'before' is inconceivable, then it is said: A finite body without an outside is also inconceivable. If you say: Its 'outside' is its surface, which is its termination, nothing more, we say: Its 'before' is the beginning of its existence, which is its limit, nothing more. It remains to say: God has existence and no world with Him. And this statement also does not require positing something else. What indicates that this is the work of the imagination is that it is particular to time and space. For the opponent, even if he believes in the eternity of the body, his imagination assents to the estimation of its origination, while we, even if we believe...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The opponent challenges Ghazali's analogy between spatial and temporal extension. He argues that 'above' and 'below' are relative, depending on the observer's position, but 'before' and 'after' for the world's existence are absolute and intrinsic. The world is spherical, so directions are not fixed. Ghazali responds by shifting terminology from 'above/below' to 'inside/outside'. He points out that if by 'outside' we mean the surface or boundary, the world does have an outside. Similarly, if by 'before' we mean the beginning or limit of existence, the world has a before. The intrinsic limits of existence (first and last) are not relative like spatial directions. Thus, the analogy holds. Ghazali concludes that the mind's resistance to these concepts stems from its conditioning by space and time, but the opponent himself, even if he believes the world is eternal, can still imagine its temporal beginning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent believes that because up/down are relative, the analogy between spatial and temporal limits is invalid.
Correction: Ghazali shows that by choosing the spatial pair 'inside/outside' (which are absolute), the analogy is restored, and before/after remain intrinsic.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali replace 'above/below' with 'inside/outside' in his response?
A) Because 'inside/outside' are easier to visualize.
B) Because 'above/below' are relative, but 'inside/outside' are absolute relations to a body's boundary.
C) Because the opponent only understands 'inside/outside'.
D) Because the Qur'an uses 'inside/outside'.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends the analogy between spatial and temporal extension by showing that 'before/after' are intrinsic limits, just as 'inside/outside' are intrinsic to a body, not relative like 'above/below'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed relative perspectives (like peer pressure or cultural trends) to make you doubt absolute truths about your purpose and origin? This lesson shows that intrinsic realities like your beginning and end are fixed. What absolute truth about your life have you been treating as negotiable?
?
The opponent confused relative directions with intrinsic limits. In your own life, where have you mistaken a relative concern (like social status) for a fixed reality? Ask yourself: what question about your own existence are you avoiding by focusing on temporary, changing circumstances?
?
Ghazali shifted from above/below to inside/outside to clarify the argument. What analogy or mental model in your own thinking might be misleading you about the nature of time, your life, or your relationship with God? Commit today to examine one assumption you hold about your 'beginning' and 'end' that you may have been taking lightly.

Second Formulation: The Argument from Potential Worlds
Ghazali presents a reductio argument for the eternity of time using multiple potential worlds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali employs a reductio ad absurdum involving multiple potential worlds to argue for the eternity of time.

Core Idea
Different possible worlds force an eternal temporal background.

In this second formulation, Ghazali deepens the argument by considering multiple potential worlds God could have created. He shows that the very possibility of creating worlds with different durations before the actual world requires an extended pre-existing time. This passage uses a clever reductio to expose the logical necessity of an eternal time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an initial premise followed by a hypothetical scenario with increasing world counts.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum, so follow the logic closely.
2
Identify the initial premise
The opponents state that God could have created the world earlier by any amount. This implies a pre-existing duration.
This is the shared assumption between Ghazali and the philosophers.
3
Follow the hypothetical
Ghazali poses a scenario with a first world (1000 rotations), then asks about a second (1100) and third (1200). Trace the logical consequences of the philosophers' answers.
Pay attention to the two branches: 'if you say no' and 'if you say yes'.
4
Understand the absurdity
The key absurdity arises when considering the third and second worlds ending simultaneously with different rotation counts. This contradicts equal speed and distance.
This forces the opponent to admit the third must be created earlier, implying time before the first world.
5
Conclude the argument
The only way to avoid contradiction is to accept that time extends infinitely before creation. Reflect on how this supports the eternity of time.
Note the footnote: 'the other' refers to the second world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صيغة ثانية لهم فى إلزام قدم الزمان قالوا : لا شك في أن الله عندكم كان قادراً على أن يخلق العالم قبل خلقه بقدر سنة ، ومائة سنة ، وألف سنة ، وما لا نهاية له ، وأن هذه التقديرات متفاوتة فى المقدار والكمية ، فلابد من إثبات شيء قبل وجود العالم ممتد مقدر بعضه أمد وأطول من البعض . فنقول : إذا قدرنا أن العالم من أول قد دار فلكه إلى الآن ألف دورة مثلا ، فهل كان الله سبحانه قادراً على أن يخلق قبله عالماً ثانياً مثله بحيث ينتهى إلى زماننا هذا بألف ومائة دورة ؟ فإن قلتم : لا ، فكأنه انقلب القديم من العجز إلى القدرة ، أو العالم من الإمكان إلى الاستحالة ، و إن قلتم : نعم ، ولابد منه ، فهل كان يقدر على أن يخلق عالماً ثالثاً بحيث ينتهى إلى زماننا بألف ومائتى دورة ؟ ، ولابد من نعم ، فنقول : هذا العالم الذى سميناه بحسب ترتيبنا فى التقدير ثالثاً - وإن كان هو الأسبق – فهل أمكن خلقه العالم الذى سميناه ثانياً وكان ينتهى إلينا بألفى ومائتى دورة ، والآخر (۱) بألف ومائة دورة ، وهما متساويان فى مسافة الحركة وسرعتها ؟ فإن قلتم : نعم ، فهو محال ، إذ يستحيل إذ يستحيل أن يتساوى حركتان في السرعة والبطء ثم تنتهيان إلى وقت واحد ، والأعداد متفاوتة ، وإن قلتم : إن العالم الثالث الذى ينتهي إلينا بألف ومائتى دورة ، لا يمكن أن يخلق مع العالم الثاني الذي ينتهي إلينا بألف ومائة دورة ، بل لابد أن يخلقه قبله بمقدار يساوى المقدار الذي تقدم به العالم الثانى على العالم الأول وسميناه الأول، لأنه أقرب إلى وهمنا، إذا ارتقنا من وقتنا (۱) يعنى بالآخر العالم الذي سماه « ثانيا » .
A second formulation of theirs for necessitating the eternity of time. They said: There is no doubt that, according to you, God was able to create the world before its creation by a year, a hundred years, a thousand years, and what has no end, and that these estimations differ in magnitude and quantity. So it is necessary to affirm something before the existence of the world, extended and measured, some of whose period is longer and longer than some. So we say: If we suppose that from its beginning the world's sphere has rotated up to now, say a thousand rotations, then was God, exalted, able to create before it a second world like it such that it would reach our time with one thousand one hundred rotations? If you say: No, then it would be as if the Eternal has changed from inability to ability, or the world from possibility to impossibility. And if you say: Yes, and it is necessary, then was He able to create a third world such that it would reach our time with one thousand two hundred rotations? And necessarily yes. So we say: This world which we have called, according to our order of estimation, third — although it is the earliest — is it possible that it [the third world] was created along with the world we called second, and it [the second] would reach us with two thousand one hundred rotations, and the other with one thousand one hundred rotations, while they are equal in distance of motion and speed? If you say: Yes, then it is impossible, for it is impossible for two motions to be equal in speed and slowness and then end at a single time while the numbers are different. And if you say: The third world that reaches us with one thousand two hundred rotations cannot be created along with the second world that reaches us with one thousand one hundred rotations, but rather it must be created before it by an amount equal to the amount by which the second world preceded the first world — and we called it first, because it is closer to our imagination, when we ascend from our time. (1) By 'the other' he means the world he called 'second'.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a second formulation (ṣīgha thāniya) of an argument for the eternity of time. It is structured as a reductio ad absurdum. The opponents (the philosophers) initially state that it is certain that God could have created the world earlier by various amounts, implying a pre-existing extended duration. Ghazali then poses a scenario: suppose the actual world has undergone 1000 rotations. Could God have created a second identical world earlier such that it would reach the present with 1100 rotations? If not, that implies a change in God's power. If yes, then could He create a third with 1200 rotations? Again yes. Now the absurdity: consider the third world (the earliest) and the second world (later). If they were created simultaneously, they would both end at the present, but the third world would have 1200 rotations and the second 1100, yet they are of equal speed and distance? That is impossible. So the opponent must say they cannot be simultaneous, so the third must be created before the second, implying a pre-existing temporal interval. Thus, time must be eternal. The footnote clarifies that 'the other' refers to the world he called 'second'.

Key Terms
قدرة
دورة
تساوي الحركة
محال
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage argues for the actual existence of multiple parallel universes.
Correction: Ghazali uses hypothetical worlds to expose a logical problem, not to assert their reality.

Checkpoint
In the second formulation, what is the absurd consequence if the third and second worlds end at the same time?
A) They would have different speeds
B) They would have the same number of rotations
C) Two motions equal in speed cannot reach the same end with different distances
D) God would be unable to create them

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's reductio shows that even the possibility of creating worlds with different past durations logically entails an eternal time before creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's power to create worlds with different pasts, does it unsettle you that your own finite mind cannot fully grasp the implications? How will you trust divine revelation over logical puzzles like this one?
?
This argument forces you to admit a duration 'before creation.' Does your heart find rest in the Ash'ari position that time is created, or does the philosopher's reasoning shake your confidence? Pray for clarity and submit your intellect to the Quran.
?
If you were to defend the belief that the world had a beginning to someone who uses this argument, what would you say? Write a short response that both acknowledges the logical force and affirms your faith.

First Refutation: The Excuse Contradicts Reason
Ghazali presents the first of three refutations: the excuse is a contradiction of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that the philosophers' excuse is a contradiction of reason because true impossibilities are only logical contradictions.

Core Idea
Impossible means logical contradiction, not different sizes.

Ghazali begins his refutation by exposing the philosophers' confusion about what 'impossible' truly means. They treat a change in the world's size as equivalent to a logical contradiction. This lesson shows why that comparison fails and why their claim is an arbitrary assertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation for the first aspect. Notice how Ghazali frames the philosophers' claim as an excuse.
2
Identify the key distinction
Ghazali contrasts imagining a larger/smaller world with imagining a logical contradiction like combining black and white. Why are these different?
The difference is that one involves physical possibility, the other logical impossibility.
3
Understand the core claim
Ghazali asserts that the only true impossibilities reduce to combining affirmation and negation (logical contradiction). Physical size changes do not fall under this category.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does Ghazali call the excuse 'cold and corrupt arbitrariness'? Consider how the philosophers have no principled basis for deeming certain sizes impossible.
This sets up the next refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن لا نقول : إن ما ليس بممكن فهو مقدور ، وكون العالم أكبر مما هو عليه أو أصغر منه ليس بممكن فلا يكون مقدوراً . وهذا العذر باطل من ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها : أن هذا مكابرة للعقل ، فإن العقل فى تقدير العالم أكبر أو أصغر مما هو عليه بذراع ، ليس هو كتقديره الجمع بين السواد والبياض ، والوجود والعدم ، والممتنع هو الجمع بين النفى والإثبات ، وإليه ترجع المحالات كلها فهو تحكم بارد فاسد الثاني : أنه إذا كان العالم على ما هو عليه لا يمكن أن يكون أكبر منه ولا أصغر ، فوجوده على ما هو عليه واجب لا ممكن ، والواجب مستغن عن علة، فقولوا بما قاله الدهريون من نفى الصانع ، ونفى سبب هو مسبب الأسباب ، وليس هذا مذهبكم الثالث : هو أن هذا الفاسد لا يعجز الخصم عن مقابلته بمثله ، فنقول : ' إنه لم يكن وجود العالم قبل وجوده ممكناً ، بل وافق الوجود الإمكان من غير زيادة ولا نقصان ، فإن قلتم فقد انتقل القديم من العجز إلى القدرة ، قلنا : لا ، لأن الوجود لم يكن ممكناً فلم يكن مقدوراً ، وامتناع حصول ما ليس بممكن لا يدل على العجز ، وإن قلتم : إنه كيف كان ممتنعاً فصار ممكناً ، قلنا : ولم يستحل أن يكون ممتنعاً في حال ممكناً فى حال ؟ كما أن الشيء إذا أخذ مع أحد الضدين امتنع اتصافه بالآخر ، وإذا أخذ لا معه أمكن ، فإن قلتم : الأحوال متساوية ، قلنا لكم : والمقادير متساوية ، فكيف يكون مقدار ممكناً ، وأكبر منه أو أصغر بمقدار ظفر ممتنعاً ، فإن لم يستحل ذلك لم يستحل هذا فهذا طريق المقاومة
If it is said: We do not say that whatever is not possible is within power. The world being larger or smaller than it is is not possible, so it is not within power. This excuse is invalid from three aspects: First, that this is a contradiction of reason, for the mind's estimation of the world as larger or smaller by a cubit is not like its estimation of combining black and white, existence and non-existence. The impossible is the combination of negation and affirmation, to which all impossibilities reduce. So this is a cold and corrupt arbitrariness. Second, if the world as it is cannot be larger or smaller, then its existence as it is is necessary, not possible. The necessary is independent of a cause. So say what the materialists (Dahriyya) said: deny the Maker and deny a cause that is the cause of causes. This is not your doctrine. Third, this corrupt argument does not prevent the opponent from countering with a similar one. We say: The existence of the world before its existence was not possible, but existence coincided with possibility without increase or decrease. If you say: then the eternal (God) moved from incapacity to power, we say: No, because existence was not possible, so it was not within power. The impossibility of something not possible does not indicate incapacity. If you say: how was it impossible then became possible? We say: why is it impossible that something be impossible in one state and possible in another? Just as a thing when taken with one opposite is impossible to be described with the other, and when taken without it, it is possible. If you say: the states are equal. We say to you: the measures are equal. So how is one measure possible, and a larger or smaller one by a finger's breadth impossible? If that is not impossible, this is not impossible. This is the way of resistance.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's detailed refutation of the philosophers' excuse that the world's size is fixed because any change would be impossible. He gives three independent arguments:

1. The claim is a contradiction of reason: imagining a larger or smaller world is not comparable to imagining contradictions like black and white together. The only true impossibilities are logical contradictions.

2. If the world's dimensions are fixed, its existence becomes necessary rather than contingent, which would imply that God is not a free agent and would lead to atheistic materialism (denying the Creator).

3. The same reasoning can be turned against them: if the world's existence before it existed was not possible (as they claim), then God would be moving from incapacity to power—which they deny. But by parity of reasoning, if states are equal, then measures are equal, so there is no basis for deeming one size possible and another impossible.

Thus, the excuse is invalid, and the philosophers' position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
مكابرة (contradiction of reason)
محالات (impossibilities)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical changes (like size) are impossible in the same way logical contradictions are impossible.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between physical possibility and logical impossibility; only the latter is truly impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's first argument, what is the only true impossibility?
A) A larger or smaller world
B) The combination of negation and affirmation
C) A change in God's power
D) The existence of the world

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' excuse fails because they mistakenly equate physical impossibility with logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali calls the philosophers' excuse 'a cold and corrupt arbitrariness.' Is there an excuse you have used to avoid acknowledging God's power in your own life—something you have assumed is impossible for God to change? What would it take for you to abandon that excuse today?
?
The philosophers claimed that a larger or smaller world is impossible, so God cannot make it. Are there ways you have limited God's creativity or power based on your own limited imagination or experiences? How can you actively remind yourself that God's power is not bound by your expectations?
?
Ghazali shows that a size change is conceivable, whereas a logical contradiction is not. Think about a situation in your life where you have mistaken a difficulty for an impossibility in God's sight. Will you ask God to help you distinguish between what is truly impossible (like a contradiction) and what is merely challenging (like a change in circumstances)?

Second Refutation: The Excuse Implies Necessity and Atheism
Ghazali presents the second refutation: the excuse leads to necessary existence and denial of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that if the world's size is fixed, its existence becomes necessary, which would deny God's free agency and lead to materialism.

Core Idea
Fixed size implies necessary existence, denying the Creator.

Ghazali's second argument turns the philosophers' logic against them. If the world cannot be larger or smaller, then it exists necessarily, not contingently. This would mean God is not a free agent—a conclusion the philosophers themselves reject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation for the second aspect. Follow the logical progression.
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify each step: if the world's size is fixed → then its existence is necessary → the necessary needs no cause → leads to denying the Creator (materialism).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Recognize the rhetorical move
Ghazali forces the philosophers to accept an absurd consequence they do not believe in. He challenges them to adopt materialism if they follow their own logic.
4
Reflect on divine free agency
Why does Ghazali think that a necessary world undermines the idea of a free Creator? How does this relate to the earlier discussion of possibility?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن لا نقول : إن ما ليس بممكن فهو مقدور ، وكون العالم أكبر مما هو عليه أو أصغر منه ليس بممكن فلا يكون مقدوراً . وهذا العذر باطل من ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها : أن هذا مكابرة للعقل ، فإن العقل فى تقدير العالم أكبر أو أصغر مما هو عليه بذراع ، ليس هو كتقديره الجمع بين السواد والبياض ، والوجود والعدم ، والممتنع هو الجمع بين النفى والإثبات ، وإليه ترجع المحالات كلها فهو تحكم بارد فاسد الثاني : أنه إذا كان العالم على ما هو عليه لا يمكن أن يكون أكبر منه ولا أصغر ، فوجوده على ما هو عليه واجب لا ممكن ، والواجب مستغن عن علة، فقولوا بما قاله الدهريون من نفى الصانع ، ونفى سبب هو مسبب الأسباب ، وليس هذا مذهبكم الثالث : هو أن هذا الفاسد لا يعجز الخصم عن مقابلته بمثله ، فنقول : ' إنه لم يكن وجود العالم قبل وجوده ممكناً ، بل وافق الوجود الإمكان من غير زيادة ولا نقصان ، فإن قلتم فقد انتقل القديم من العجز إلى القدرة ، قلنا : لا ، لأن الوجود لم يكن ممكناً فلم يكن مقدوراً ، وامتناع حصول ما ليس بممكن لا يدل على العجز ، وإن قلتم : إنه كيف كان ممتنعاً فصار ممكناً ، قلنا : ولم يستحل أن يكون ممتنعاً في حال ممكناً فى حال ؟ كما أن الشيء إذا أخذ مع أحد الضدين امتنع اتصافه بالآخر ، وإذا أخذ لا معه أمكن ، فإن قلتم : الأحوال متساوية ، قلنا لكم : والمقادير متساوية ، فكيف يكون مقدار ممكناً ، وأكبر منه أو أصغر بمقدار ظفر ممتنعاً ، فإن لم يستحل ذلك لم يستحل هذا فهذا طريق المقاومة
If it is said: We do not say that whatever is not possible is within power. The world being larger or smaller than it is is not possible, so it is not within power. This excuse is invalid from three aspects: First, that this is a contradiction of reason, for the mind's estimation of the world as larger or smaller by a cubit is not like its estimation of combining black and white, existence and non-existence. The impossible is the combination of negation and affirmation, to which all impossibilities reduce. So this is a cold and corrupt arbitrariness. Second, if the world as it is cannot be larger or smaller, then its existence as it is is necessary, not possible. The necessary is independent of a cause. So say what the materialists (Dahriyya) said: deny the Maker and deny a cause that is the cause of causes. This is not your doctrine. Third, this corrupt argument does not prevent the opponent from countering with a similar one. We say: The existence of the world before its existence was not possible, but existence coincided with possibility without increase or decrease. If you say: then the eternal (God) moved from incapacity to power, we say: No, because existence was not possible, so it was not within power. The impossibility of something not possible does not indicate incapacity. If you say: how was it impossible then became possible? We say: why is it impossible that something be impossible in one state and possible in another? Just as a thing when taken with one opposite is impossible to be described with the other, and when taken without it, it is possible. If you say: the states are equal. We say to you: the measures are equal. So how is one measure possible, and a larger or smaller one by a finger's breadth impossible? If that is not impossible, this is not impossible. This is the way of resistance.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's detailed refutation of the philosophers' excuse that the world's size is fixed because any change would be impossible. He gives three independent arguments:

1. The claim is a contradiction of reason: imagining a larger or smaller world is not comparable to imagining contradictions like black and white together. The only true impossibilities are logical contradictions.

2. If the world's dimensions are fixed, its existence becomes necessary rather than contingent, which would imply that God is not a free agent and would lead to atheistic materialism (denying the Creator).

3. The same reasoning can be turned against them: if the world's existence before it existed was not possible (as they claim), then God would be moving from incapacity to power—which they deny. But by parity of reasoning, if states are equal, then measures are equal, so there is no basis for deeming one size possible and another impossible.

Thus, the excuse is invalid, and the philosophers' position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
دهريون (materialists)
واجب (necessary)
ممكن (possible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary world can still have a Creator.
Correction: A necessary being needs no cause; if the world is necessary, it cannot be created by a free agent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's second argument, if the world's existence is necessary, what follows?
A) It still requires a creator
B) It is independent of a cause
C) It could have been different
D) God's power is limited

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' excuse leads to a necessary world, which would deny the need for a Creator—an absurd consequence they do not accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat the patterns and order in your life as if they were fixed and necessary, forgetting that Allah alone is the Necessary Being and everything else is contingent upon His will? What would change if you truly internalized that your health, wealth, and daily routines are all possible, not necessary?
?
When you see the precise order of the universe, does it lead you to gratitude and dependence on the Creator, or does it subtly make you feel that the world runs on its own, independent of Him? Examine your heart: do you sometimes act as if the world is self-sufficient, denying the constant need for Allah's causation?
?
The philosophers' flawed reasoning led them to a conclusion they themselves rejected—atheism. Reflect: are there areas in your life where your logic or assumptions lead to conclusions that contradict your faith? Will you courageously confront those assumptions, even if it means uprooting comfortable ideas?

Third Refutation: Turning the Argument Against Them
Ghazali presents the third refutation: a parity argument that turns the philosophers' logic against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third argument that the same reasoning can be used against the philosophers, showing their position is arbitrary.

Core Idea
If states are equal, then measures are equal.

Ghazali's final refutation is a powerful tu quoque argument. He shows that the philosophers' own logic about possibility and states can be turned back on them, undermining their excuse from within.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation for the third aspect. Notice the back-and-forth structure.
2
Understand the analogy
Ghazali compares the world's existence before creation (which the philosophers say was impossible) to the possibility of different sizes. If states can differ, so can measures.
He uses a parity argument: what applies to time (states) must apply to size (measures).
3
See the application
The philosophers might object that states are equal, but Ghazali counters that measures are also equal. So there is no basis for deeming one size possible and another impossible.
4
Realize the conclusion
The excuse is arbitrary and collapses under its own logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The impossible is the combination of negation and affirmation, to which all impossibilities reduce. So this is a cold and corrupt arbitrariness. Third, this corrupt argument does not prevent the opponent from countering with a similar one. We say: The existence of the world before its existence was not possible, but existence coincided with possibility without increase or decrease. If you say: then the eternal (God) moved from incapacity to power, we say: No, because existence was not possible, so it was not within power. The impossibility of something not possible does not indicate incapacity. If you say: how was it impossible then became possible? We say: why is it impossible that something be impossible in one state and possible in another? Just as a thing when taken with one opposite is impossible to be described with the other, and when taken without it, it is possible. If you say: the states are equal. We say to you: the measures are equal. So how is one measure possible, and a larger or smaller one by a finger's breadth impossible? If that is not impossible, this is not impossible. Ghazali's Definitive Answer: The Estimation of Possibilities Is Meaningless
Ghazali states his definitive answer and dismisses the estimation of possibilities as baseless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definitive refutation of the philosophers' argument based on the estimation of possibilities.

Core Idea
The estimation of possibilities has no meaning; only God's eternal power matters.

After presenting the philosophers' arguments and various replies, Imam al-Ghazali now gives his own definitive answer. He cuts to the core: the entire discussion of estimating possibilities is meaningless. The only sound position is that God is eternal and powerful, able to act whenever He wills. This lesson presents that decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Note Ghazali's assertive opening: والتحقيق فى الجواب.
Pay attention to the phrase لا معنى له.
2
Identify the key components of Ghazali's answer
Break down the passage into (a) dismissal of estimation, (b) affirmation of God's attributes, (c) non-necessity of extended time, (d) role of illusion.
3
Reflect on the role of illusion
Consider why Ghazali attributes the contrary view to illusion (وهم). What does this tell you about his epistemological approach?
This is a central move in his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق : فى الجواب أن ما ذكروه من تقدير الإمكانات لا معنى له ، وإنما المسلم أن الله تعالى قديم قادر ، لا يمتنع عليه الفعل أبداً ، إن أراده وليس في هذا القدر ما يوجب إثبات زمان ممتد ، إلا أن يضيف الوهم بتلبسه إليه شيئاً آخر
The definitive answer: In the response, what they mentioned about the estimation of possibilities has no meaning. Rather, what is accepted is that God is eternal, powerful, it is not impossible for Him to act at any time if He wills it. And there is nothing in this that necessitates the affirmation of extended time, unless illusion adds something else to it.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's own final answer to the entire discussion. He dismisses the whole notion of estimating possibilities as meaningless (لا معنى له). The correct and accepted position is simply that God is eternal and all-powerful; He can act whenever He wills. This does not require affirming a pre-existent extended time. Any suggestion to the contrary arises only from the illusion (وهم) of the mind, which has no real basis. Thus, the philosophers' argument is not only refuted but shown to be founded on a false premise.

Key Terms
تقدير الإمكانات (estimation of possibilities)
قديم (eternal)
قادر (powerful)
وهم (illusion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the estimation of possibilities is a real and meaningful concept that must be accounted for in any discussion of God's power and creation.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the estimation of possibilities is meaningless (لا معنى له). The only meaningful statement is that God is eternal and powerful, able to act whenever He wills.

The Philosophers' Fourth Argument on Annihilation
Ghazali presents the fourth argument and gives a brief answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that possibility requires a substrate, implying the indestructibility of matter, and Ghazali's concise refutation.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that possibility requires a substrate, so matter must be eternal.

The philosophers present a fourth argument based on the nature of possibility. They claim that even after annihilation, the possibility of existence remains as a relative attribute, necessitating an eternal substrate. Ghazali dismisses this by referring to his earlier refutation, but notes the argument's importance due to two additional proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the sequence: statement of impossibility, then the argument, then the answer.
The argument is presented as a claim of the philosophers.
2
Identify the core premise
The premise is that if the world were annihilated, the possibility of its existence would remain because the possible cannot become impossible. This possibility is a relative attribute requiring a substrate.
This is the key logical step.
3
Understand the conclusion
From the premise, they conclude that every event requires a prior matter and every nonexistent requires a matter to cease from. Thus, matter and principles are indestructible; only forms and accidents perish.
The conclusion directly opposes the Islamic view of creation ex nihilo.
4
Note Ghazali's answer
Ghazali says the answer is what has preceded (referring to his earlier discussion). He adds that he singled out this argument because it contains two additional proofs.
Ghazali does not elaborate here; the refutation is assumed.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why Ghazali would mention this argument if the answer is already given. What does this tell us about the philosophical debate?
This connects to the learning objective about understanding the broader argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسلكهم الرابع ، فهو محال ، لأنهم يقولون : إذا عدم العالم بقى إمكان وجوده ، إذ الممكن لا ينقلب مستحيلاً ، وهو وصف إضافي ، فيفتقر كل حادث بزعمهم إلى مادة سابقة ، وكل منعدم فيفتقر إلى مادة ينعدم عنها ، فالمواد والأصول لا تنعدم ، وإنما تنعدم الصور والأعراض الحالة فيها والجواب عن الكل ما سبق : وإنما أفردنا هذه المسألة لأن لهم فيها دليلين آخرين .
As for their fourth path, it is impossible. Because they say: If the world were to cease to exist, the possibility of its existence would remain, since the possible does not become impossible, and it is a relative attribute. So every event, according to their claim, requires a prior matter, and every nonexistent requires a matter from which it ceases to exist. Thus matters and principles do not cease to exist; only the forms and accidents subsisting in them cease. The answer to all of that is what has preceded. And we have singled out this issue because they have two other proofs in it.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' fourth argument against the possibility of the world's annihilation and gives a brief refutation. The argument: if the world were annihilated, its possibility of existence would remain (since the possible cannot become impossible). That possibility is a relative attribute, so it requires a substrate. Therefore, every coming-into-being (ḥādith) requires a prior matter, and every ceasing-to-be requires a matter from which it ceases. Thus the prime matter (mawādd) and principles (uṣūl) are indestructible; only forms and accidents cease. Ghazali says the answer to all this has already been given (presumably in the earlier discussion). He then notes that he has singled out this issue because they have two additional proofs related to it.

Key Terms
مسلك
إمكان
مادة
صورة
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali engages with the argument in detail here.
Correction: Ghazali merely states the argument and refers to a previous answer without detailed refutation.

Checkpoint
What does the philosophers' fourth argument claim about the possibility of existence after annihilation?
A) It becomes impossible.
B) It remains as a relative attribute.
C) It ceases to exist.
D) It is transferred to another world.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' fourth argument attempts to prove the indestructibility of matter, but Ghazali dismisses it by invoking his prior refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a sophisticated argument that seems to contradict your faith, do you tend to wrestle with it repeatedly, or do you trust that the scholars have already answered it? What does this lesson teach you about the weight of trusting prophetic knowledge over your own reasoning?
?
The philosophers assumed that possibility must have a substrate to exist. In your own life, do you sometimes look for a material 'substrate' of security before fully trusting Allah? What would it mean to let go of that need for a worldly foundation?
?
Ghazali dismissed the entire argument by referring to what he had already said. Are there doubts or intellectual objections you keep revisiting instead of accepting the clear answers from revelation and the scholarly tradition? What is holding you back from putting that matter to rest and moving forward?

The Impossibility of Annihilation: Ghazali's Refutation of the Second Proof
Ghazali presents and refutes the second proof for the impossibility of the world's non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' argument that the world cannot cease to exist, and why non-existence cannot be considered an act of God.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not a thing, so it cannot be an act of God.

Ghazali now turns to the second proof offered by the philosophers for the impossibility of the world's non-existence. They claim that there is no cause that could annihilate the world. In this passage, Ghazali exposes the incoherence of treating non-existence as an object of divine action. He argues that if God were to annihilate the world, there would be no existing act to attribute to Him, since non-existence is nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Find the philosophers' claim: the world's atoms cannot become non-existent because there is no intelligible cause. Notice they assume non-existence requires a cause.
3
Analyze Ghazali's two objections
First objection: If God's will causes non-existence, either His will changes (impossible) or His eternal will attaches to a changing object (problematic). Second, stronger objection: Non-existence is not a thing, so it cannot be an act. Ask: what would the 'act' of annihilation be?
Ghazali concludes that the concept of an 'act of annihilation' is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة. الدليل الثاني لهم في استحالة عدم العالم أن قالوا: إن العالم لا تنعدم جواهره، لأنه لا يعقل سبب معدم لها، وما لم يكن منعدماً ثم انعدم، فلابد أن يكون بسبب، وذلك السبب لا يخلو، إما أن يكون إرادة القديم سبحانه وهو محال، لأنه إذا لم يكن مريداً لعدمه، ثم صار مريداً، فقد تغير، أو يؤدي إلى أن يكون القديم وإرادته على نعت واحد في جميع الأحوال، والمراد يتغير من العدم إلى الوجود ثم من الوجود إلى العدم. وما ذكرناه من استحالة وجود حادث بإرادة قديمة، يدل على استحالة العدم. ويزيد ههنا إشكال آخر أقوى من ذلك، هو أن المراد فعل المريد لا محالة وكل من لم يكن فاعلاً ثم صار فاعلاً، إن لم يتغير هو في نفسه، فلابد أن يصير فعله موجوداً بعد أن لم يكن موجوداً، فإنه لو بقى كما كان، إذ لم يكن له فعل، والآن أيضاً لا فعل له، فإذن لم يفعل شيئاً؛ والعدم ليس بشيء فكيف يكون فعلاً؟! فإذا أعدم العالم وتجدد له فعل لم يكن، فما ذلك الفعل؟! أهو وجود العالم؟! وهو محال، إذ انقطع الوجود! أو فعله عدم العالم؟! وعدم العالم ليس بشيء موجوداً حتى يقال: هو الذي فعله حتى يكون فعلاً، فإن أقل درجات الفعل
The proofs. The second proof for the impossibility of the non-existence of the world: they said that the world's atoms do not become non-existent because there is no intelligible cause that annihilates them. And what was not non-existent then becomes non-existent must necessarily be due to a cause, and that cause is none other than either the will of the Eternal (Exalted is He), which is impossible, because if He was not willing its non-existence and then became willing, He would have changed; or it leads to the Eternal and His will being in a single state in all circumstances, while the intended object changes from non-existence to existence and then from existence to non-existence. What we mentioned about the impossibility of a temporal event arising from an eternal will indicates the impossibility of non-existence. And here there arises another objection stronger than that, namely: the intended object is necessarily the act of the willing agent, and everyone who was not an agent and then became an agent, if he does not change in himself, must have his act come into existence after it did not exist. For if he remained as he was, since he had no act, and now also he has no act, then he has done nothing. Non-existence is not a thing, so how can it be an act? So if He annihilates the world and a new act of His comes into being which was not, what is that act? Is it the existence of the world? That is impossible, since existence has ceased! Or is His act the non-existence of the world? But the non-existence of the world is not an existent thing such that it could be said: 'it is what He did' so as to be an act, for the least degree of an act [is ...]
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Ghazali first presents the philosophers' second proof: the world's atoms cannot become non-existent because there is no intelligible cause for their annihilation. If they did become non-existent, a cause is required, which must be God's will. But then either God's will changes (impossible) or His will is eternal while the object of will changes, which is also problematic given Ghazali's earlier argument that an eternal will cannot produce a temporal event. Ghazali then adds a stronger objection: the act of an agent must be an existing thing. Non-existence (ʿadam) is not a thing, so it cannot be an act. If God annihilates the world, what 'act' occurs? Not the world's existence (since it ceases), nor its non-existence (since non-existence is not an entity). Therefore, the notion of an 'act of annihilation' is incoherent. The argument is cut off at 'the least degree of an act', but the point is clear: non-existence cannot be the product of an act.

Key Terms
استحالة (istiḥāla)
عدم (ʿadam)
إرادة (irāda)
فعل (fiʿl)
قديم (qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers assume that non-existence requires a cause and can be an object of divine action.
Correction: Ghazali argues that non-existence is not a thing at all, so it cannot be caused or be an act. The idea of an 'act of annihilation' is incoherent because every act must produce an existing effect.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that non-existence cannot be an act of God?
A) Because non-existence is not a contingent property
B) Because non-existence is not an existing entity that can be the object of an act
C) Because God's will is eternal and unchanging
D) Because the world's atoms are indestructible

Key Takeaway
The act of annihilation is incoherent because non-existence is not an entity that can be the object of an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is not a thing that God 'does', then when you face the prospect of your own death, are you more comforted by the fact that God is not 'causing' your annihilation as an act, but rather your existence is a gift that ends? How does this reshape your reliance on the Giver rather than the gift?
?
The philosophers assumed non-existence needs a cause. Do you ever assume that your spiritual failures (e.g., missed prayers, forgotten duties) are caused by circumstances or others, rather than being a lack you are responsible for? How does Ghazali's point that non-existence is not a thing challenge you to own your omissions as real failures of action?
?
If God's act must produce an existing thing, then every existing thing in your life—your health, your faith, your relationships—is directly from Him as an act. What specific existing thing have you taken for granted today that you now see as a fresh act of God? Will you thank Him for that act right now?

Ghazali's Defense of Nonexistence as an Act of God
Ghazali defends that nonexistence can be an act of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that nonexistence, though not a thing, can be attributed to God's power as an act of annihilation.

Core Idea
Nonexistence, though not a thing, is a real occurrence attributable to divine power.

In this passage, Ghazali directly confronts the philosophical objection that annihilation cannot be an act of God because nonexistence is not a thing that can be produced. He responds by redefining what it means for something to be an 'act'—it is simply an occurrence attributed to God's will and power. This short but powerful argument defends the Ash'ari view that God's power extends over both existence and nonexistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the rhythm of Ghazali's argument sink in.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the complete argument up to that point.
2
Identify Ghazali's core claim
Find where Ghazali states that bringing into existence and annihilating are both by God's will. This is the central thesis.
He says: 'الإيجاد والإعدام بإرادة القادر'.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
Notice the objection: 'What act is produced when God annihilates?' Ghazali answers: the new occurrence is nonexistence itself.
This redefines 'act' as any new occurrence, not necessarily a positive entity.
4
Analyze the second objection and counter
The opponent says nonexistence is not a thing, so how can it be produced? Ghazali retorts: since it is not a thing, how can it occur? Its occurrence is rationally conceivable, so it can be attributed to power.
This is a powerful dialectical move.
5
Consider the comparison and the unfinished ending
Ghazali compares his opponents to those who deny that nonexistence supervenes on accidents, arguing that we all accept such supervention. The passage cuts off as he begins a further objection; think about how he might complete the argument.
What would you say to the objection that this only applies if one allows annihilation after existence?

The answer: As for the divisions you have mentioned, even if it were possible for us to defend each one and show that refuting it according to your principles is not sound because your principles contain things of similar nature, we will not go into length and will confine ourselves to one division and say: On what basis do you deny the one who says: Bringing into existence and annihilating are by the will of the Powerful; if God the Exalted and Glorified wills, He brings into existence; if He wills, He annihilates. This is the meaning of His being Powerful to perfection, and He in all of that does not change in Himself; rather the act changes. As for your statement: The agent must produce an act, so what is produced from Him? We say: What is produced from Him is that which becomes new, and that is nonexistence (al-'adam), since there was no nonexistence, then nonexistence became new, so it is what is produced from Him. If you say: It is not a thing, so how is it produced from Him? We say: Since it is not a thing, how does it occur? And the meaning of its being produced from Him is only that it occurs, being attributed to His power. So if its occurrence is conceivable, why would its attribution to power not be conceivable? And what is the difference between you and one who denies the supervening of nonexistence on accidents and forms altogether, saying: Nonexistence is not a thing, so how does it supervene, and how can it be described as supervening and becoming new? We do not doubt that nonexistence is conceivable to supervene on accidents and forms. That which is described with supervention is rationally conceivable to occur, whether you call it a thing or not. So attributing that rationally conceivable occurrence to the power of the Powerful is also rationally conceivable. If it is said: This only follows according to the school of one who allows the nonexistence of a thing after its existence, so it is said to him: What has supervened? And according to us, the existing thing does not become nonexistent; rather the meaning of annihilation is
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage contains Ghazali's response to both the Ash'ari and the philosophers. He begins by noting that although he could refute each of their divisions, he will focus on one key point. He then defends the position that creation and annihilation are both by God's will: when God wills creation, He brings existence; when He wills annihilation, He brings nonexistence. This is the perfection of His power, and God Himself does not change—only the act changes. The opponent objects that an agent must produce an act, so what act does God produce when He annihilates? Ghazali answers: the new occurrence is nonexistence itself (al-'adam). If they object that nonexistence is not a thing, so how can it be produced? Ghazali retorts: since it is not a thing, how can it occur? The meaning of 'produced' is simply that it occurs and is attributed to God's power. If its occurrence is rationally conceivable, then its attribution to power is also conceivable. He compares this to those who deny that nonexistence can supervene on accidents and forms at all, arguing that since we all accept that nonexistence does supervene, it must be an occurrence attributable to power. The passage ends with an objection from someone who allows annihilation after existence, asking 'what has supervened?' and the beginning of Ghazali's response, but the text is cut off.

Misconception: Nonexistence cannot be an act of God because it is not a thing (shay') and therefore cannot be produced.
Correction: Ghazali argues that 'act' does not require a positive entity; any rationally conceivable occurrence attributed to God's power qualifies as an act, even nonexistence.

D) He admits the objection is valid but says it doesn't matter

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends that nonexistence, though not a positive entity, is a real occurrence attributable to God's power because its occurrence is rationally conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience loss or destruction in your life—whether of a loved one, property, or status—do you pause to recognize that this non-existence is an act of God's power just as much as creation? How might this realization change your reaction to trials?
?
Every moment you exist is a direct act of God's preservation; His power keeps you from being annihilated. Are you living with a conscious gratitude for the fact that you are not destroyed this very second? What would change if you actually felt that?
?
Ghazali argues that attributing nonexistence to God's power is rationally conceivable. Yet many people hesitate to credit God with destruction, preferring to attribute it to natural causes or chance. Is there a hidden discomfort in your heart about God bringing about nonexistence? Confess it, and seek to align your understanding with the truth that His power is absolute over all things.
