The Third Argument: The One and Multiplicity
Ghazali states the philosophers' principle and the resulting problem of multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third argument against the philosophers regarding the impossibility of the world being a direct act of God based on their own principle that from the One only one proceeds.

Core Idea
From the absolutely One can come only one; the world is many, so it cannot be directly from God.

Ghazali now presents a new argument that targets a foundational principle of the philosophers: from the One only one proceeds. He shows that this principle, which they accept, leads to a contradiction when applied to the world's multiplicity. In this passage, he lays out the problem clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise structure: it states the principle, the nature of the First Principle, the nature of the world, and the conclusion.
The passage is a single argument.
2
Identify the philosophers' principle
The principle is 'لا يصدر من الواحد إلا واحد' (from the one only one proceeds). Understand that this is a key metaphysical axiom for them.
This is the condition shared between agent and act.
3
Apply the principle to God and the world
Consider: God is one in every respect (absolutely simple). The world is composed of diverse things. According to the principle, the world cannot be a direct act of God. See the logical flow.
Ghazali is using their own principle against them.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If the philosophers are consistent, they must either reject the principle or explain how multiplicity arises from the One. This sets up their theory of intermediaries.
This argument exposes a tension in their system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في استحالة كون العالم فعلا لله تعالى على أصلهم، لشرط مشترك بين الفاعل والفعل، وهو أنهم قالوا: لا يصدر من الواحد إلا شيء واحد؛ والمبدأ الأول واحد من كل وجه، والعالم مركب من مختلفات، فلا يتصور أن يكون فعلا الله تعالى بموجب أصلهم
The third aspect: on the impossibility of the world being an act of God, the Exalted, according to their principle, due to a condition shared between the agent and the act, which is that they said: from the one only one thing proceeds; and the First Principle is one in every respect, and the world is composed of diverse things, so it is inconceivable that it be an act of God, the Exalted, according to their principle.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the core of Ghazali's third argument against the philosophers. It is a statement of the problem. Ghazali begins by identifying a condition that the philosophers themselves impose on the relationship between agent and act: from a cause that is one in every respect, only a single effect can proceed. The philosophers affirm that the First Principle—God—is absolutely one, without any multiplicity. Yet the world is manifestly composed of many different things (various substances, accidents, etc.). Therefore, according to the philosophers' own principle, it is impossible for the world to be a direct act of God. This sets up the need for an explanation of how multiplicity can arise from the One, which the philosophers attempt to provide through intermediaries.

Key Terms
لا يصدر من الواحد إلا واحد
المبدأ الأول
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali agrees with the philosophers' principle 'from the one only one proceeds' as a true metaphysical axiom.
Correction: Ghazali does not necessarily endorse the principle; he is using it to show an internal inconsistency in the philosophers' system. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' principle stated here, from the One that is one in every respect, what can proceed?
A) Many things
B) Only one thing
C) Nothing at all
D) Composite things

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' own principle (from one only one proceeds) makes it impossible for the world, with its multiplicity, to be directly from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself thinking that God is too transcendent or 'simple' to be directly involved in the messy details of your daily life? In what specific situation today can you consciously acknowledge that from the One alone every single thing—no matter how small—proceeds? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
The philosophers argued that multiplicity cannot come directly from the One. As a believer, you affirm that God creates everything directly. Is there an area of your life where you unconsciously attribute outcomes to 'secondary causes' (luck, effort, other people) instead of seeing them as directly from God? Look at the source again: the world is 'composed of diverse things'—do you trust that God's oneness is powerful enough to directly create that diversity in your own life?

The Philosophers' Self-Contradictory Stance
Ghazali classifies groups and refutes philosophers by exposing internal contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophers' position inherently contradictory without needing further refutation.

Core Idea
The philosophers combine eternal world and a creator, making their doctrine self-refuting.

Ghazali begins by mapping out the theological landscape: the people of truth, who logically infer a Maker from the world's origination, and the materialists, who deny a Maker altogether. Then he turns to the philosophers, who try to have it both ways. In this passage, you'll see how Ghazali exposes this tension as a fatal flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for structure
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali first sets up two consistent positions, then introduces the philosophers as a third inconsistent one.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions 'people of truth' and 'Dahriyya'. Note their positions on the world's origin and Maker.
3
Understand the contradiction
The philosophers affirm both an eternal world and a creator. Ask yourself why this is contradictory. The commentary will explain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٤] في بيان عجزهم عن الاستدلال على وجود الصانع للعالم فنقول : الناس فرقتان : فرقة أهل الحق وقد رأوا أن العالم حادث ، وعلموا ضرورة أن الحادث لا يوجد من نفسه ، فافتقر إلى صانع ، فعقل مذهبهم في القول بالصانع وفرقة أخرى هم الدهرية ، وقد رأوا أن العالم قديم ، كما هو عليه ، ولم يثبتوا له صانعاً ، ومعتقدهم مفهوم ، وإن كان الدليل يدل على بطلانه . وأما الفلاسفة فقد رأوا أن العالم قديم ، ثم أثبتوا له صانعاً وهذا المذهب بوضعه متناقض ، لا يحتاج فيه إلى إبطال .
Issue [4]: On explaining their inability to deduce proof for the existence of the Maker of the world. We say: People are two groups: The group of the people of truth, who saw that the world is originated, and knew necessarily that the originated does not come into existence from itself, so it needed a Maker, so their doctrine regarding the affirmation of a Maker is rational. And another group, the Dahriyya (materialists), who saw that the world is eternal as it is, and did not affirm a Maker for it, and their belief is understandable, even though proof indicates its falsity. As for the philosophers, they saw that the world is eternal, then affirmed a Maker for it, and this doctrine in its very formulation is contradictory, not needing to be refuted.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a refutation by contradiction. Ghazali begins with the title of the issue: showing the philosophers' inability to prove the existence of the Creator. He then divides people into two main groups: the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) who correctly infer a Maker from the origination of the world, and the Dahriyya (eternalists or materialists) who deny a Maker and hold the world eternal—their position is at least consistent, though false. The philosophers, however, combine two incompatible positions: they hold the world eternal (like the Dahriyya) yet affirm a Maker (like the people of truth). This juxtaposition is inherently contradictory (متناقض بوضعه) and thus requires no further refutation; its self-contradiction is sufficient.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
حادث
صانع
الدهرية
قديم
متناقض
إبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' position is simply false like the Dahriyya.
Correction: Ghazali shows it is self-contradictory, not merely false, because it combines incompatible claims.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which group's position is inherently contradictory?
A) The people of truth
B) The Dahriyya
C) The philosophers
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers does not require extended argument; their position is self-contradictory because it combines an eternal world with a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali says the philosophers' position is 'self-contradictory in its very formulation.' Think about your own beliefs about Allah's existence and the world. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if examined closely, would be self-contradictory? What specific step will you take this week to identify and resolve such contradictions in your faith?
?
The philosophers wanted to have both an eternal world and a creator, but that made their position incoherent. In your life, are you trying to combine two incompatible commitments—like pursuing worldly success and spiritual purity without prioritising one over the other? What is one practical change you must make today to end this contradiction?
?
Ghazali does not even need to refute the philosophers at length because their error is self-evident. Is there an error in your own practice of Islam that is so obvious that you have been ignoring it, thinking it requires deep study to fix, when really the contradiction is plain? What will you do about it right now?

Philosophers' Definition of the First Cause and Proof by Infinite Regress
Ghazali presents the philosophers' alternative definition of God and their proof by infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of the First Cause as an uncaused cause and their proof via infinite regress.

Core Idea
The philosophers' First Cause is not a free agent but an uncaused cause, proven by regress.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection against the Ash'ari conception of God as a free agent. They propose an alternative: the First Cause is an uncaused cause of the world, and they offer a proof based on the impossibility of an infinite regress. This sets up the debate Ghazali will later refute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Philosophers' Objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the philosophers' redefinition of 'Maker' as an uncaused cause.
Notice they explicitly deny a free agent acting after not acting.
2
Examine the Infinite Regress Proof
Follow the logical steps: either the world has a cause or not; if it has a cause, that cause must also have a cause or not; an infinite chain is impossible, so a first cause exists.
3
Understand the Conclusion
Notice that the proof establishes a being with no cause of its existence, which they call the First Principle, and that this cannot be the heavens because of their multiplicity.
4
Prepare for Refutation
Remember that Ghazali is presenting the philosophers' position, which he will later critique. Keep this in mind.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن إذا قلنا : إن للعالم صانعاً ، لم نرد به فاعلا مختاراً ، يفعل بعد أن لم يكن يفعل كما يشاهد فى أصناف الفاعلين من الخياط ، والنساج ، والبناء ، بل نعنى به علة العالم ، ونسميه المبدأ الأول ، على معنى أنه لا علة لوجوده ، وهو علة لوجود غيره ، فإن سميناه صانعاً فبهذا التأويل وثبوت موجود لا علة لوجوده ، يقول عليه البرهان القطعى على قرب ، فإنا نقول : العالم ، موجوداته إما أن يكون لها علة ، أو لا علة لها ، فإن كان لها علة فتلك العلة لها علة أم لا علة لها ؟ ! وكذا القول فى علة العلة ، فإما أن تتسلسل إلى غير نهاية وهو محال ، وإما أن تنتهى إلى طرف ، فالأخير علة أولى ، لا علة لوجودها ، فنسميها المبدأ الأول . وإن كان العالم موجوداً بنفسه لا علة له ، فقد ظهر المبدأ الأول ، فإنه لم نعن به إلا موجوداً لا علة له، وهو ثابت بالضرورة. نعم لا يجوز أن يكون المبدأ الأول هو السموات ، لأنها عدد ، ودليل
If it is said: When we say that the world has a Maker, we do not mean by it a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) who acts after not having acted as is observed in the types of agents such as the tailor, weaver, and builder. Rather we mean by it the cause of the world, and we call it the First Principle (al-mabdaʾ al-awwal), meaning that there is no cause for its existence, and it is the cause of the existence of other than itself. If we call it 'Maker' then by this interpretation. And the existence of a being with no cause for its existence is established by an apodictic proof in brief. For we say: The world and its existents either have a cause or have no cause. If it has a cause, then does that cause have a cause or not? And the same applies to the cause of the cause. Either it goes on in an infinite chain, which is impossible, or it terminates at an endpoint; the last is a first cause, with no cause for its existence; we call it the First Principle. And if the world exists by itself with no cause, then the First Principle is manifest, for we mean by it nothing other than a being with no cause, and it is established by necessity. Indeed, it is not permissible that the First Principle be the heavens, because they are multiple, and a proof...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection and their alternative account. The objection begins: when we affirm a Maker, we do not mean a voluntary agent (fāʿil mukhtār) like human artisans who act after inactivity; rather we mean a cause (ʿilla) of the world, which they call the First Principle (al-mabdaʾ al-awwal). This First Principle is defined as that which has no cause for its existence (lā ʿilla li-wujūdihi) and is the cause of everything else. They claim that the existence of such an uncaused being is provable by an apodictic proof (burhān qaṭʿī). The proof is the famous infinite regress argument: every existent either has a cause or not. If it has a cause, then that cause either has a cause or not; you either go to infinity (impossible) or terminate at a first cause without a cause. If the world exists without a cause, that itself is the First Principle—an uncaused being. So in either case, an uncaused being is established. However, this cannot be the heavens because they are multiple (and multiplicity requires a cause). The proof is presented as a valid logical demonstration, but Ghazali will likely refute it later; here he just presents it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers mean a free agent when they say 'Maker'.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that they mean an impersonal, uncaused cause, not a voluntary agent.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what does 'Maker' mean?
A) A free agent who acts after not acting
B) The cause of the world and the first principle with no cause of its existence
C) The heavens
D) An eternal agent who always acts

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define the First Cause as an uncaused cause and prove its necessity by showing that infinite regress is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is the Creator,' do you ever find yourself thinking of Him as a distant, impersonal force like the philosophers' First Cause? What would it take to truly feel His freedom and personal love in your daily life?
?
The philosophers proved an uncaused cause by logic. Does your belief in Allah rest more on arguments or on a personal relationship? How can you move from knowing the proof to trusting the Person?
?
If the infinite regress argument shows that all things depend on an uncaused cause, what does that mean for your sense of self-sufficiency? Are there areas where you live as if you are the first cause of your own life? How can you consciously depend on Allah today?

The Philosophers' Causal Argument
Philosophers define and prove the First Principle through causal argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of the First Principle and the structure of their causal argument against infinite regress.

Core Idea
Every being must trace back to an uncaused cause: that is the First Principle.

The philosophers begin by clarifying what they mean by 'Maker'—not a free agent but an uncaused cause. They then present a decisive proof that such an existent must exist, using the impossibility of infinite regress. This argument is foundational to their metaphysics and is the target of Ghazali's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' clarification
Notice how they distinguish their concept of Maker from ordinary agents. For them, 'Maker' means cause, not free choice.
This sets up their rationalist approach.
2
Follow the causal argument
They argue that either the world has a cause or not. If it has a cause, that cause either has a cause or not, leading to either infinite regress (impossible) or a first cause.
3
Identify the conclusion
The argument concludes that an existent with no cause is necessarily established. They call it the First Principle.
They also note that it cannot be a body or composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن إذا قلنا : إن للعالم صانعاً ، لم نرد به فاعلا مختاراً ، يفعل بعد أن لم يكن يفعل كما يشاهد فى أصناف الفاعلين من الخياط ، والنساج ، والبناء ، بل نعنى به علة العالم ، ونسميه المبدأ الأول ، على معنى أنه لا علة لوجوده ، وهو علة لوجود غيره ، فإن سميناه صانعاً فبهذا التأويل وثبوت موجود لا علة لوجوده ، يقوم عليه البرهان القطعى على قرب ، فإنا نقول : العالم ، موجوداته إما أن يكون لها علة ، أو لا علة لها ، فإن كان لها علة فتلك العلة لها علة أم لا علة لها ؟ ! وكذا القول فى علة العلة ، فإما أن تتسلسل إلى غير نهاية وهو محال ، وإما أن تنتهى إلى طرف ، فالأخير علة أولى ، لا علة لوجودها ، فنسميها المبدأ الأول . وإن كان العالم موجوداً بنفسه لا علة له ، فقد ظهر المبدأ الأول ، فإنه لم نعن به إلا موجوداً لا علة له، وهو ثابت بالضرورة. نعم لا يجوز أن يكون المبدأ الأول هو السموات ، لأنها عدد ، ودليل التوحيد يمنعه ، فيعرف بطلانه بالنظر فى صفة المبدأ ، ولا يجوز أن يقال : إنه جسم ، أو جسم سماء واحدة ، أو شمس واحدة ، أو غيرها ، لأنه والجسم مركب من الصورة والهيولى ، والمبدأ الأول لا يجوز أن يكون مركباً. وذلك يعرف بنظر ثان والمقصود أن موجوداً ، لا علة لوجوده ، ثابت بالضرورة والاتفاق - وإنما الخلاف في الصفات - وهو الذى نعنيه بالمبدأ الأول . والجواب من وجهين : أحدهما : أنه يلزم على مساق مذهبكم ، أن تكون أجسام العالم قديمة كذلك لا علة لها ، وقولكم إن بطلان ذلك يعلم بنظر ثان فسيبطل ذلك عليكم في مسألة التوحيد ، وفى نفى الصفات ، بعد الثاني : وهو الخاص بهذه المسألة أن يقال : ثبت تقديراً أن هذه الموجودات لها علة ولعلتها علة ولعلة العلة علة كذلك وهكذا إلى غير نهاية، وقولكم : إنه يستحيل إثبات علل لا نهاية لها ، لا يستقيم منكم ، فإنا نقول : عرفتم ذلك ضرورة بغير وسط أو عرفتموه بوسط ؟ ! ولا سبيل إلى دعوى الضرورة ، وكل مسلك ذكرتموه في النظر بطل عليكم بتجويز حوادث لا أول لها ، وإذا جاز أن يدخل في الوجود ما لا نهاية له ، فلم يبعد أن يكون بعضها علة للبعض ، وينتهى من الطرف الأخير إلى معلول لا معلول له ، ولا ينتهى من الجانب الآخر إلى علة لا علة لها ؟ ! كما أن الزمان السابق له آخر وهو الآن الراهن ، ولا أول له فإن زعمتم أن الحوادث الماضية ، ليست موجودة معاً في الحال ، ولا في بعض الأحوال ، والمعدوم لا يوصف بالتناهى ، وعدم التناهي ؛ فيلزمكم في النفوس البشرية المفارقة للأبدان ، فإنها لا تفنى عندكم ، والموجود المفارق للبدن من النفوس ، لا نهاية لأعدادها ، إذ لم تزل نطفة من إنسان ، وإنسان من
For if it is said: When we say that the world has a Maker, we do not mean by it a free agent that acts after not having acted, as observed among kinds of agents like the tailor, weaver, builder; rather we mean by it the cause of the world, and we call it the First Principle, meaning that it has no cause for its existence, and it is the cause for the existence of others. If we call it Maker, it is by this interpretation. And the establishment of an existent that has no cause for its existence is proven by a decisive proof soon. For we say: The world and its existents either have a cause or have no cause. If they have a cause, then does that cause have a cause or not? And likewise for the cause of the cause. Either it regresses infinitely, which is impossible, or it ends at a terminus; the final is a first cause which has no cause for its existence, we call it the First Principle. And if the world exists by itself without a cause, then the First Principle has appeared, for we mean by it only an existent that has no cause, and it is established necessarily. Yes, it is not permissible that the First Principle be the heavens, because they are multiple, and the proof of unity prevents that; its falsehood is known by considering the attribute of the Principle. And it is not permissible to say that it is a body, or a body of a single heaven, or a single sun, or other than that, because the body is composed of form and matter, and the First Principle cannot be composite. And that is known by a second consideration. And the point is that an existent having no cause for its existence is established necessarily and by agreement—only difference is in attributes—and that is what we mean by the First Principle. The answer is from two aspects: First: It follows from the course of your doctrine that the bodies of the world are likewise eternal without a cause, and your saying that the falsehood of that is known by a second consideration will be refuted against you in the problem of unity and negation of attributes. Second: Which is specific to this problem: it may be said: Granted that these existents have a cause, and that cause has a cause, and the cause of the cause has a cause, and so on to infinity; and your saying that it is impossible to establish infinite causes is not consistent from you. For we say: Did you know that necessarily without a middle term, or did you know it by a middle term? There is no way to claim necessity. And every path you have mentioned in consideration is refuted against you by your allowing events with no beginning. And if it is permissible that into existence enters what has no end, then why is it unlikely that some of them are causes for others, and it ends from the final side at an effect that has no effect, and does not end from the other side at a cause that has no cause? Just as past time has an end which is the present moment, but has no beginning. For if you claim that past events do not exist together at present nor at any time, and the non-existent is not described as finite or infinite, then it follows for you concerning the human souls that separate from bodies—for they do not perish according to you—and the existent separated from the body among souls has no end to its number, since there has not ceased a sperm from a man, and a man from...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection and Ghazali's two-fold refutation. The philosophers clarify that by 'Maker' they do not mean a free agent but a cause (illa). They call it the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) meaning an existent that has no cause for its existence and is the cause of others. They provide a causal argument: everything either has a cause or not; if it has a cause, that cause either has a cause or not, leading to either infinite regress (impossible) or a first cause. If there is no cause, then that is the first cause itself. Thus an uncaused cause is established necessarily. They then argue that the first principle cannot be the heavens (multiple bodies) or a body because bodies are composite. Ghazali's first refutation: Their own reasoning would imply that the bodies of the world are also eternal and uncaused, but they claim this is known false by a second consideration—this will be refuted later in the discussion on unity and attributes. Second refutation: Even if we grant infinite regress of causes, they claim it is impossible, but they themselves allow infinite series of past events (like human souls after death). So they cannot consistently hold that infinite regress is impossible. Ghazali pointedly asks whether they know this necessarily or by argument—they cannot claim necessity. Their acceptance of past events without beginning undermines their rejection of infinite regress. The passage ends by beginning to develop the example of infinite human souls, but is cut off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument is a proof for a free creator similar to human agents.
Correction: The philosophers explicitly deny that the Maker is a free agent; it is a necessary cause.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what does 'First Principle' mean?
A) The first creator who acts freely
B) An existent that has no cause for its existence and is the cause of others
C) The heavens themselves
D) A composite being

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' causal argument establishes the necessary existence of an uncaused cause, which they call the First Principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every being requires a cause, what does that say about your own existence? How does it feel to know you are contingent and your existence depends entirely on the First Principle? Does this truth change how you live today?
?
The philosophers argue that the cause of the world is not a free agent like human makers. How does this understanding affect your relationship with God? Is there an area of your life where you act as if you are the uncaused cause, independent of Him?
?
If the first principle is uncaused and the cause of all else, what does that imply about your reliance on Him for every moment? Are there any parts of your life you still think are self-sufficient?

Ghazali's Two-Fold Refutation
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' causal argument in two ways.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two criticisms of the philosophers' causal argument: one regarding implications about the world's bodies, and the second highlighting inconsistency about infinite regress.

Core Idea
The philosophers cannot reject infinite regress while accepting infinite past events like human souls.

After presenting the philosophers' argument, Ghazali offers a two-part response. The first points out that their own reasoning would make the bodies of the world eternal and uncaused, which they deny. The second refutation targets their rejection of infinite regress: since they accept infinite series of past events (such as human souls), they cannot consistently argue that an infinite causal chain is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's first refutation
Notice that he claims the philosophers' method would imply that the world's bodies are eternal and without cause, a conclusion they themselves reject.
This is a charge of inconsistency in their own system.
2
Identify the second refutation's structure
Ghazali grants the possibility of infinite regress and challenges the philosophers' claim that it is impossible. He asks whether they know this necessarily or by argument.
3
Analyze the example of human souls
The philosophers accept an infinite number of human souls that have separated from bodies. This is an actual infinite series—so why can't causes be infinite?
The passage cuts off here, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If there is no cause, then that is the first cause itself. Thus an uncaused cause is established necessarily. They then argue that the first principle cannot be the heavens (multiple bodies) or a body because bodies are composite. So they cannot consistently hold that infinite regress is impossible. Ghazali pointedly asks whether they know this necessarily or by argument—they cannot claim necessity. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies that any causal argument is valid.
Correction: Ghazali only refutes this particular formulation and shows its inconsistency; he does not reject causality entirely.

Checkpoint

A) The creation of the world in time
B) The existence of an infinite number of human souls
C) The oneness of God
D) The impossibility of a body

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's refutation exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' position: they accept infinite series of past events but reject infinite causal regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you hold a belief that logical consistency is important, how do you react to Ghazali's exposure of inconsistency in the philosophers' position? Where in your own life might you accept something as impossible while simultaneously believing in things that imply it? Are you willing to examine those areas with the same scrutiny Ghazali applied?
?
Imagine you are convinced that an infinite regress is impossible, yet you believe in an eternal afterlife with no end. Does that not also imply an infinite series? How does the distinction between infinite in the past and infinite in the future hold up under Ghazali's challenge? What does that reveal about your own assumptions?
?
Ghazali uses the example of human souls to argue that infinite numbers exist. If you believe in a Day of Judgment with countless souls, does the idea of infinite numbers trouble you? How does that affect your view of God's power and the nature of creation? Will you let this lesson push you to think more deeply about what you accept without question?

Refuting the Objection: Necessary Existence Not Dependent on Possible Things
Ghazali refutes the objection that necessary existence depends on possible existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the claim that the Necessary Existence is constituted by possible existences, and how he uses the analogy of time and rotations to argue that necessity applies to the aggregate, not each part.

Core Idea
Necessity of the whole does not require necessity of each part.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key objection from the philosophers: if the Necessary Existence has no cause, but possible existences depend on it, does that not make the Necessary Existence dependent on them? Ghazali's response uses a clever analogy involving time and rotations to show that necessity can belong to an aggregate without each individual component being necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection and Initial Response
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated and Ghazali gives his initial answer. Notice that he redefines the terms according to his own definition.
2
Identify the Analogy
Identify the analogy of time and rotations. Understand how time is eternal but each rotation is temporal, and how the aggregate of rotations has no beginning.
3
Apply the Analogy to the Problem
See how Ghazali applies this analogy: similarly, each possible existent may have a cause, but the aggregate (the Necessary Existence) does not. Understand the logical move.
4
Understand the Implications
Grasp the conclusion that the philosophers cannot deny infinite causes and therefore cannot prove the First Principle, and their distinction is arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زائدة على ذاته ، والكل ليس بممكن . على معنى أنه ليس له علة زائدة على ذاته خارجة عنه ؛ وإن أريد بلفظ الممكن غير ما أردناه ، فهو ليس بمفهوم . فإن قيل : فهذا يؤدى إلى أن يتقوم واجب الوجود بممكنات الوجود ، وهو محال . قلنا : إن أردتم بالواجب والممكن ما ذكرناه فهو نفس المطلوب، ولا نسلم أنه محال ، وهو كقول القائل : يستحيل أن يتقوم القديم بالحوادث : والزمان عندهم قديم ، وآحاد الدورات حادثة ، وهى ذوات أوائل ، والمجموع لا أول له ، فقد تقوم ما لا أول له بذوات أوائل، وصدق ذوات الأوائل على الآحاد ، ولم يصدق على المجموع ، فكذلك يقال على كل واحد : إن له علة ، ولا يقال : إن للمجموع علة ، وليس كل ما صدق على الآحاد ، يلزم أن يصدق على المجموع ، إذ قد يصدق على كل واحد ، أنه واحد ، وأنه بعض ، وأنه جزء ، ولا يصدق على المجموع ، وكل موضع عيناه من الأرض ، فإنه قد استضاء بالشمس في النهار ، وأظلم بالليل ، وكل واحد حادث ، بعد أن لم يكن ، أى له أول ، والمجموع عندهم ما له أول . فتبين من يجوز حوادث لا أول لها ، وهى صور العناصر الأربعة أن المتغيرات ، فلا يتمكن من إنكار علل لا نهاية لها ، ويخرج من هذا ، أنه لا سبيل لهم إلى الوصول إلى إثبات المبدأ الأول، لهذا الإشكال ، ويرجع فرقهم إلى التحكم المحض
Superadded to His essence, and the whole is not possible. Meaning that He has no cause superadded to His essence external to Him; and if by the term 'possible' is meant other than what we intended, then it is not understood. If it is said: This leads to the Necessary Existence being constituted by possible existences, which is impossible. We say: If by the necessary and the possible you mean what we mentioned, then it is the very thing sought, and we do not concede that it is impossible. It is like one saying: It is impossible for the eternal to be constituted by temporal events. Time, according to them, is eternal, and the individuals of rotations are temporal, and they are things with beginnings, and the aggregate has no beginning. So that which has no beginning has been constituted by things with beginnings. The truth of 'things with beginnings' applies to individuals, but does not apply to the aggregate. Likewise, it is said of each individual that it has a cause, but it is not said that the aggregate has a cause. Not everything that is true of individuals necessarily is true of the aggregate. For it may be true of each individual that it is one, that it is a part, and that it is a component, but it is not true of the aggregate. Every spot we designate from the earth, it was illuminated by the sun in the day and became dark at night. Each individual is originated after not having been, i.e., it has a beginning, but the aggregate, according to them, has no beginning. So it became clear that whoever allows events with no beginning—which are the forms of the four elements—that these are changing, then he cannot deny infinite causes. From this results that there is no way for them to reach the proof of the First Principle due to this difficulty, and their distinction reduces to sheer arbitrariness.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that if necessary existence has no cause but depends on possible existences, then it is constituted by them, which is impossible. Ghazali's answer is that necessity applies to the aggregate, not to each individual. He uses the analogy of time and rotations: time is eternal, but each rotation is temporal. The aggregate of rotations has no beginning, though each individual does. Similarly, each possible existent may have a cause, but the aggregate (the Necessary Existence as a whole) does not need a cause. He then argues that the philosophers who accept eternal rotations (events without a beginning) cannot deny infinite causes, and thus cannot prove the First Principle. Their position is arbitrary.

ممكن الوجود

ذوات أوائل
المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Necessary Existence must be necessary in every aspect and cannot relate to possible things.
Correction: Ghazali shows that necessity of the whole does not require each part to be necessary; the aggregate can be necessary while individuals are contingent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ghazali's response to the objection that the Necessary Existence depends on possible existences?
A) The Necessary Existence cannot have any relation to possible things.
B) Necessity applies to the aggregate, not to each individual part.
C) The Necessary Existence is itself a possible existent.
D) The philosophers have no proof for the eternity of rotations.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the objection by showing that necessity applies to the aggregate, not each individual, and that the philosophers' own acceptance of eternal events undermines their argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the existence of God, do you ever assume that because the universe is made up of countless caused things, the One who brought it all about must also be caused? Where in your heart does that assumption come from, and how does this lesson's distinction between part and whole challenge it?
?
Ghazali exposes a contradiction in the philosophers' position: they accept eternal events yet deny infinite causes. Are there beliefs you hold that, when examined closely, contradict each other? Will you take time this week to identify one such inconsistency and resolve it?
?
This lesson demonstrates that what is true of each part may not be true of the whole. In your own life, do you judge God's actions or attributes based on how you understand created things? How can you train yourself to release that comparison and submit to His uniqueness?

Cumulative Persistence and Infinite Regress
Ghazali responds to the objection that rotations and souls are not simultaneously present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the concept of cumulative persistence to re-establish the problem of infinite regress even when past events no longer exist.

Core Idea
Persistence can create infinite accumulation from finite origins.

The philosophers object that since past rotations and souls no longer exist, they cannot form an infinite series—only present beings count. Ghazali reframes the issue by asking whether things can originate and then persist. If they can, then daily origination with persistence yields an infinite number of existents up to the present, regardless of whether the originating event is over. This lesson unpacks the objection, the two philosophical views on souls, and Ghazali's decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Notice its core claim: only what exists in external reality counts; mental estimations do not create infinite series.
The objection uses rotations and elemental forms as examples.
2
Examine the views on souls
Identify the two positions: (1) souls are eternal and unite after death, so no multiplicity; (2) souls depend on bodies and perish. Both imply no infinite regress of souls.
3
Understand Ghazali's question
Ghazali asks whether something can occur and then persist. This is the key pivot. Consider why this matters for the infinite regress.
The philosophers affirm infinite rotations, so they must allow persistence of effects.
4
See how persistence re-establishes the problem
If things can persist, then daily origination with persistence leads to an infinite number of existents today. This works for souls, angels, or anything persistent, regardless of whether the originating rotation is over.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الدورات ليست موجودة فى الحال ، ولا صور العناصر ، وإنما الموجود منها صورة واحدة بالفعل ، وما لا وجود له ، لا يوصف بالتناهى وعدم التناهي ، إلا إذا قدر في الوهم وجودها ، ولا يبعد ما يقدر في الوهم ، وإن كانت المقدرات أيضاً بعضها علل لبعض ، فالإنسان قد يفرض ذلك في وهمه ، وإنما الكلام في الموجود في الأعيان ، لا في الأذهان . فلا يبقى إلا نفوس الأموات وقد ذهب بعض الفلاسفة إلى أنها كانت واحدة أزلية قبل التعلق بالأبدان وعند مفارقة الأبدان تتحد ، فلا يكون فيها عدد ، فضلا أن توصف بأنها لا نهاية لها ، وقال آخرون : النفس تابعة لمزاج البدن ، وإنما معنى الموت عدمها ، ولا قوام لها بجوهرها دون الجسم ، فإذن لا وجود للنفوس ، إلا في حق الأحياء ، والأحياء الموجودون محصورون ، ولا تنتفى النهاية عنهم ، والمعدومون لا يوصفون أصلاً ، لا بوجود النهاية ولا بعدمها إلا في الوهم ، إذا فرضوا موجودين والجواب : أن هذا الإشكال في النفوس ، أوردناه على ابن سينا و الفارابي والمحققين منهم ، إذ حكموا بأن النفس جوهر قائم بنفسه ، وهو و المفسرين من عدل هذا اختيار : أرسطاليس الأوائل ؛ ومن عن المسلك فنقول له : هل يتصور أن يحدث شيء يبقى أم لا ؟ ! فإن قالوا : لا ، فهو محال ، وإن قالوا : نعم ، قلنا : فإذا قدرنا كل يوم حدوث شيء وبقاءه ، اجتمع إلى الآن لا محالة موجودات لا نهاية لها ، فالدورة وإن كانت منقضية ، فحصول موجود فيها يبقى ولا ينقضى ، غير مستحيل ، وبهذا التقدير يتقرر الإشكال ، ولا غرض فى أن يكون ذلك الباقى ، نفس آدمى ، أو جنى ، أو شيطان ، أو ملك ، أو ما شئت من الموجودات ، وهذا لازم على كل مذهب لهم ، إذ أثبتوا دورات لا نهاية لها .
If it is said: The rotations are not present at the moment, nor the forms of the elements. Rather, what exists of them is only one form in actuality. That which has no existence is not described by finitude or infinitude, except if their existence is estimated in the imagination. And what is estimated in the imagination is not far-fetched, even if the estimated things are also causes of each other. For a person may suppose that in his imagination. But the discussion concerns what exists in external entities, not in minds. Then nothing remains except the souls of the dead. Some philosophers held that they were one eternal thing before attachment to bodies, and upon separation from bodies they unite, so there is no number among them, let alone being described as infinite. Others said: The soul is dependent on the temperament of the body, and the meaning of death is its non-existence; it has no subsistence by its essence without the body. So there is no existence for souls except in the case of the living. The living who exist are limited, and finitude is not removed from them. The non-existent are not described at all, neither by existence of finitude nor by its non-existence, except in imagination, if they are supposed as existing. The answer: This difficulty concerning souls we raised against Ibn Sina and al-Farabi and the verifiers among them, since they ruled that the soul is a substance subsisting by itself, and it is, and the exegetes from this just choice: Aristotle of the ancients. And whoever from this path, we say to him: Is it conceivable that something should occur and then persist, or not? If they say: No, that is impossible. If they say: Yes, we say: Then if we suppose each day an occurrence of something and its persistence, there would have accumulated up to now necessarily existences without end. So the rotation, even if it is ended, the obtaining of an existent in it that persists and does not end is not impossible. By this estimation the difficulty is established. There is no purpose in that the persisting thing be a human soul, or a jinn, or a devil, or an angel, or whatever you wish from existents. And this is necessary for every school of theirs, since they have affirmed infinite rotations.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objection argues that rotations and elemental forms are not present simultaneously, so they cannot form an infinite series; only what exists in reality counts, not mental estimations. It then turns to souls, noting two philosophical views: one that souls are eternal and unite after death (no infinite number), and another that souls are dependent on bodies and perish with death (only limited souls exist). Ghazali's answer focuses on souls: he says this difficulty was raised against Ibn Sina and al-Farabi who hold the soul to be a self-subsistent substance. He then asks whether something can originate and persist. If they admit persistence, then by supposing daily origination and persistence, an infinite number of existents would accumulate up to now. Thus the rotation, though ended, can contain a persisting existent. This re-establishes the difficulty of an infinite regress, regardless of whether the persistent is a human soul or any other being. The philosophers cannot escape this because they affirm infinite rotations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers think that since past events no longer exist, they cannot contribute to an infinite series.
Correction: Ghazali shows that if events produce persistent effects, those effects accumulate, leading to an actual infinite regardless of the event's presence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's response, how can the difficulty of infinite series be re-established even if rotations are not present?
A) By supposing that souls are eternal and unite after death.
B) By supposing daily origination of things that persist, accumulating infinite existents.
C) By arguing that rotations are imaginary and thus infinite in mind.
D) By denying that souls exist at all.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that even if past events no longer exist, the persistence of originated things creates an infinite accumulation, re-establishing the problem of infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argues that only present existences matter. Reflect on a past sin you have committed that you think is 'gone.' Has its effect truly vanished, or does it still accumulate in your soul? What will you do today to address that persistence before Allah?
?
Ghazali shows that if something can persist, then daily persistence leads to infinite accumulation. Consider a bad habit you repeat daily. How many times has it persisted? Do you believe it will ever stop unless you actively break it? What concrete step will you take right now to halt its perpetuation?
?
This lesson addresses different views on the soul's persistence. If you truly believe your soul persists forever, how does that change the urgency of your next decision? Is there an action you have been postponing that requires immediate attention in light of eternity?

Refuting the Philosophers' Proof for God's Oneness
Ghazali presents and refutes the philosophers' proof for God's oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' argument for God's oneness, particularly his objection to the division of necessity of existence.

Core Idea
The philosophers' division of necessity of existence is invalid because the term is ambiguous.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the first philosophical proof for God's oneness and then dismantles it. The philosophers argue that if there were two necessary existents, they would share a type, leading to a contradiction. Ghazali shows that their key division is based on a misunderstanding of what 'necessity of existence' means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the philosophers' analogy with humanity.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
The philosophers argue that if there were two necessary existents, they would share the type 'necessity of existence', which must belong to each either by essence or by cause. Both options lead to contradiction.
Trace the logic from the premise to the conclusion.
3
Understand Ghazali's refutation
Ghazali points out that the term 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous. If it means 'negation of cause', then the division into 'by essence' or 'by cause' is meaningless. He also rejects the analogy with humanity.
Focus on why the division is erroneous.
4
Evaluate the analogy
The philosophers compare the type 'necessity of existence' to the type 'human', but Ghazali argues that humanity is a universal that applies to multiple individuals due to a cause (matter), whereas the uncaused has no such cause.
Consider whether the analogy holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٥] في بيان عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل ، على أن الله واحد وأنه لا يجوز فرض اثنين واجب الوجود كل واحد منهما لا علة له . واستدلالهم على هذا بمسلكين : المسلك الأول : قولهم : إنهما لو كانا اثنين ، لكان نوع وجوب الوجود مقولا على كل واحد منهما ، وما قيل عليه : إنه واجب الوجود فلا يخلو : أن يكون وجوب وجوده لذاته ، أو وجوب الوجود له لعلة . فإن كان لذاته فلا يتصور أن يكون لغيره ، وإن كان لعلة فتكون ذات واجب الوجود معلولا ، وقد اقتضت علة له وجوب الوجود ، ونحن لا نريد بواجب الوجود ، إلا ما لا ارتباط لوجوده بعلة ، بجهة من الجهات . وزعموا : أن نوع الإنسان مقول على زيد وعلى عمرو ؛ وليس زيد إنساناً لذاته ، إذ لو كان إنساناً لذاته ، لما كان عمرو إنساناً ؛ بل لعلة جعلته إنساناً ، وقد جعلت عمراً أيضاً إنساناً ، فتكثرت الإنسانية بتكثر المادة الحاملة لها ، وتعلقها بالمادة معلول ليس لذات الإنسانية ؛ فكذلك ثبوت وجوب الوجود لواجب الوجود ، إن كان لذاته ، فلا يكون إلا له ، وإن كان لعلة ، فهو إذن معلول ، وليس بواجب الوجود، فقد ظهر بهذا أن واجب الوجود لا بد أن يكون واحداً . قلنا : قولكم : نوع وجوب الوجود لواجب الوجود لذاته أو لعلة ، تقسيم خطأ فى وضعه ، فإنا قد بينا أن لفظ وجوب الوجود فيه إجمال ، إلا أن يراد به نفى العلة ، فنستعمل هذه العبارة فنقول : لم يستحيل ثبوت موجودين لا علة لهما ، وليس أحدهما علة للآخر ؟! فقولكم : إن الذي لا علة له ؛ لا علة له لذاته أو لسبب ، تقسيم خطأ ؛ لأن نفى العلة واستغناء الوجود عن العلة لا يطلب
Question [5]: On explaining their inability to establish the proof that God is one and that it is not permissible to suppose two necessary existents (wajib al-wujud), each of which has no cause. And their argument for this is by two approaches: The first approach: Their saying: If there were two, then the type (naw') of necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) would be predicated of each of them. And that which is said to be necessary of existence is not free from either its necessity of existence being for its essence (li-dhatihi) or its necessity of existence being for it from a cause. If it is for its essence, then it is inconceivable that it be for another. And if it is from a cause, then the essence of the necessary existent would be an effect (ma'lul), and the cause would require necessity of existence for it. And we do not mean by 'necessary of existence' except that whose existence has no connection to a cause in any respect. And they claimed that the type 'human' is predicated of Zayd and 'Amr, but Zayd is not a human by his essence, for if he were a human by his essence, then 'Amr would not be a human. Rather, by a cause that made him human, and it also made 'Amr human, so humanity multiplies by the multiplication of the matter bearing it, and its attachment to matter is an effect not of the essence of humanity. Similarly, the establishment of necessity of existence for the necessary existent, if it is for its essence, then it exists only for Him, and if it is from a cause, then He is an effect and not a necessary existent. Thus it has become clear that the necessary existent must be one. We say: Your saying: The type of necessity of existence for the necessary existent is either for its essence or from a cause, this division is erroneous in its formulation. For we have already explained that the expression 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous (ijmal), unless what is meant is the negation of cause. So we use this expression and say: Why is it impossible for there to exist two existents with no cause, neither being a cause for the other? So your saying: That which has no cause has no cause either by its essence or by a reason, this division is erroneous, because the negation of cause and independence of existence from cause does not require...
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical proof for the oneness of God and Ghazali's refutation. The philosophers argue: if there were two necessary existents, they would share the type 'necessity of existence'. This type must either belong to each by essence or by cause. If by essence, it is unique to that one; if by cause, then that one is an effect. But a necessary existent cannot be an effect. Therefore only one can exist. They draw an analogy with humanity: humanity is a universal that applies to multiple individuals due to a cause (the matter), not by essence. Ghazali refutes this by pointing out that the division is based on ambiguous terminology. 'Necessity of existence' either means 'having no cause' or something else. If it means 'having no cause', then it is simply a negation, and there is no reason why two uncaused beings cannot exist. The philosophers' analogy with humanity fails because humanity is a universal that applies to multiple individuals due to a cause, but for the uncaused, there is no cause, so the analogy is invalid. Furthermore, the philosophers' division of 'for its essence or from a cause' is erroneous because it assumes that the negation of cause can be characterized as 'for its essence' or 'from a cause', but this is a misapplication of language.

ذات
نفي العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity of existence is a positive attribute that can be divided into essence-dependent or cause-dependent.
Correction: Ghazali argues that 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous; if it means negation of cause, it cannot be divided in that way.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the main flaw in the philosophers' proof for God's oneness?
A) They assume two necessary existents are possible.
B) They misuse the term 'necessity of existence' by dividing it into essence and cause.
C) They rely on an analogy with humanity which is invalid.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' proof for God's oneness by exposing the ambiguity of the term 'necessity of existence' and invalidating their division and analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you certain that your belief in God's oneness is based on clear divine revelation, or have you unknowingly relied on flawed philosophical reasoning? Examine the foundations of your tawhid today.
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems logical, do you first question the meaning of its key terms? The philosophers' ambiguity led to error—where in your own understanding might subtle ambiguities be hiding?
?
If a famous proof for God's oneness can be dismantled by a simple linguistic clarification, how much of what you think you know about Allah is truly secure? Will you seek knowledge that cuts through confusion?

The Philosophers' First Argument for Tawhid
The philosophers present their first argument for tawhid using a logical division and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first argument for the oneness of God, which posits that if there were two necessary beings, they would share a type, leading to a contradiction.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that a necessary being must be unique because shared necessity implies causation.

The philosophers claim that God's oneness can be proven by considering the implications of two necessary beings. They argue that if two existed, they would share the type 'necessary being', which would require each to either be necessary by itself (making the other unnecessary) or by a cause (making it contingent). This leads to the conclusion that there can only be one necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Carefully read the passage presenting the argument. Identify the key division: necessity of existence is either by itself or by a cause.
Notice the precise logical structure.
2
Understand the analogy with humanity
The philosophers use an analogy: Zayd and 'Amr are human by a cause, not by themselves. Grasp how this analogy supports their conclusion.
Humanity multiplies by matter, similar to how necessity would multiply.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that two necessary beings cannot coexist. Think about whether the division seems valid at first glance.
This sets up Ghazali's upcoming critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٥]
في بيان عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل ، على أن الله واحد وأنه لا يجوز فرض اثنين ، واجبي الوجود ، كل واحد منهما لاعلة له
واستدلالهم على هذا بمسلكين :
المسلك الأول : قولهم : إنهما لو كانا اثنين ، لكان نوع وجوب الوجود مقولا على كل واحد منهما ، وما قيل عليه : إنه واجب الوجود فلا يخلو : أن يكون وجوب وجوده لذاته ، أو لعلة .
فإن كان لذاته ، فلا يكون إلا له ، وإن كان لعلة فتكون ذات واجب الوجود معلولا ، وقد اقتضت علة له وجوب الوجود ، ونحن لا نريد بواجب الوجود ، إلا ما لا ارتباط لوجوده بعلة ، بجهة من الجهات ، وزعموا : أن نوع الإنسان مقول على زيد وعلى عمرو ؛ وليس زيد إنساناً لذاته ، إذ لو كان إنساناً لذاته ، لما كان عمرو إنساناً ؛ بل لعلة جعلته إنساناً ، وقد جعلت عمراً أيضاً إنساناً ، فتكثرت الإنسانية بتكثر المادة الحاملة لها ، وتعلقها بالمادة معلول ليس لذات الإنسانية ؛ فكذلك ثبوت وجوب الوجود لواجب الوجود ، إن كان لذاته ، فلا يكون إلا له ، وإن كان لعلة ، فهو إذن معلول ، وليس بواجب الوجود، فقد ظهر بهذا أن واجب الوجود لا بد أن يكون واحداً .
قلنا : قولكم : نوع وجوب الوجود لواجب الوجود لذاته أو لعلة ، تقسيم خطأ فى وضعه ، فإنا قد بينا أن لفظ وجوب الوجود فيه إجمال ، إلا أن يراد به نفى العلة ، فنستعمل هذه العبارة فنقول : لم يستحيل ثبوت موجودين لا علة لهما ، وليس أحدهما علة للآخر ؟! فقولكم : إن الذي لا علة له ؛ لا علة له لذاته أو لسبب ، تقسيم خطأ ؛ لأن نفى العلة واستغناء الوجود عن العلة لا يطلب له علة فأى معنى لقول القائل : إن ما لا علة له ، لا علة له إما لذاته أو لعلة ، إذ قولنا : لا علة له ، سلب محض ، والسلب المحض، لا يكون له علة ولا سبب، ولا يقال فيه : إنه لذاته أو لا لذاته
وإن عنيتم بوجوب الوجود ، وصفاً ثابتاً لواجب الوجود ، سوى لا علة لوجوده ، فهو غير مفهوم في نفسه ، والذي ينسبك (١) من نفى العلة لوجوده ، وهو سلب محض ، لا يقال فيه : إنه لذاته أو لعلة ؟ ! حتى يبنى على وضع هذا التقسيم غرض . فدل أن هذا برهان من خرف (٢) لا أصل له . بل نقول : إن معنى قولكم : إنه واجب الوجود ، أنه لا علة لوجوده ، ولا علة لكونه بلا علة ، وليس كونه بلا علة معللا أيضاً بذاته ، بل لا علة لوجوده ، ولا علة لكونه بلا علة أصلا .
كيف ؟ ! وهذا التقسيم لا يتطرق إلى بعض صفات الإثبات ، فضلا عما يرجع إلى السلب ، إذ لو قال قائل : السواد لون لذاته أو لعلة ؟ ! فإن كان لذاته ، لزم أن لا تكون الحمرة لوناً ، وأن لا يكون هذا النوع - أعنى اللونية . إلا لذات السواد ، وإن كان السواد لوناً لعلة ، جعلته لوناً ، فينبغي أن يعقل سواد ليس بلون ، أى لم تجعله العلة لوناً ، فإن ما يثبت للذات ، زائداً على الذات لعلة ، يمكن تقدير عدمه فى الوهم ، وإن لم يتحقق في الوجود ، ولكن يقال : هذا التقسيم خطأ فى الوضع ، فلا يقال للسواد : إنه لون لذاته ، قولا يمنع أن يكون ذلك لغير ذاته ، فكذلك لا يقال : إن هذا الموجود واجب لذاته ، أى لا علة له لذاته ، قولا يمنع أن يكون ذلك لغير ذاته بحال
Issue [5]: On explaining their inability to establish proof that God is one and that it is not permissible to suppose two necessary beings, each without a cause. Their demonstration of this is via two paths: The first path: Their saying: If there were two, then the type 'necessary being' would be predicated of each. And that of which it is said 'it is a necessary being' is not free: either its necessity of existence is by itself or by a cause. If it is by itself, then it applies only to itself; if by a cause, then the essence of the necessary being would be an effect, and the cause would have necessitated existence for it. And we only mean by 'necessary being' that which has no connection of its existence to a cause in any respect. And they claimed that the type 'human' is predicated of Zayd and 'Amr; and Zayd is not human by himself, for if he were human by himself, then 'Amr would not be human; rather, it is by a cause that made him human, and it also made 'Amr human, so humanity multiplies by the multiplicity of the matter that bears it, and its attachment to matter is an effect not due to the essence of humanity. Similarly, the affirmation of necessity of existence for a necessary being: if it is by itself, then it applies only to itself; if by a cause, then it is an effect and not a necessary being. Thus it has become clear that the necessary being must be one. We say: Your statement: The type 'necessary being' for the necessary being is either by itself or by a cause — a division erroneous in its formulation. For we have explained that the expression 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous, unless what is meant by it is the negation of a cause. Then we use this phrase and say: Why is it impossible that there be two existents with no cause, neither being a cause for the other? So your statement: That which has no cause — it has no cause either by itself or by a cause — is an erroneous division. Because the negation of a cause and the independence of existence from a cause does not require a cause. So what meaning is there in someone's saying: That which has no cause — it has no cause either by itself or by a cause? Since our saying 'it has no cause' is pure negation, and pure negation has no cause or reason, nor is it said of it 'by itself' or 'not by itself'. And if you mean by 'necessity of existence' an attribute subsisting in the necessary being, other than 'having no cause for its existence', then it is unintelligible in itself. That which is derived from the negation of a cause for its existence, which is pure negation, cannot be said of it 'by itself' or 'by a cause' — such that a purpose could be built upon the placement of this division. So it indicates that this proof is from senility, having no basis. Rather, we say: The meaning of your saying that He is a necessary being is that there is no cause for His existence, and there is no cause for His being without cause. His being without cause is not also caused by Himself. Rather, there is no cause for His existence and no cause for His being without cause at all. How? This division does not even apply to some positive attributes, let alone what pertains to negation. For if someone said: Blackness is a color by itself or by a cause? If by itself, then it would be necessary that redness not be a color, and that this type — i.e., colorness — belong only to the essence of blackness. And if blackness is a color by a cause that made it a color, then it should be conceivable that there be a blackness that is not a color, i.e., the cause did not make it a color. For what is established for an essence, additional to the essence, by a cause, can be supposed absent in the mind, even if it does not actually occur. But it is said: This division is erroneous in its formulation, so it is not said of blackness that it is a color by itself — a statement that prevents that being for other than itself. Similarly, it is not said of this existent that it is necessary by itself, i.e., has no cause by itself — a statement that prevents that being for other than itself absolutely.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first argument for the oneness of God (tawhid) and Ghazali's comprehensive refutation. The passage begins by stating the issue: the philosophers claim that God is one and that it is impossible to suppose two necessary beings (wajib al-wujud). They have two arguments (maslakayn). Ghazali first presents their first argument: If there were two necessary beings, then the type 'necessary being' would be predicated of each. For each individual, its necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) is either by itself (li dhatihi) or by a cause (li 'illa). If by itself, then it would be unique to that essence, so the other would not be necessary. If by a cause, then it is caused (ma'lul) and thus not truly necessary. The philosophers illustrate with the analogy of humanity: Zayd is human by a cause, not by himself, otherwise 'Amr would not be human; humanity multiplies by matter. Similarly, necessary existence must be one. Ghazali then refutes this. He points out that the division is erroneous in its formulation (taqsim khata' fi wad'ihi). The term 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous (mujmal). If it means 'negation of a cause' (nafy al-'illa), then it is pure negation (salb mahd). Pure negation does not admit of a cause, so asking whether it is by itself or by a cause is nonsensical. Ghazali insists that the philosophers cannot legitimately pose this dichotomy. He further argues that even if they mean a positive attribute by 'necessity of existence', it is unintelligible. He then draws an analogy with the attribute 'color' for blackness: the division into 'by itself' or 'by cause' does not apply properly to attributes like color either, and similarly it cannot apply to the negation of cause. Therefore, the entire argument is baseless. Ghazali emphasizes that the correct meaning of 'wajib al-wujud' is simply that there is no cause for its existence, and there is no cause for that negation itself; the negation is not even caused by the essence. This refutation shows a deep understanding of logical categories and the nature of negation.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
لذاته (li dhatihi)
لعلة (li 'illa)
نوع (type)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' main conclusion in this argument?
A) There can be two necessary beings if they are different types.
B) The necessary being must be one because shared necessity leads to contradiction.
C) Necessary beings are contingent on a cause.
D) Humanity and necessary being are analogous in all respects.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that two necessary beings are impossible because shared necessity would require causation or uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that there is only one necessary being who needs no cause, would you still fear anything else? Identify one fear you hold that contradicts this belief, and commit to addressing it tonight.
?
The philosophers said that if necessity were by itself, it applies only to itself. How does this challenge your tendency to attribute ultimate value to anything other than God? Write down one thing you treat as necessary for your happiness and resolve to reorder your heart.
?
When you ask others for help, do you sometimes feel they are independent sources? The argument shows that only the necessary being is truly independent. Contemplate a moment today when you will consciously rely solely on God.

Ghazali's Refutation: The Flawed Division in the Philosophers' Argument
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument by showing the division is misapplied to a negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique that the philosophers' division into 'by itself or by a cause' is misapplied to the negation of a cause implied by 'necessary being'.

Core Idea
Pure negation cannot be divided into by-itself or by-cause, exposing the argument as baseless.

Ghazali systematically dismantles the philosophers' first argument by focusing on the meaning of 'necessary being'. He argues that if it means 'negation of cause', then it is pure negation, which does not admit of the division into 'by itself' or 'by a cause'. He further illustrates with an analogy of color to show the division's inapplicability to negations.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's opening critique
Start with Ghazali's claim that the division is erroneous in its formulation. Pay attention to his wording.
He uses the phrase 'taqsim khata' fi wad'ihi'.
2
Understand the pure negation argument
Ghazali says 'necessary being' means negation of a cause, which is pure negation. Pure negation has no cause, so the division is meaningless.
This is the core of his refutation.
3
Analyze the color analogy
Ghazali uses an analogy: the attribute 'color' for blackness cannot be divided into by-itself or by-cause without absurdity. See how this reinforces his point.
The analogy shows the division is invalid even for positive attributes.

Source Text

مسألة [٥]
في بيان عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل ، على أن الله واحد وأنه لا يجوز فرض اثنين ، واجبي الوجود ، كل واحد منهما لاعلة له
واستدلالهم على هذا بمسلكين :
المسلك الأول : قولهم : إنهما لو كانا اثنين ، لكان نوع وجوب الوجود مقولا على كل واحد منهما ، وما قيل عليه : إنه واجب الوجود فلا يخلو : أن يكون وجوب وجوده لذاته ، أو لعلة .
فإن كان لذاته ، فلا يكون إلا له ، وإن كان لعلة فتكون ذات واجب الوجود معلولا ، وقد اقتضت علة له وجوب الوجود ، ونحن لا نريد بواجب الوجود ، إلا ما لا ارتباط لوجوده بعلة ، بجهة من الجهات ، وزعموا : أن نوع الإنسان مقول على زيد وعلى عمرو ؛ وليس زيد إنساناً لذاته ، إذ لو كان إنساناً لذاته ، لما كان عمرو إنساناً ؛ بل لعلة جعلته إنساناً ، وقد جعلت عمراً أيضاً إنساناً ، فتكثرت الإنسانية بتكثر المادة الحاملة لها ، وتعلقها بالمادة معلول ليس لذات الإنسانية ؛ فكذلك ثبوت وجوب الوجود لواجب الوجود ، إن كان لذاته ، فلا يكون إلا له ، وإن كان لعلة ، فهو إذن معلول ، وليس بواجب الوجود، فقد ظهر بهذا أن واجب الوجود لا بد أن يكون واحداً .
قلنا : قولكم : نوع وجوب الوجود لواجب الوجود لذاته أو لعلة ، تقسيم خطأ فى وضعه ، فإنا قد بينا أن لفظ وجوب الوجود فيه إجمال ، إلا أن يراد به نفى العلة ، فنستعمل هذه العبارة فنقول : لم يستحيل ثبوت موجودين لا علة لهما ، وليس أحدهما علة للآخر ؟! فقولكم : إن الذي لا علة له ؛ لا علة له لذاته أو لسبب ، تقسيم خطأ ؛ لأن نفى العلة واستغناء الوجود عن العلة لا يطلب له علة فأى معنى لقول القائل : إن ما لا علة له ، لا علة له إما لذاته أو لعلة ، إذ قولنا : لا علة له ، سلب محض ، والسلب المحض، لا يكون له علة ولا سبب، ولا يقال فيه : إنه لذاته أو لا لذاته
وإن عنيتم بوجوب الوجود ، وصفاً ثابتاً لواجب الوجود ، سوى لا علة لوجوده ، فهو غير مفهوم في نفسه ، والذي ينسبك (١) من نفى العلة لوجوده ، وهو سلب محض ، لا يقال فيه : إنه لذاته أو لعلة ؟ ! حتى يبنى على وضع هذا التقسيم غرض . فدل أن هذا برهان من خرف (٢) لا أصل له . بل نقول : إن معنى قولكم : إنه واجب الوجود ، أنه لا علة لوجوده ، ولا علة لكونه بلا علة ، وليس كونه بلا علة معللا أيضاً بذاته ، بل لا علة لوجوده ، ولا علة لكونه بلا علة أصلا .
كيف ؟ ! وهذا التقسيم لا يتطرق إلى بعض صفات الإثبات ، فضلا عما يرجع إلى السلب ، إذ لو قال قائل : السواد لون لذاته أو لعلة ؟ ! فإن كان لذاته ، لزم أن لا تكون الحمرة لوناً ، وأن لا يكون هذا النوع - أعنى اللونية . إلا لذات السواد ، وإن كان السواد لوناً لعلة ، جعلته لوناً ، فينبغي أن يعقل سواد ليس بلون ، أى لم تجعله العلة لوناً ، فإن ما يثبت للذات ، زائداً على الذات لعلة ، يمكن تقدير عدمه فى الوهم ، وإن لم يتحقق في الوجود ، ولكن يقال : هذا التقسيم خطأ فى الوضع ، فلا يقال للسواد : إنه لون لذاته ، قولا يمنع أن يكون ذلك لغير ذاته ، فكذلك لا يقال : إن هذا الموجود واجب لذاته ، أى لا علة له لذاته ، قولا يمنع أن يكون ذلك لغير ذاته بحال
Issue [5]: On explaining their inability to establish proof that God is one and that it is not permissible to suppose two necessary beings, each without a cause. Their demonstration of this is via two paths: The first path: Their saying: If there were two, then the type 'necessary being' would be predicated of each. And that of which it is said 'it is a necessary being' is not free: either its necessity of existence is by itself or by a cause. If it is by itself, then it applies only to itself; if by a cause, then the essence of the necessary being would be an effect, and the cause would have necessitated existence for it. And we only mean by 'necessary being' that which has no connection of its existence to a cause in any respect. And they claimed that the type 'human' is predicated of Zayd and 'Amr; and Zayd is not human by himself, for if he were human by himself, then 'Amr would not be human; rather, it is by a cause that made him human, and it also made 'Amr human, so humanity multiplies by the multiplicity of the matter that bears it, and its attachment to matter is an effect not due to the essence of humanity. Similarly, the affirmation of necessity of existence for a necessary being: if it is by itself, then it applies only to itself; if by a cause, then it is an effect and not a necessary being. Thus it has become clear that the necessary being must be one. We say: Your statement: The type 'necessary being' for the necessary being is either by itself or by a cause — a division erroneous in its formulation. For we have explained that the expression 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous, unless what is meant by it is the negation of a cause. Then we use this phrase and say: Why is it impossible that there be two existents with no cause, neither being a cause for the other? So your statement: That which has no cause — it has no cause either by itself or by a cause — is an erroneous division. Because the negation of a cause and the independence of existence from a cause does not require a cause. So what meaning is there in someone's saying: That which has no cause — it has no cause either by itself or by a cause? Since our saying 'it has no cause' is pure negation, and pure negation has no cause or reason, nor is it said of it 'by itself' or 'not by itself'. And if you mean by 'necessity of existence' an attribute subsisting in the necessary being, other than 'having no cause for its existence', then it is unintelligible in itself. That which is derived from the negation of a cause for its existence, which is pure negation, cannot be said of it 'by itself' or 'by a cause' — such that a purpose could be built upon the placement of this division. So it indicates that this proof is from senility, having no basis. Rather, we say: The meaning of your saying that He is a necessary being is that there is no cause for His existence, and there is no cause for His being without cause. His being without cause is not also caused by Himself. Rather, there is no cause for His existence and no cause for His being without cause at all. How? This division does not even apply to some positive attributes, let alone what pertains to negation. For if someone said: Blackness is a color by itself or by a cause? If by itself, then it would be necessary that redness not be a color, and that this type — i.e., colorness — belong only to the essence of blackness. And if blackness is a color by a cause that made it a color, then it should be conceivable that there be a blackness that is not a color, i.e., the cause did not make it a color. For what is established for an essence, additional to the essence, by a cause, can be supposed absent in the mind, even if it does not actually occur. But it is said: This division is erroneous in its formulation, so it is not said of blackness that it is a color by itself — a statement that prevents that being for other than itself. Similarly, it is not said of this existent that it is necessary by itself, i.e., has no cause by itself — a statement that prevents that being for other than itself absolutely.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first argument for the oneness of God (tawhid) and Ghazali's comprehensive refutation. The passage begins by stating the issue: the philosophers claim that God is one and that it is impossible to suppose two necessary beings (wajib al-wujud). They have two arguments (maslakayn). Ghazali first presents their first argument: If there were two necessary beings, then the type 'necessary being' would be predicated of each. For each individual, its necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) is either by itself (li dhatihi) or by a cause (li 'illa). If by itself, then it would be unique to that essence, so the other would not be necessary. If by a cause, then it is caused (ma'lul) and thus not truly necessary. The philosophers illustrate with the analogy of humanity: Zayd is human by a cause, not by himself, otherwise 'Amr would not be human; humanity multiplies by matter. Similarly, necessary existence must be one. Ghazali then refutes this. He points out that the division is erroneous in its formulation (taqsim khata' fi wad'ihi). The term 'necessity of existence' is ambiguous (mujmal). If it means 'negation of a cause' (nafy al-'illa), then it is pure negation (salb mahd). Pure negation does not admit of a cause, so asking whether it is by itself or by a cause is nonsensical. Ghazali insists that the philosophers cannot legitimately pose this dichotomy. He further argues that even if they mean a positive attribute by 'necessity of existence', it is unintelligible. He then draws an analogy with the attribute 'color' for blackness: the division into 'by itself' or 'by cause' does not apply properly to attributes like color either, and similarly it cannot apply to the negation of cause. Therefore, the entire argument is baseless. Ghazali emphasizes that the correct meaning of 'wajib al-wujud' is simply that there is no cause for its existence, and there is no cause for that negation itself; the negation is not even caused by the essence. This refutation shows a deep understanding of logical categories and the nature of negation.

Key Terms
تقسيم خطأ (erroneous division)
سلب محض (pure negation)
نفى العلة (negation of cause)
لذاته (by itself)
لعلة (by a cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Ghazali rejects the logical division itself.
Correction: Ghazali rejects its application to a negation, not the division per se. He clarifies that pure negation cannot be divided into 'by itself' or 'by a cause'.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say the philosophers' division is erroneous?
A) Because 'necessary being' is a positive attribute that cannot be divided.
B) Because 'necessary being' means negation of a cause, which is pure negation and has no cause.
C) Because the analogy with humanity is flawed.
D) Because two necessary beings can exist without contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' division fails because it is applied to a pure negation, which does not admit of such division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson reveals that applying human categories of 'by itself' or 'by a cause' to Allah can lead to error. In your own heart, where might you be dividing Allah's attributes in ways that limit His transcendence? How can you actively guard against that?
?
Ghazali insisted that pure negation requires no cause. Reflect: Are there aspects of your reliance on Allah that you mistakenly think must be 'caused' by your own actions or merits? How does recognizing Allah's absolute independence change the way you depend on Him today?
