Existence without Essence is Inconceivable
Ghazali asserts the inconceivability of existence without essence and provides supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that existence without a quiddity or reality is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Existence without essence is a contradiction: there can be no existence without a reality.

Ghazali presents his second approach against the philosophers who claim that the Necessary Existent has no quiddity beyond existence. He argues that such a notion is incoherent. In this lesson, we examine his core reasoning that absolute existence cannot be conceived without a specific reality.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's argument
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali begins with a strong assertion.
2
Understand the analogy with non-existence
Ghazali parallels absolute non-existence and absolute existence. Why does he start with non-existence?
Both are only conceivable in relation to something.
3
Identify the contradiction
He concludes that denying essence leads to 'existence with no existent'. Why is that contradictory?
Existence without an existent is nonsense.
4
Apply to contingent beings
Ghazali argues that if such existence were conceivable, it could be said of contingents. Why does this show impossibility?
The absurdity of contingent existence without essence reinforces the argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
He says the philosophers end in pure negation. What does this imply about their concept of God?
Their transcendence reduces to emptiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثانى : هو أن نقول : وجود بلا ماهية ولا حقيقة غير معقول ، وكما لا نعقل عدماً مرسلاً ، إلا بالإضافة إلى موجود يقدر عدمه ، فلا نعقل وجوداً مرسلاً ، إلا بالإضافة إلى حقيقة معينة، لا سما إذا تعين ذاتاً واحدة ، فكيف يتعين واحداً متميزاً عن غيره بالمعنى ، ولا حقيقة له ؟ ! فإن نفى الماهية نفى للحقيقة ، وإذا انتفت حقيقة الموجود ، لم يعقل الوجود ؛ فكأنهم قالوا : وجود لا موجود ، وهو متناقض . ويدل عليه أنه لو كان هذا معقولا ، لجاز أن يكون في المعلولات وجود لا حقيقة له ، يشارك الأول فى كونه وجوداً لا حقيقة له ولا ماهية له ، ويباينه في أن له علة ، والأول لا علة له ، فلم لا يتصور هذا في المعلولات ؟! وهل له سبب إلا أنه غير معقول في نفسه ؟! وما لا يعقل في نفسه ، فبأن تنفى علته لا يصير معقولا ! وما يعقل ، فبأن تقدر له علة لا يخرج عن كونه معقولا والتناهى إلى هذا الحد في المعقولات ، غاية ظلماتهم ، فقد ظنوا أنهم يتنزهون فيما يقولون ، فانتهى كلامهم إلى النفى المجرد ، فإن نفى الماهية نفي للحقيقة ، ولا يبقى مع نفى الحقيقة ، إلا لفظ الوجود ، ولا مسمى له أصلا ، إذا لم يضف إلى ماهية فإن قيل : حقيقته أنه واجب الوجود ، وهو الماهية . قلنا : ولا معنى للواجب إلا نفى العلة ، وهو سلب لا تتقوم به حقيقة حتى ذات ، ونفى العلة عن الحقيقة لازم للحقيقة ، فلتكن الحقيقة معقولة ، توصف : بأنها لا علة لها ، ولا يتصور عدمها ؛ إذ لا معنى للوجوب إلا هذا على أن الوجوب إن زاد على الوجود ، فقد جاءت الكثرة ؛ وإن لم يزد ، فكيف يكون هو الماهية ؟ ! والوجود ليس بماهية ، فكذا ما لا يزيد عليه .
The second approach: it is that we say: existence without quiddity and without reality is inconceivable. Just as we do not conceive absolute non-existence except in relation to an existent whose non-existence is supposed, so we do not conceive absolute existence except in relation to a specific reality, especially when it is determined as a single essence. How then can it be determined as one, distinct from others in meaning, while having no reality? For the negation of quiddity is the negation of reality, and when the reality of the existent is negated, existence is not understood; it is as if they said: existence with no existent, which is contradictory. And it is indicated by the fact that if this were conceivable, then it would be possible for there to be in contingent things an existence with no reality, sharing with the First in being an existence with no reality and no quiddity, but differing from it in having a cause, while the First has no cause. So why is this not conceivable in contingent things? Is there any reason for it except that it is inconceivable in itself? And what is inconceivable in itself does not become conceivable by negating its cause! And what is conceivable does not cease being conceivable by positing a cause for it. Their reaching this extreme in intelligibles is the utmost of their darkness, for they thought they were being transcendent in what they say, but their discourse ended in pure negation. For the negation of quiddity is the negation of reality, and with the negation of reality nothing remains except the word 'existence', which has no referent at all if not attached to a quiddity. If it is said: its reality is that it is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), and that is the quiddity. We say: 'wājib' means nothing but the negation of a cause, which is a negation that does not constitute a reality, even a self. And the negation of a cause is a concomitant of a reality, so let the reality be conceivable, described as having no cause and its non-existence inconceivable; for the meaning of necessity (wujūb) is only this. Besides, if necessity is additional to existence, then multiplicity arises; and if it is not additional, then how can it be the quiddity? Existence is not a quiddity, and similarly that which does not add to it.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second approach against the philosophers' conception of the Necessary Existent. It begins by stating that existence without a quiddity or reality is inconceivable. Just as absolute non-existence is only understood in relation to a supposed existent, absolute existence is only understood in relation to a specific reality. If the Necessary Existent is determined as a single self distinct from others, it must have a reality; denying that leads to contradiction. Ghazali then provides a further proof: if such existence were conceivable, it could be posited in contingent beings, but it is not, showing its inconceivability. He then addresses the objection that the Necessary Existent's quiddity is its being necessary of existence. Ghazali refutes this by analyzing the term 'necessary' (wājib) as merely the negation of a cause, which is a negative attribute that does not constitute a positive reality. He then examines whether necessity adds to existence or not: if it adds, multiplicity arises; if not, it cannot serve as a quiddity, since existence itself is not a quiddity.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyya)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa)
وجود (wujūd)
علة (ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence can be conceived without any specific essence or reality.
Correction: Ghazali argues that existence without essence is contradictory; every existent must have a reality.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about existence without quiddity?
A) It is conceivable for God alone.
B) It is inconceivable and contradictory.
C) It is possible for contingent beings.
D) It is the philosophers' definition of existence.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be divorced from essence; every existent must have a reality, otherwise existence becomes an empty word.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah exists, do you ever reduce Him to a mere concept of 'existence' without truly recognizing His specific reality as described in the Quran and Sunnah? How does this passage challenge any tendency to think of Allah as just an abstract 'being'?
?
The passage says the philosophers' discourse ended in pure negation. Have you ever, in your own theological reflections, inadvertently emptied Allah of His attributes, thinking that affirming His essence alone is sufficient? What attribute do you need to re-embrace today to ensure your faith is substantive?
?
Reflect: are there areas in your life where you try to be 'intellectually sophisticated' about faith but end up losing the substance? What is one concrete step you can take this week to ensure your belief in Allah is not just a word but a reality with content?

The Meaning of 'Wajib' and Why It Cannot Be the Quiddity
Ghazali refutes the objection that wajib al-wujud is the quiddity of the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the claim that the Necessity of Existence (wujūb) is the quiddity of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Necessity (wujūb) is merely the negation of a cause, not a positive quiddity.

The philosophers might object that the quiddity of the Necessary Existent is its being necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd). Ghazali refutes this by analyzing the term 'wājib' as a negation. In this lesson, we examine his logical dissection and the dilemma regarding whether necessity adds to existence or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the objection and Ghazali's immediate response.
2
Analyze the meaning of 'wājib'
Ghazali says 'wājib' means nothing but the negation of a cause. Why is this a negation, not a positive attribute?
Negation does not constitute a reality.
3
Understand the dilemma
If necessity (wujūb) adds to existence, then multiplicity arises in God. If it does not add, how can it be the quiddity?
Existence itself is not a quiddity, so a non-additional attribute cannot be one.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that 'wājib' cannot serve as the quiddity. What does this imply about the philosophers' concept?
They fail to provide a positive essence for God.

The second approach: it is that we say: existence without quiddity and without reality is inconceivable. Just as we do not conceive absolute non-existence except in relation to an existent whose non-existence is supposed, so we do not conceive absolute existence except in relation to a specific reality, especially when it is determined as a single essence. How then can it be determined as one, distinct from others in meaning, while having no reality? The Philosophers' Inconsistency: Eternal Bodies
Ghazali argues against the philosophers' proof that God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers cannot use the contingency argument to prove God is not a body, and how he refutes their composition-based objection.

Core Idea
If philosophers admit eternal bodies, they cannot disprove a bodily First.

Ghazali here turns the philosophers' own assumptions against them. They claim the First cannot be a body because bodies are composite and therefore need a cause. But they themselves believe in eternal bodies like the celestial spheres. Ghazali seizes this inconsistency and also undermines the claim that composition necessarily implies a composer.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ghazali's initial challenge
Read the first few lines: Ghazali says the argument from contingency only works for those who believe bodies are originated. The philosophers accept eternal bodies, so they cannot use it.
Notice Ghazali directly names the sun and the outermost sphere as examples.
2
Understand the philosophers' objection
The philosophers object that a body is composite and divisible in several ways, and the Necessary Existence is one and indivisible.
3
Grasp Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali says they have already refuted this: composition does not prove a composer, just as one can conceive an existent without a cause.
This is a key logical move: parity of reasoning.
4
Reflect on the deeper critique
Ghazali points out that the philosophers' entire edifice rests on unproven assumptions about quiddity and existence.
This ties into earlier discussions in the Tahafut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [۹] في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أن الأول ليس بجسم . فنقول : هذا إنما يستقيم لمن يرى أن الجسم حادث، من حيث إنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، وكل حادث فيفتقر إلى محدث . فأما أنتم إذا عقلتم جسم قديماً ، لا أول لوجوده ، مع الحوادث ، فلم يمتنع أن يكون الأول جسماً ؟ ! إما الشمس ، وإما الفلك الأقصى ، وإما غيره . فإن قيل : لأن الجسم لا يكون إلا مركباً منقسماً إلى جزأين ، بالكمية وإلى الهيولى والصورة بالقسمة المعنوية ، وإلى أوصاف يختص بها لا محالة ، حتى يباين سائر الأجسام ، وإلا فالأجسام متساوية في أنها أجسام ، وواجب الوجود واحد ، لا يقبل القسمة بهذه الوجوه كلها . قلنا : وقد أبطلنا هذا عليكم ، وبينا أنه لا دليل لكم عليه ، سوى المجتمع إذا افتقر بعض أجزائه إلى البعض ، كان معلولا ، وقد تكلمنا عليه، وبينا أنه إذا لم يبعد تقدير موجود لا موجد له ، لم يبعد تقدير مركب لا مركب له ، وتقدير موجودات لا موجد لها ، إذ نفى العدد والتثنية ، بنيتموه على نفى التركيب . ونفى التركيب ، على نفى الماهية سوى الوجود ، وما هو الأساس الأخير فقد استأصلناه ، وبينا تحكمكم فيه . فإن قيل : الجسم إن لم تكن له نفس ، فلا يكون فاعلا ، وإن كان له نفس ، فنفسه علة له ، فلا يكون الجسم أولا . قلنا : نفسنا ليست علة لوجود جسمنا ، ولا نفس الفلك بمجردها علة.
Problem [9]: On disabling them from establishing the proof that the First is not a body. We say: This only holds for one who considers that the body is originated, by virtue of its not being free of accidents, and every originated thing is in need of an originator. As for you, if you conceive an eternal body with no beginning to its existence, despite accidents, then what prevents the First from being a body? Either the sun, or the outermost sphere, or something else. If it is said: Because a body is only composite, divisible into two parts quantitatively, and into hyle and form by conceptual division, and into properties that necessarily distinguish it from other bodies, otherwise bodies are equal in being bodies, and the Necessary Existence is one, not admitting division in all these respects. We say: We have already refuted this against you, and shown that you have no proof for it except that a composite, when some of its parts need others, is an effect. And we have discussed it and shown that if it is not improbable to posit an existent without a cause, it is not improbable to posit a composite without a composer, and to posit existents without a cause, for you have based the negation of number and duality on the negation of composition, and the negation of composition on the negation of quiddity besides existence, and as for that ultimate foundation, we have uprooted it and shown your arbitrariness in it. If it is said: The body, if it does not have a soul, then it cannot be an agent; and if it has a soul, then its soul is a cause for it, so the body cannot be the First. We say: Our soul is not a cause for the existence of our body, nor is the soul of the sphere by itself a cause.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is the ninth problem (mas'ala) of Ghazali's Tahafut, where he argues that the philosophers are unable to prove that the First (God) is not a body. Ghazali says that the method of proving the body's originatedness (huduth) from its not being free of accidents only works for those who hold that view. But the philosophers themselves admit eternal bodies like the celestial spheres, so they cannot use that argument. If they object that a body is composite and divisible (by quantity, hyle/form, and distinguishing properties), and the Necessary Existence is one and indivisible, Ghazali replies that they have already refuted this reasoning. Their claim that composition implies being an effect (ma'lul) is based on the premise that a composite whose parts are interdependent must have a cause. But Ghazali counters: just as one can conceive a self-existent entity without a cause, one can conceive a self-composite entity without a composer. Moreover, the philosophers' entire edifice rests on negating composition, then negating quiddity besides existence—both of which Ghazali claims to have already undermined. Finally, if they object that a body without a soul cannot act, and if it has a soul, the soul is a cause for it, so the body cannot be the First, Ghazali dismisses this by noting that our soul is not a cause for our body's existence, nor is the soul of the sphere by itself a cause. Thus the philosophers fail to prove God is not a body.

جسم
مركب
قسمة
هيولى
صورة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is composite, it must have a composer.
Correction: Ghazali argues that composition does not necessarily imply a composer; it is conceivable to have a self-sufficient composite.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main objection to the philosophers' argument that the First cannot be a body?
A) Bodies cannot exist without accidents.
B) The philosophers themselves accept eternal bodies like the spheres, so their argument fails.
C) The soul is the cause of the body.
D) Bodies are always originated.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali exposes the philosophers' inconsistency in admitting eternal bodies while denying a bodily First, and refutes the claim that composition implies a composer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the philosophers' willingness to accept eternal bodies but not a bodily First, do you find yourself making similar convenient exceptions in your own reasoning about God? How can you ensure consistency in your beliefs?
?
Ghazali points out that if you can conceive an existent without a cause, you can conceive a composite without a composer. Are you willing to accept the logical consequences of your own premises, even when they challenge your comfort or long-held assumptions?
?
This passage shows how arguments can be built on foundations that are arbitrary. Where in your own understanding of God do you rely on assumptions that you have not examined? What would it cost you to question them?

The Soul Objection and Ghazali's Response
Ghazali refutes the soul-based objection against a bodily First.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the philosophers' argument that a body without a soul cannot act, and that if it has a soul, the soul would be a cause, making it not the First.

Core Idea
The soul is not the cause of the body's existence, so the philosophers' objection fails.

The philosophers try another approach: if God is a body, He must either have a soul (and then the soul causes the body) or not (and then cannot act). Ghazali responds by denying that the soul is a cause of the body's existence, using our own body and soul as an example. This refutation shows that the philosophers' dilemma is not valid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' dilemma
Read the objection: a body without a soul cannot act, but with a soul the soul becomes a cause, so it cannot be the First.
2
See Ghazali's counterexample
Ghazali points out that our own soul is not the cause of our body's existence.
This is a direct analogy.
3
Apply to the celestial sphere
Similarly, the soul of the sphere is not by itself a cause of the sphere's existence.
So the argument collapses.

?
The philosophers assumed a body with a soul must have the soul as its cause. Is there any relationship in your life—a person, a possession, or a status—that you treat as if it is self-caused or caused by your own soul? What would it look like to surrender that false sense of causality to Allah tonight?
?
If your soul does not cause your body, then death is not the soul 'causing' the body to cease, but rather Allah taking both. How does this reframe your fear of death? Are you ready to hand your soul back to its true Owner, not as a cause but as a trust?

The Materialist Challenge to the Philosophers
Ghazali states the problem and outlines the materialist doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that philosophers who deny temporal origination cannot refute the materialist claim that the world has no creator.

Core Idea
Without temporal origination, philosophers cannot prove a creator.

In this opening part of the tenth problem, Ghazali presents a devastating challenge: if the world is eternal, as philosophers claim, then why not accept the materialist view that it has no creator at all? He then elaborates the materialist position in detail, showing how they explain all change through eternal circular motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the problem
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ghazali sets up a dilemma: philosophers who hold bodies eternal cannot logically exclude the materialist denial of a creator.
The phrase 'what prevents you from the doctrine of the materialists?' is the key challenge.
2
Identify the materialist's key claims
The materialist (दe) says the world is eternal, no body ever comes into or goes out of existence; only forms and accidents change. Celestial bodies and the four elements are eternal.
The materialist also says souls arise but their causal chain ends at circular motion.
3
Reflect on why this challenges the philosophers
Ask yourself: If the philosophers also believe the celestial bodies are eternal, how can they argue that these bodies need a creator? The materialist offers an alternative explanation using only eternal causes.
This shows that the philosophers' metaphysics contains a logical gap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ ۱۰ ] في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أن للعالم صانعاً وعلة فنقول : إن من ذهب إلى أن كل جسم فهو حادث ، لأنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، عقل مذهبهم فى قولهم : إنه يفتقر إلى صانع وعلة وأما أنتم فما الذى يمنعكم من مذهب الدهرية ؟ ! وهو مذهب الدهرية ؟ ! وهو أن العالم قديم كذلك ولا علة له ، ولا صانع ؛ وإنما العلة للحوادث ، وليس يحدث في العالم جسم ولا ينعدم جسم، وإنما تحدث الصور والأعراض . فإن الأجسام التي هي في السموات ، قديمة ، والعناصر الأربعة التي هي حشو فلك القمر ، وأجسامها وموادها ، قديمة ، وإنما تتبدل عليها الصور بالامتزاجات والاستحالات ، وتحدث النفوس الإنسانية ، والحيوانية ، والنباتية ؛ وهذه الحوادث تنتهى عللها إلى الحركة الدورية ، والحركة الدورية قديمة ، ومصدرها نفس قديمة للفلك ؛ فإذن لا علة للعالم ، ولا صانع لأجسامه ، بل هو كما هو عليه ، لم يزل قديماً كذلك بلا علة – أعنى الأجسام – فما معنى قولهم : إن هذه الأجسام ، وجودها بعلة وهي قديمة ؟ ! فإن قيل : كل ما لا علة له فهو واجب الوجود ، وقد ذكرنا من صفات واجب الوجود ، ما تبين به أن الجسم لا يكون واجب الوجود . قلنا : وقد بينا فساد ما ادعيتموه من صفات واجب الوجود ، وأن البرهان لا يدل إلا على قطع السلسلة ، وقد انقطعت عند الدهرى في أول الأمر ، إذ يقول : لا علة للأجسام ، وأما الصور والأعراض فبعضها علة للبعض ، إلى أن تنتهى إلى الحركة الدورية ، وهى : بعضها سبب للبعض ، كما هو مذهب
Tenth Problem: Regarding their incapacity to establish a proof that the world has a maker and cause. So we say: He who holds that every body is temporally originated, because it is never free from accidents, understands their position in saying that it needs a maker and cause. But as for you, what prevents you from the doctrine of the materialists? That is the doctrine of the materialists? That is that the world is eternal likewise, and has no cause, and no maker; rather the cause is for accidents, and no body comes into existence or ceases to exist in the world; only forms and accidents arise. For the bodies that are in the heavens are eternal, and the four elements that fill the sphere of the moon, their bodies and matters are eternal, and only forms change upon them through mixtures and transformations, and human, animal, and plant souls come to be; and these accidents trace their causes back to circular motion, and circular motion is eternal, and its source is an eternal soul of the sphere; so then there is no cause for the world, and no maker for its bodies, but it is as it is, eternally so without cause—I mean the bodies. So what is the meaning of their saying: These bodies exist by a cause while they are eternal? For if it is said: Everything that has no cause is necessary of existence, and we have mentioned from the attributes of the Necessary Existent that which makes clear that a body cannot be necessary of existence. We say: We have already shown the corruption of what you claim of the attributes of the Necessary Existent, and that demonstration only indicates the cutting of the chain, and it is cut at the materialist from the very beginning, since he says: There is no cause for the bodies. As for the forms and accidents, some are causes of others, until they end at circular motion, and it: some are causes of others, as is the doctrine.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth problem of the Tahafut. Ghazali states that those who accept that every body is temporally originated (as the Ash'ari theologians do) can coherently argue for a creator. But the philosophers, who deny temporal origination, have no argument to refute the materialists (دهرية). Ghazali then elaborates the materialist position: the world is eternal, bodies never come into or go out of existence, only forms and accidents change. The celestial bodies are eternal, the four elements are eternal, and only their forms change through mixture. Souls are generated but their causal chain ends at the eternal circular motion of the spheres, which is caused by an eternal soul. Therefore, there is no need for a creator of the bodies. If the philosophers object that whatever lacks a cause is necessary of existence and they have argued that a body cannot be necessary, Ghazali replies that they have already shown that the philosophers' arguments for the attributes of the Necessary Existent are corrupt, and that demonstration only proves a regress must stop, but the materialist stops the regress at the beginning by simply denying a cause for bodies. The materialist's chain of causes for accidents ends at circular motion, which is also uncaused in their view.

Key Terms
دهرية
صانع
علة
حادث
قديم
حركة دورية
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ghazali is presenting the materialist view as his own position or as an actual possibility he endorses.
Correction: Ghazali is using the materialist position as a reductio ad absurdum against the philosophers. He does not believe the materialist view is true; he is showing that if the philosophers accept the eternity of bodies, they cannot logically refute materialism.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the key reason the philosophers cannot refute the materialists?
A) The materialists have better arguments
B) The philosophers also believe bodies are eternal
C) Ghazali is mistaken
D) The philosophers accept temporal origination

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' denial of temporal origination leaves them defenseless against the materialist claim that the world has no creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that the world is simply 'there' without needing a cause, just as the materialists assert? What in your daily routine reveals whether you truly live as if everything depends on God or as if things run on their own?
?
This passage shows that even a belief in God can be logically inconsistent if you hold the wrong premises. What assumptions do you hold about God or the world that, if examined, might actually undermine your own conviction?
?
If you were challenged to prove that the universe needs a Creator, could you do it without relying on the idea that the world had a beginning? How does your own certainty about God's existence hold up when you are pushed to defend it rationally?

Responding to the Objection of the Necessary Existent
Ghazali replies to the philosophers' objection about the necessary existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' objection that the materialist position violates the principle that everything without a cause is necessary of existence.

Core Idea
The materialist stops the regress before it starts.

After presenting the materialist challenge, Ghazali anticipates an objection from the philosophers: that anything without a cause would be necessary of existence, and they have previously argued that a body cannot be such. Ghazali's reply is incisive: the philosophers' arguments for the necessary existent are already flawed, and the materialist simply denies a cause for bodies at the outset, so the chain is cut before it even begins.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's response
Read the passage slowly. The philosophers object: 'Everything with no cause is necessary of existence, and we have shown a body cannot be necessary.' Ghazali responds by attacking their proof and offering a different analysis.
Notice the phrase 'We have already shown the corruption' – Ghazali refers to his earlier critiques.
2
Understand the philosophical principle
The principle is: whatever exists without a cause is necessary of existence (وجب الوجود). The philosophers use this to argue that the world must have a cause, otherwise it would be necessary, which they think is impossible for a body.
This is a standard argument from contingency.
3
Analyze Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali says their proof of the Necessary Existent is corrupt, and that demonstration only cuts the infinite regress. The materialist cuts the regress at the very first step by denying a cause for bodies. Hence the objection fails.
Ghazali turns the tables: the materialist's position is consistent if one simply stops the regress at bodies.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ ۱۰ ] في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أن للعالم صانعاً وعلة فنقول : إن من ذهب إلى أن كل جسم فهو حادث ، لأنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، عقل مذهبهم فى قولهم : إنه يفتقر إلى صانع وعلة وأما أنتم فما الذى يمنعكم من مذهب الدهرية ؟ ! وهو مذهب الدهرية ؟ ! وهو أن العالم قديم كذلك ولا علة له ، ولا صانع ؛ وإنما العلة للحوادث ، وليس يحدث في العالم جسم ولا ينعدم جسم، وإنما تحدث الصور والأعراض . فإن الأجسام التي هي في السموات ، قديمة ، والعناصر الأربعة التي هي حشو فلك القمر ، وأجسامها وموادها ، قديمة ، وإنما تتبدل عليها الصور بالامتزاجات والاستحالات ، وتحدث النفوس الإنسانية ، والحيوانية ، والنباتية ؛ وهذه الحوادث تنتهى عللها إلى الحركة الدورية ، والحركة الدورية قديمة ، ومصدرها نفس قديمة للفلك ؛ فإذن لا علة للعالم ، ولا صانع لأجسامه ، بل هو كما هو عليه ، لم يزل قديماً كذلك بلا علة – أعنى الأجسام – فما معنى قولهم : إن هذه الأجسام ، وجودها بعلة وهي قديمة ؟ ! فإن قيل : كل ما لا علة له فهو واجب الوجود ، وقد ذكرنا من صفات واجب الوجود ، ما تبين به أن الجسم لا يكون واجب الوجود . قلنا : وقد بينا فساد ما ادعيتموه من صفات واجب الوجود ، وأن البرهان لا يدل إلا على قطع السلسلة ، وقد انقطعت عند الدهرى في أول الأمر ، إذ يقول : لا علة للأجسام ، وأما الصور والأعراض فبعضها علة للبعض ، إلى أن تنتهى إلى الحركة الدورية ، وهى : بعضها سبب للبعض ، كما هو مذهب
Tenth Problem: Regarding their incapacity to establish a proof that the world has a maker and cause. So we say: He who holds that every body is temporally originated, because it is never free from accidents, understands their position in saying that it needs a maker and cause. But as for you, what prevents you from the doctrine of the materialists? That is the doctrine of the materialists? That is that the world is eternal likewise, and has no cause, and no maker; rather the cause is for accidents, and no body comes into existence or ceases to exist in the world; only forms and accidents arise. For the bodies that are in the heavens are eternal, and the four elements that fill the sphere of the moon, their bodies and matters are eternal, and only forms change upon them through mixtures and transformations, and human, animal, and plant souls come to be; and these accidents trace their causes back to circular motion, and circular motion is eternal, and its source is an eternal soul of the sphere; so then there is no cause for the world, and no maker for its bodies, but it is as it is, eternally so without cause—I mean the bodies. So what is the meaning of their saying: These bodies exist by a cause while they are eternal? For if it is said: Everything that has no cause is necessary of existence, and we have mentioned from the attributes of the Necessary Existent that which makes clear that a body cannot be necessary of existence. We say: We have already shown the corruption of what you claim of the attributes of the Necessary Existent, and that demonstration only indicates the cutting of the chain, and it is cut at the materialist from the very beginning, since he says: There is no cause for the bodies. As for the forms and accidents, some are causes of others, until they end at circular motion, and it: some are causes of others, as is the doctrine.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth problem of the Tahafut. Ghazali states that those who accept that every body is temporally originated (as the Ash'ari theologians do) can coherently argue for a creator. But the philosophers, who deny temporal origination, have no argument to refute the materialists (دهرية). Ghazali then elaborates the materialist position: the world is eternal, bodies never come into or go out of existence, only forms and accidents change. The celestial bodies are eternal, the four elements are eternal, and only their forms change through mixture. Souls are generated but their causal chain ends at the eternal circular motion of the spheres, which is caused by an eternal soul. Therefore, there is no need for a creator of the bodies. If the philosophers object that whatever lacks a cause is necessary of existence and they have argued that a body cannot be necessary, Ghazali replies that they have already shown that the philosophers' arguments for the attributes of the Necessary Existent are corrupt, and that demonstration only proves a regress must stop, but the materialist stops the regress at the beginning by simply denying a cause for bodies. The materialist's chain of causes for accidents ends at circular motion, which is also uncaused in their view.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
علة
دهري
سلسلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali accepts the philosophers' argument for the Necessary Existent and only rejects its application to bodies.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says he has already shown the corruption of their entire proof for the Necessary Existent's attributes, not just its application.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main response to the philosophers' objection based on the Necessary Existent?
A) The materialist denies a cause for bodies, so the chain is cut at the start
B) The philosophers' proof is correct but misapplied
C) He accepts the objection and abandons the materialist argument
D) He argues that circular motion is itself the necessary existent

Key Takeaway
Ghazali exposes the circularity in the philosophers' argument and reaffirms the materialist's ability to simply deny a cause for bodies, thereby avoiding the need for a transcendent creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that Ghazali exposes the flaw in the philosophers' proof: the regress can be cut at the material world. If you rely on logical proofs for your faith, does this passage unsettle you? Are you willing to admit that your belief in God may not be provable in a way that compels the materialist? How does that challenge the certainty you thought you had?
?
Ghazali shows that the materialist simply denies a cause for bodies. This means that no amount of reasoning forces belief. In your own life, have you ever used intellectual pride to look down on those who reject God? How does this passage humble you and call you to engage with genuine humility, knowing that the final step is not logic but a gift of guidance?

The Materialist Position and Ghazali's Challenge
Ghazali describes the materialist position and challenges philosophers to refute it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist doctrine as described by Ghazali and why it poses a serious challenge to philosophers who believe in an eternal world.

Core Idea
If bodies are eternal, the materialist can deny a creator without contradiction.

Ghazali sets up a dilemma for philosophers who claim the world is eternal. He first acknowledges the argument of the mutakallimun that bodies are originated and then turns to the philosophers: what prevents you from becoming materialists? He then outlines the materialist position in detail, showing that if bodies are eternal, there is no need for a maker. This lesson unpacks that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the materialist doctrine
List the key claims: bodies are eternal, only forms and accidents change, causes of accidents trace back to eternal circular motion.
Ghazali contrasts this with the mutakallimun view.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: why is this a powerful challenge to the philosophers? What would they need to disprove the materialist?
Consider that the materialist does not need a creator for bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ ١٠ ] في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على أن للعالم صانعاً وعلة فنقول : إن من ذهب إلى أن كل جسم فهو حادث ، لأنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، عقل مذهبهم فى قولهم : إنه يفتقر إلى صانع وعلة وأما أنتم فما الذى يمنعكم من مذهب الدهرية؟ وهو مذهب الدهرية؟ وهو أن العالم قديم كذلك ولا علة له ، ولا صانع ؛ وإنما العلة للحوادث ، وليس يحدث في العالم جسم ولا ينعدم جسم، وإنما تحدث الصور والأعراض . فإن الأجسام التي هي في السموات ، قديمة ، والعناصر الأربعة التي هي حشو فلك القمر ، وأجسامها وموادها ، قديمة ، وإنما تتبدل عليها الصور بالامتزاجات والاستحالات ، وتحدث النفوس الإنسانية ، والحيوانية ، والنباتية ؛ وهذه الحوادث تنتهى عللها إلى الحركة الدورية ، والحركة الدورية قديمة ، ومصدرها نفس قديمة للفلك ؛ فإذن لا علة للعالم ، ولا صانع لأجسامه ، بل هو كما هو عليه ، لم يزل قديماً كذلك بلا علة – أعنى الأجسام – فما معنى قولهم : إن هذه الأجسام ، وجودها بعلة وهي قديمة ؟ ! فإن قيل : كل ما لا علة له فهو واجب الوجود ، وقد ذكرنا من صفات واجب الوجود ، ما تبين به أن الجسم لا يكون واجب الوجود . — قلنا : وقد بينا فساد ما ادعيتموه من صفات واجب الوجود ، وأن البرهان لا يدل إلا على قطع السلسلة ، وقد انقطعت عند الدهرى في أول الأمر ، إذ يقول : لا علة للأجسام ، وأما الصور والأعراض فبعضها علة للبعض ، إلى أن تنتهى إلى الحركة الدورية ، وهى : بعضها سبب للبعض ، كما هو مذهب الفلاسفة ، وينقطع تسلسلها بها ، ومن تأمل ما ذكرناه ، علم عجز كل من يعتقد قدم الأجسام ، عن دعوى علة لها ، ولزمه الدهر والإلحاد كما صرح به فريق ، فهم الذين وفوا بمقتضى نظر هؤلاء
Issue 10: On Rendering Them Incapable of Providing Proof that the World Has a Maker and Cause. So we say: Whoever holds that every body is originated because it is not free from accidents has understood their doctrine in saying that it needs a maker and cause. But as for you, what prevents you from the doctrine of the Materialists? It is the doctrine of the Materialists? That the world is eternal likewise, having no cause and no maker; rather the cause is for accidents, and no body comes into being in the world nor does a body cease to exist, only forms and accidents arise. For the bodies that are in the heavens are eternal, and the four elements that fill the sphere of the moon, their bodies and matter are eternal, only forms change upon them through mixtures and transformations, and human, animal, and plant souls arise; and the causes of these accidents terminate in circular motion, and circular motion is eternal, and its source is an eternal soul of the sphere; thus the world has no cause, and its bodies have no maker, rather it is as it is, has always been eternal thus without cause – I mean the bodies – so what is the meaning of their saying: 'These bodies exist through a cause while they are eternal'? If it is said: 'Everything that has no cause is necessary of existence, and we have mentioned of the attributes of the Necessary of existence what makes clear that a body cannot be necessary of existence.' We said: We have already shown the corruption of what you have claimed of the attributes of the Necessary of existence, and that demonstration only indicates cutting off the chain, and it has been cut off at the Materialist from the very beginning, when he says: bodies have no cause, while forms and accidents are causes of one another until they terminate in circular motion, and it is: some of them are causes of others, as is the doctrine of the philosophers, and their chain is cut off by it. And whoever reflects on what we have mentioned knows the incapacity of everyone who believes in the eternity of bodies to claim a cause for them, and he is compelled to materialism and atheism, as a group has declared, and they are those who have fulfilled the requisite of the view of these [philosophers].
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a challenge to the philosophers who affirm an eternal world. Ghazali begins by noting that those who hold bodies are originated due to being subject to accidents have a consistent argument for a creator. He then questions the philosophers: what prevents them from accepting materialism? He describes the materialist view in detail: bodies are eternal; only forms and accidents change; the chain of causes for accidents terminates in eternal circular motion and a soul of the sphere. Thus the materialist denies any cause for bodies and no creator. An objection is raised: everything without a cause is necessary of existence, and bodies cannot be necessary. Ghazali responds that the philosophers' demonstration only cuts off the chain of causes, but the materialist cuts it at the very beginning by denying a cause for bodies. Therefore, the philosophers cannot refute materialism; anyone who believes bodies are eternal is forced into materialism and atheism, as some have openly admitted.

Key Terms
دهري
صانع
علة
حوادث
صور
أعراض
حركة دورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Materialism is easily refuted by the argument from contingency.
Correction: Ghazali shows that if bodies are considered eternal, the contingency argument fails; the materialist has a coherent alternative.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what do materialists claim about the ultimate cause of accidents?
A) They are caused by a transcendent creator.
B) They terminate in eternal circular motion.
C) They are uncaused.
D) They are caused by the four elements.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali forces philosophers to confront that their eternal world view leads logically to materialism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The materialist says the world is self-sufficient, needing no creator. Do you ever live as though the universe runs on its own, forgetting that every moment of your existence depends directly on Allah's will and preservation? What concrete reminders can you put in place today to affirm Allah's constant role as al-Qayyum?
?
Ghazali forces philosophers to follow their beliefs to their logical end. Are you willing to examine your own beliefs—about God, your purpose, or your daily choices—to their full consequences, even if it means uncomfortable changes? What is one belief you hold that you have not fully acted upon?
?
The materialist attributes all change to natural cycles and denies a transcendent cause. When you see the beauty and order of nature, do you instinctively credit the creation rather than the Creator? How can you train your heart to see Allah's hand directly in every blessing and trial?

Ghazali's Response to the Necessary Existence Objection
Ghazali rebuts the philosophers' objection and declares materialism unavoidable for them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the materialist position against the objection that bodies cannot be necessary of existence, and why he concludes the philosophers cannot refute materialism.

Core Idea
The philosophers' argument only cuts the chain; the materialist cuts it at the root.

After presenting the materialist challenge, Ghazali anticipates the philosophers' likely objection: whatever has no cause is necessary of existence, and they have argued that a body cannot be necessary. Ghazali counters by pointing out that the philosophers' own proof merely stops a chain of contingent causes, whereas the materialist simply denies any cause for bodies from the start. Thus the objection fails, and anyone who believes bodies are eternal is forced into atheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage containing the philosophers' objection and Ghazali's reply. Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We said...'
This is a typical dialectical format.
2
Analyze Ghazali's counter-argument
Identify why Ghazali says the philosophers' demonstration only 'cuts the chain' and how the materialist 'cuts it at the beginning.'
The materialist asserts bodies have no cause, so the chain never starts.
3
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that anyone who posits eternal bodies is compelled to materialism and atheism. Reflect on why this is a powerful critique.
Some philosophers openly admitted this, according to Ghazali.

Issue 10: On Rendering Them Incapable of Providing Proof that the World Has a Maker and Cause. But as for you, what prevents you from the doctrine of the Materialists? It is the doctrine of the Materialists? That the world is eternal likewise, having no cause and no maker; rather the cause is for accidents, and no body comes into being in the world nor does a body cease to exist, only forms and accidents arise. For the bodies that are in the heavens are eternal, and the four elements that fill the sphere of the moon, their bodies and matter are eternal, only forms change upon them through mixtures and transformations, and human, animal, and plant souls arise; and the causes of these accidents terminate in circular motion, and circular motion is eternal, and its source is an eternal soul of the sphere; thus the world has no cause, and its bodies have no maker, rather it is as it is, has always been eternal thus without cause – I mean the bodies – so what is the meaning of their saying: 'These bodies exist through a cause while they are eternal'? If it is said: 'Everything that has no cause is necessary of existence, and we have mentioned of the attributes of the Necessary of existence what makes clear that a body cannot be necessary of existence.' Commentary
This passage is a challenge to the philosophers who affirm an eternal world. Ghazali begins by noting that those who hold bodies are originated due to being subject to accidents have a consistent argument for a creator. He then questions the philosophers: what prevents them from accepting materialism? According to Ghazali, why does the philosophers' demonstration fail to refute the materialist?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ponder the existence of the universe, do you ever catch yourself assuming it simply 'is' without needing a Creator? Ghazali warns that such an assumption leads to atheism. How can you actively strengthen your certainty that every moment is caused and sustained by Allah?
?
The materialist cuts the causal chain at the beginning because it feels more comfortable to avoid accountability to a Creator. Is there any area of your life where you prefer to think 'this just happened' rather than acknowledging Allah's control? What would it cost you to truly submit that area to Him today?
?
Ghazali's debate seems abstract, but the consequence is eternal. Do you truly believe that the existence of the universe demands a cause? If so, what does that demand of you right now—in your daily habits, thoughts, and priorities?

Refuting the Argument from Possibility and Necessity for Bodies
Objection-and-answer on bodies' necessity/possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the argument from possibility and necessity for proving bodies need a creator, and how he reduces it to the simple question of whether bodies have a cause.

Core Idea
Ghazali's critical move: the terms 'necessary' and 'possible' obscure the real issue—do bodies have a cause?

In this passage, Ghazali responds to an objection that bodies must be either necessary of existence (impossible) or possible (needing a cause). He argues that these terms are not understood and that the real question is simply whether bodies have a cause. The materialist's denial of a cause is not contradicted by this argument, and further objections about parts are also refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's initial response
Notice how the objector forces a dichotomy between necessary and possible, and how Ghazali rejects this framing outright.
Ghazali says the terms are not understood and hide obfuscations.
2
Understand the reduction to 'having a cause'
See how Ghazali replaces 'necessary/possible' with 'has a cause/does not have a cause'. This clarifies the real issue.
The materialist can simply say 'no cause' and the argument fails.
3
Examine the objection about parts
The objector raises that bodies have parts and the whole depends on them. Ghazali concedes but says parts could be eternal without a cause.
This is a sub-objection within the main response.
4
See the refutation's conclusion
Ghazali concludes that without belief in the origination of bodies, belief in the Maker has no basis.
This links to broader themes in the Tahafut.

فإن قيل : الدليل عليه أن هذه الأجسام ، إما أن تكون واجبة الوجود ، وهو محال ، وإما أن تكون ممكنة ، وكل ممكن يفتقر إلى علة قلنا : لا يفهم لفظ « واجب الوجود » و « ممكن الوجود » فكل تلبيساتهم مخبأة في هاتين اللفظتين ، فلنعدل إلى المفهوم ، وهو نفي العلة وإثباتها ، فكأنهم يقولون : هذه الأجسام ، لها علة أم لا علة لها ؟ ! فيقول الدهرى ، لا علة لها فما المستنكر ؟ ! وإذا عنى بالإمكان هذا (۱) فنقول : إنه واجب ، وليس بممكن ، وقولهم : إن الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون واجباً تحكم لا أصل له . فإن قيل : لا ينكر أن الجسم له أجزاء ، وأن الجملة إنما تتقوم بالأجزاء وأن الأجزاء تكون سابقة على الذات في الجملة . . قلنا : ليكن كذلك فالجملة تقومت بالأجزاء واجتماعها، ولا علة للأجزاء، ولا لاجتماعها ، بل هي قديمة كذلك بلا علة فاعلة فلا يمكنهم رد هذا ، إلا بما ذكروه من لزوم نفى الكثرة عن الموجود الأول وقد أبطلناه عليهم ، ولا سبيل لهم سواه . فبان أن من لا يعتقد حدوث الأجسام ، فلا أصل لاعتقاده في الصانع أصلا
(۱) يشير إلى « ما له علة »
For if it is said: The proof of that is that these bodies are either necessary of existence, which is impossible, or possible, and every possible needs a cause. We say: The term 'necessary of existence' and 'possible of existence' are not understood; all their obfuscations are hidden in these two terms. So let us turn to what is understood, which is the negation and affirmation of a cause. It is as if they say: Do these bodies have a cause or not? The Materialist says: They have no cause. So what is objectionable? And if by possibility (1) this [i.e., having a cause] is meant, then we say: It is necessary, not possible. And their statement that a body cannot be necessary is an arbitrary claim with no basis. For if it is said: It cannot be denied that the body has parts, and the whole is constituted by the parts, and the parts are prior to the whole in the whole. We say: Let it be so; the whole is constituted by the parts and their combination, yet the parts have no cause, nor their combination, rather they are eternal thus without an efficient cause. They cannot refute this except by what they mentioned of the necessity of negating multiplicity from the First Existent, which we have already invalidated for them. They have no other way. So it becomes clear that whoever does not believe in the origination of bodies has no basis at all for his belief in the Maker.
(1) It refers to 'that which has a cause'.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the argument from possibility and necessity. The objector argues that bodies must be either necessary (impossible for a body) or possible (requiring a cause). Ghazali replies that these terms are not understood; they are obfuscations. The real question is simply whether bodies have a cause. The materialist denies a cause outright, and there is nothing objectionable in that. If by 'possible' they mean 'having a cause,' then the materialist's position (no cause) would be 'necessary' – not subject to that classification. The claim that a body cannot be necessary is arbitrary. A further objection is raised about parts: bodies have parts and the whole depends on them. Ghazali responds that even if so, the parts themselves could be eternal without a cause. The philosophers' only recourse is to argue that the First Existent must be simple (free from multiplicity), but Ghazali states he has already refuted that. Thus, anyone who denies the origination of bodies has no basis for belief in a Creator.

ممكن الوجود
إمكان
تحكم
أجزاء
كثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from possibility and necessity is sufficient to prove a creator for bodies.
Correction: Ghazali shows the argument fails if bodies are assumed eternal; the materialist can simply deny a cause.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the real issue in the argument about bodies being necessary or possible?
A) Whether bodies have attributes.
B) Whether bodies have a cause.
C) Whether bodies are composed of parts.
D) Whether bodies are in motion.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali dismantles the argument from possibility and necessity by showing it reduces to an arbitrary denial of a cause for bodies, which the materialist can accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the argument from possibility and necessity to prove God's existence to yourself or others? After reading Ghazali's critique, are you willing to admit that it may not be as solid as you thought? What does that mean for the foundation of your belief?
?
Ghazali says the terms 'necessary of existence' and 'possible of existence' are obfuscations. Are there any terms or concepts in your own faith that you use without truly understanding their meaning? Will you commit to examining them more deeply today, starting with one specific term?
?
Imagine a materialist tells you, 'The universe simply exists, no cause needed.' Based on this lesson, do you have a response that does not rely on the assumption that the universe is originated? If not, how will you seek a more solid argument for your faith?

The Terminological Trap: Refuting the Necessary/Possible Argument
Ghazali deconstructs the necessary/possible argument as terminological obfuscation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' distinction between necessary and possible existence is a terminological smokescreen that does not prove a cause for eternal bodies.

Core Idea
The real issue is whether there is a cause, not whether something is possible.

Ghazali begins by exposing the hidden weakness in the philosophers' favorite argument. They claim eternal bodies must be either necessary (impossible) or possible (needing a cause). But Ghazali shows that the terms 'necessary' and 'possible' are not the real issue — the real question is whether there is a cause or not. And if the atheist simply says 'no cause,' the philosophers have no reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and understand the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the philosophers present a dilemma: bodies are either necessary or possible, and possibility requires a cause.
The passage begins with Ghazali's own assertion that belief in eternal bodies leads to atheism.
2
Notice Ghazali's rephrasing
Ghazali ignores the terms and rephrases the question as 'Does the body have a cause or not?' The atheist simply answers 'no cause,' which is logically acceptable.
This exposes the terminological obfuscation.
3
Grasp why the atheist's denial is unanswerable
If by 'possible' the philosophers mean 'has a cause,' then the body would be 'necessary' (without cause), which they arbitrarily claim a body cannot be. Their objection is baseless.
This shows the argument does not force a belief in a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلاسفة ، وينقطع تسلسلها بها ، ومن تأمل ما ذكرناه ، علم عجز كل من يعتقد قدم الأجسام ، عن دعوى علة لها ، ولزمه الدهر والإلحاد كما صرح به فريق ، فهم الذين وفوا بمقتضى نظر هؤلاء
فإن قيل : الدليل عليه أن هذه الأجسام ، إما أن تكون واجبة الوجود ، وهو محال ، وإما أن تكون ممكنة ، وكل ممكن يفتقر إلى علة
قلنا : لا يفهم لفظ « واجب الوجود » و « ممكن الوجود » فكل تلبيساتهم مخبأة في هاتين اللفظتين ، فلنعدل إلى المفهوم ، وهو نفي العلة وإثباتها ، فكأنهم يقولون : هذه الأجسام ، لها علة أم لا علة لها ؟ ! فيقول الدهرى ، لا علة لها فما المستنكر ؟ ! وإذا عنى بالإمكان هذا (۱) فنقول : إنه واجب ، وليس بممكن ، وقولهم : إن الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون واجباً تحكم لا أصل له .
فإن قيل : لا ينكر أن الجسم له أجزاء ، وأن الجملة إنما تتقوم بالأجزاء وأن الأجزاء تكون سابقة على الذات في الجملة .
قلنا : ليكن كذلك فالجملة تقومت بالأجزاء واجتماعها، ولا علة للأجزاء، ولا لاجتماعها ، بل هي قديمة كذلك بلا علة فاعلة فلا يمكنهم رد هذا ، إلا بما ذكروه من لزوم نفى الكثرة عن الموجود الأول وقد أبطلناه عليهم ، ولا سبيل لهم سواه .
فبان أن من لا يعتقد حدوث الأجسام ، فلا أصل لاعتقاده في الصانع أصلا
The philosophers, and their chain is cut off by it. Whoever ponders what we have mentioned knows the incapacity of everyone who believes in the eternity of bodies to claim a cause for them, and he is compelled to atheism and disbelief as a group has declared; they are the ones who fulfilled the requirement of these people's view.
If it is said: The evidence for it is that these bodies are either necessary of existence, which is impossible, or possible, and every possible needs a cause.
We say: The expression 'necessary of existence' and 'possible of existence' is not understood; all their obfuscations are concealed in these two expressions. So let us turn to the understood meaning, which is negation and affirmation of a cause. It is as if they say: These bodies, do they have a cause or no cause? The atheist says: They have no cause. So what is objectionable? And if by possibility is meant this (i.e., having a cause), then we say: It is necessary, not possible. And their statement that a body cannot be necessary is an arbitrary claim with no basis.
If it is said: It is not denied that the body has parts, and the whole is constituted by the parts, and the parts are prior to the essence in the whole.
We say: Let it be so. The whole is constituted by the parts and their aggregation, and there is no cause for the parts, nor for their aggregation. Rather, they are eternal in that way without an efficient cause. So they cannot refute this except by what they mentioned of the necessity of negating multiplicity from the First Existent, and we have already refuted that against them, and they have no way other than it.
Thus it is clear that whoever does not believe in the temporal origination of bodies has no basis for his belief in the Maker at all.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that eternal bodies must have a cause. Ghazali begins by summarizing the consequence for those who believe in the eternity of bodies: they cannot claim a cause for them, which leads to atheism. Then he presents an objection from the philosophers: bodies are either necessary (impossible) or possible (needing a cause). Ghazali responds by pointing out that the terms 'necessary' and 'possible' are obscure and the real issue is whether there is a cause. The atheist can simply deny a cause. If by 'possible' they mean 'has a cause,' then the body would be necessary, not possible. The philosophers' claim that a body cannot be necessary is baseless. Then another objection is raised: bodies have parts and the whole depends on parts, so parts are prior. Ghazali answers: Even if that is true, there is still no cause for the parts or their aggregation; they are eternal without an efficient cause. The philosophers cannot refute this except by appealing to the argument that the First must be free from multiplicity, which Ghazali says he has already refuted. So the lesson concludes that anyone who does not accept the temporal origination of bodies has no foundation for belief in a Creator.

B) Whether the body has a cause or not

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' distinction between necessary and possible existence is a terminological ruse that does not force belief in a cause for eternal bodies.

?

?
The atheist in the passage simply denies a cause for the universe. Are you living as if there is no ultimate cause for your existence, or as if that cause is too distant to matter? What would it mean for you to honestly confront the question of why you exist?
?
Ghazali calls the philosophers' claim that a body cannot be necessary an 'arbitrary claim with no basis.' Where do you hold beliefs about God or the universe that you have accepted without rigorous examination, simply because they are comfortable? Will you commit today to questioning those foundations with the same critical spirit?

Refuting the Argument from Parts: Eternal Bodies Without a Cause
Ghazali counters the argument from parts by denying any efficient cause for parts or their aggregation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the claim that the composition of bodies implies a need for a cause, and why he says the philosophers' only recourse is already refuted.

Core Idea
Parts and composition do not necessitate an efficient cause for eternal bodies.

The philosophers raise a second objection: bodies have parts, and the whole depends on the parts, so the parts are prior and the body is contingent. Ghazali replies that even if this is true, it does not prove a cause for the parts or their aggregation. They could be eternal and without an efficient cause. The only way the philosophers can refute this is by appealing to the principle that the First must be free from multiplicity, which Ghazali claims to have already refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about parts
Read the Arabic and translation. The philosophers argue that bodies have parts, and the whole is constituted by them, so the parts are prior and the body depends on them.
This is a different approach: not from necessary/possible but from composition.
2
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali concedes the structure but denies that it implies an efficient cause. He says there is no cause for the parts nor for their aggregation; they are simply eternal.
He accepts the premise but denies the conclusion.
3
Recognize the philosophers' only recourse
Ghazali points out that the only way to refute him is to argue that the First Existent must be free from multiplicity, which he says he has already refuted. Thus the philosophers have no valid argument left.
This reinforces that without temporal creation, belief in a Creator is baseless.

Ghazali's Muslim Proof for God's Knowledge of All Things
Ghazali states the Muslim proof for God's knowledge and challenges philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muslim theological argument that God knows all things because He wills them, and how this argument challenges the philosophers.

Core Idea
God knows all contingent things because He wills them.

In this passage from the Tahafut al-Falasifa, Ghazali presents the core Muslim argument for God's universal knowledge. He begins by dividing existence into contingent and eternal, then shows that since all contingent things are willed by God, they must be known by Him. The argument proceeds logically from will to knowledge to life, concluding that God knows His own essence and all things. Finally, Ghazali challenges the philosophers to provide a proof if they deny this grounding in divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from the Muslim argument to the challenge to philosophers.
The argument is dense; read slowly to follow the logical steps.
2
Identify the key premise
Pay attention to the statement: 'the willed, necessarily, must be known to the willer.' This is the crucial link between God's will and His knowledge.
This premise is what grounds the entire proof.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the steps: contingency → will → knowledge → life → knowledge of self and others. Write them down in order.
Notice how each step builds on the previous one.
4
Understand the challenge to philosophers
Ghazali asks: if you deny that the world is created by God's will, how can you claim God knows anything other than Himself? This sets up the need for an alternative proof.
This challenge reveals the weakness in the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [١١] في تعجيز من يرى منهم أن الأول يعلم غيره ويعلم الأنواع والأجناس بنوع كلى. فنقول: أما المسلمون لما انحصر عندهم الوجود في حادث وقديم، ولم يكن عندهم قديم إلا الله سبحانه وصفاته، وكان ما عداه حادثاً من جهته بإرادته، حصل عندهم مقدمة ضرورية في علمه، فإن المراد بالضرورة لا بد أن يكون معلوماً للمريد، فبنوا عليه أن الكل معلوم له، لأن الكل مراد له، وحادث بإرادته، فلا كائن إلا وهو حادث بإرادته، ولم يبق إلا ذاته. ومهما ثبت أنه مريد عالم بما أراده فهو حي بالضرورة، وكل حي يعرف غيره، فهو بأن يعرف ذاته أولى، فصار الكل عندهم معلوماً لله تعالى وعرفوه بهذا الطريق، بعد أن بان لهم أنه مريد لإحداث العالم. فأما أنتم، إذا زعمتم أن العالم القديم لم يحدث بإرادته، فمن أين عرفتم أنه يعرف غير ذاته؟! فلابد من الدليل عليه.
Question [11]: On incapacitating those among them who hold that the First knows other than Himself and knows species and genera in a universal manner. We say: As for the Muslims, since existence was confined for them to contingent and eternal, and they had no eternal except God—exalted is He—and His attributes, and everything else was contingent from Him by His will, they arrived at a necessary premise concerning His knowledge: for the willed, necessarily, must be known to the willer. So they built upon this that everything is known to Him, because everything is willed by Him and contingent by His will; thus there is no existent except that it is contingent by His will, and nothing remains except His essence. And whenever it is established that He is a willer who knows what He wills, He is necessarily living, and every living being knows other than itself; so it is more fitting that He knows His essence. Thus everything became known to God—exalted is He—in their view, and they came to know Him by this path, after it became clear to them that He is a willer of the origination of the world. As for you, if you claim that the world is eternal and did not originate by His will, then how do you know that He knows other than His essence? There must be a proof for that.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct elements: first, Ghazali's statement of the Muslim theological proof for God's knowledge of all things, and second, his challenge to the philosophers who deny the world's creation through will. The Muslim proof begins by dividing existence into the contingent (hadith) and the eternal (qadim). The only eternal is God and His attributes; everything else is contingent and brought into existence by God's will. From this, a necessary premise arises: that which is willed must be known to the willer. Since all contingent things are willed by God, they are all known to Him. Additionally, because God is a willer who knows what He wills, He is necessarily living (hayy). Every living being knows other than itself; a fortiori, God knows His own essence. Thus, Muslims conclude that all things are known to God. They attained this knowledge after realizing that God is the willer of the world's origination. Then Ghazali turns to the philosophers: if they claim the world is eternal and not originated by God's will, they cannot simply assert that God knows other than Himself; they must provide a proof. This sets up the refutation that follows.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
مريد
معلوم
إرادة
علم
حي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge of things is based on the existence of those things independently of His will.
Correction: Ghazali shows that God's knowledge follows from His will: because He wills all contingent things, He must know them.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's presentation of the Muslim argument, what is the necessary relationship that grounds God's knowledge of all things?
A) God is living, so He must know all things.
B) God is eternal, so He knows all things.
C) All contingent things are willed by God, and the willed must be known to the willer.
D) God created the world, so He must know it.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of all things is necessarily entailed by His will to create them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you truly believe that God knows you completely—your intentions, struggles, and hidden sins—or do you sometimes act as if He is unaware? What would change in your daily life if you fully embraced this truth?
?
Ghazali argued that God's knowledge of all things is necessary because He wills them. Since you are a contingent being willed by God, does knowing that He knows you intimately make you more aware of your purpose? How will you live today in light of that certainty?

The Intellect Strengthens While Senses Weaken
Ghazali contrasts the weakening of senses with the strengthening of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the intellect strengthens through contemplation, unlike bodily senses, and that any fatigue is due to reliance on the imaginative faculty.

Core Idea
The intellect grows stronger with use, while senses grow weak.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' assumption that intellectual power fades like physical senses. He shows that the intellect actually strengthens through contemplation, and any fatigue is due to reliance on the bodily faculty of imagination. This passage is a key rebuttal in his critique of the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast Ghazali draws between senses and intellect.
The passage begins mid-sentence—'For sight'—so it is part of a larger argument.
2
Identify the contrast between senses and intellect
Ghazali describes how senses become fatigued or impaired by intense stimuli (e.g., loud sounds, bright lights, intense sweetness). Then he states that the intellect is the opposite: continuous contemplation of intelligibles strengthens it.
Look for the phrase 'والأمر فى القوة العقلية بالعكس' (the matter concerning the intellectual power is the opposite).
3
Understand the role of the imaginative power
Ghazali explains that if the intellect sometimes tires, it is because it uses the imaginative power, a bodily instrument. The imagination weakens, not the intellect itself.
The imaginative power (القوة الخيالية) is a faculty tied to the body.
4
Grasp the general principle of variation
Ghazali argues that it is not necessary for what applies to one type of sense or body to apply to another. Bodies vary: some are weakened by motion, others strengthened. So the intellect does not have to weaken like the senses.
The principle is: 'الحكم الثابت لبعض الأشياء، ليس يلزم أن يثبت للكل' (the judgment established for some things does not necessarily hold for all).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للبصر ، فإنهما ربما يفسدان ، أو يمنعان ، عقبيهما ، من إدراك الصوت الخفى ، والمرئيات الدقيقة ، بل من ذاق الحلاوة الشديدة ، لا يحس بعدها بحلاوة دونها . والأمر فى القوة العقلية بالعكس ، فإن إدامتها للنظر إلى المعقولات ، لا يتعبها ودرك الضروريات الجلية يقويها ، على درك النظريات الخفية ، ولا يضعفها ، وإن عرض لها فى بعض الأوقات كلال ، فذلك لاستعمالها القوة الخيالية ، واستعانتها بها ، فتضعف آلة القوة الخيالية ، فلا تخدم العقل . وهذا ، من الطراز السابق ، فإنا نقول : لا يبعد أن تختلف الحواس الجسمانية ، في هذه الأمور ، فليس ما يثبت منها للبعض ، يجب أن يثبت للآخر بل لا يبعد أن تتفاوت الأجسام ، فيكون منها ما يضعفها نوع من الحركة ، ومنها ما يقويها نوع من الحركة ولا يوهنها ، وإن كان يؤثر فيها ، فيكون ثم سبب يجدد قوتها ، بحيث لا تحس بالأثر فيها . فكل هذا ممكن، إذ الحكم الثابت لبعض الأشياء، ليس يلزم أن يثبت للكل .
For sight, for they (i.e., the two senses) perhaps become corrupted, or they prevent what follows them from perceiving the faint sound and the minute visible objects. Rather, he who tastes intense sweetness does not feel after it a sweetness less than it. And the matter concerning the intellectual power is the opposite: for its continuous contemplation of the intelligibles does not tire it, and the attainment of clear necessary truths strengthens it for attaining the hidden theoretical truths, and does not weaken it. And if at some times fatigue occurs to it, that is because of its use of the imaginative power and its seeking aid from it, so the instrument of the imaginative power weakens and does not serve the intellect. And this, from the previous pattern, for we say: It is not unlikely that the bodily senses differ in these matters, for what is established for some of them does not have to be established for the other. Rather, it is not unlikely that bodies vary, so among them is that which a type of motion weakens, and among them is that which a type of motion strengthens and does not enervate, even if it affects it. And then there is a cause that renews its strength, such that it does not feel the effect in it. So all this is possible, since the judgment established for some things does not necessarily hold for all.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal by Ghazali against the philosophers' analogy between bodily senses and the intellect. He begins by describing how senses become fatigued or impaired by intense stimuli: for example, after tasting intense sweetness, one cannot perceive a lesser sweetness, and loud sounds or bright lights can hinder the perception of faint ones. He contrasts this with the intellectual power: continuous contemplation of intelligibles does not tire it; rather, attaining clear necessary truths strengthens it for grasping more subtle theoretical truths. If fatigue sometimes occurs, it is due to the intellect's reliance on the imaginative faculty, which tires because it is a bodily instrument. Ghazali then generalizes: it is not necessary that what applies to one type of sense or body applies to another. Bodies can vary such that some are weakened by motion while others are strengthened by it. Therefore, the fact that the senses weaken with age or use does not imply that the intellect must also weaken. The key point is that a property established for one thing does not have to be universal.

Key Terms
القوة العقلية (intellectual power)
القوة الخيالية (imaginative power)
المعقولات (intelligibles)
الضروريات الجلية (clear necessary truths)
النظريات الخفية (hidden theoretical truths)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the intellect sometimes tires, it must be a bodily faculty like the senses.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that such fatigue is due to reliance on the imagination, not the intellect itself. The intellect strengthens with use.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the intellect sometimes experience fatigue?
A) Because the intellect itself is weakened by continuous use.
B) Because it uses the imaginative power, which is a bodily instrument that tires.
C) Because the body weakens after age forty.
D) Because the intellect is not capable of sustained thought.

Key Takeaway
The intellect does not weaken with use; it strengthens, and any fatigue is due to the bodily imagination, not the intellect itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you find yourself mentally exhausted after studying, do you attribute it to your intellect growing weak or to your body's distractions? How can you apply Ghazali's insight to push through that fatigue knowing the intellect itself strengthens with use?
?

?
Ghazali says the intellect is not weakened by continuous contemplation. Think of a time you avoided deep thinking because it felt tiring. Will you now see that tiredness as a sign of your bodily imagination, not your intellect? What will you do differently this week to exercise your intellect?
