The World's Contingency Implies Size Variation and Creation Ex Nihilo
Ghazali argues from world's size variability to contingency and creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the world's capability of being larger or smaller to argue for its contingency and the possibility of creation from nothing.

Core Idea
The world's changeability proves it is not necessary, pointing to creation ex nihilo.

Ghazali confronts the philosophers' claim that the world cannot be larger or smaller. He argues that if it were not capable of such change, it would be necessary, but the world is contingent. This change implies the annihilation or creation of matter, supporting the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo. In this passage, he also critiques the notion of 'possible before existence' as meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the overall argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the logical structure
Identify the conditional: if the world cannot be larger/smaller, then it is necessary. Then the negation: the world is not necessary. Therefore, the conclusion: it can be larger/smaller.
3
Understand the counter to philosophers
Ghazali considers the philosophical objection that size change is impossible. He replies that possibility before existence is meaningless; only actual existence matters.
This refutes the idea that the world could have been created earlier.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports creation ex nihilo. The change requires annihilation or creation of matter, showing matter is not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرى الغزالى أن العالم إذا لم يكن قابلا لأن يكون أكبر مما هو عليه أو أصغر ، فإنه يكون واجباً . والعالم ليس بواجب فإذن هو قابل لأن يكون أكبر أو أصغر ؛ وذلك يكون بإعدام جزء من المادة ، أو خلق جزء منها وهذا يفيد إمكان خلق المادة من لا شيء ، وصيرورتها إلى ما أشرنا إليه فى مقدمة الطبعة الثالثة، فكيف ساغ لا شيء وهو لبعض الباحثين أن يدعوا بداهة استحالة ذلك ؟ يقول الغزالى : إذا ساغ للفلاسفة أن يقولوا : إن كون العالم أكبر مما هو عليه أصغر ، مستحيل ، عارضناهم بقولنا : إن وجود العالم قبل وجوده لم يكن ممكناً ، وإنما الممكن وجوده في الوقت الذي وجد فيه ويعود الغزالي فيقرر أن ما يدعيه الفلاسفة من إمكان خلق العالم قبل خلقه بمدة، ثم بمدة أطول وهكذا ، كلام لا معنى له عند التحقيق
Al-Ghazali holds that if the world were not capable of being larger than it is or smaller, then it would be necessary. And the world is not necessary; therefore it is capable of being larger or smaller. And that would be by annihilating part of matter or creating part of it. And this indicates the possibility of creating matter from nothing, and its transformation to what we pointed to in the introduction to the third edition. So how is it permissible for some researchers to claim the obvious impossibility of that? Al-Ghazali says: 'If it is permissible for philosophers to say that it is impossible for the world to be larger or smaller, we oppose them by saying that the existence of the world before its existence was not possible; rather, what is possible is its existence at the time it came to exist.' And al-Ghazali returns to affirm that what the philosophers claim about the possibility of creating the world a period before its creation, then a longer period, and so on, is meaningless upon investigation.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the world's contingency. Ghazali reasons: if the world could not be larger or smaller, it would be necessary (i.e., it would have to be exactly as it is). But the world is not necessary (it is contingent), so it must be able to be larger or smaller. This change implies either annihilation or creation of matter, which shows the possibility of creation ex nihilo. Ghazali then counters a philosophical claim that the world cannot be larger or smaller: he says that the world's existence before it existed was not possible, only at the time it actually came to exist. So the idea of creating it earlier is meaningless.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib, necessary)
ممكن (mumkin, possible)
مادة (mādda, matter)
لا شيء (lā shay', nothing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That contingency implies possibility of earlier creation.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'possible before existence' is meaningless; only actual time matters.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what follows if the world were not capable of being larger or smaller?
A) It would be impossible
B) It would be necessary
C) It would be eternal
D) It would be infinite

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the world's capacity for size change to prove its contingency and the possibility of creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's very size shows its dependence on God, why do you still live as though your existence is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly believed that every moment you exist is a gift from nothing?
?
Al-Ghazali argues that the possibility of creation before actual existence is meaningless. Where in your life are you clinging to possibilities that never materialized, delaying your submission to God's timing?
?
The world could have been larger or smaller—it is not necessary. Does your trust in God reflect that He alone is the Necessary? Or do you treat worldly things as if they must be a certain way, and how might you realign your heart today?

The Argument from Possible Size for Contingency and Creation Ex Nihilo
Ghazali presents the argument that the world's possible change in size proves contingency and refutes impossibility of creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the world's potential to change size to argue for its contingency and the possibility of creation from nothing.

Core Idea
If the world can change size, it is contingent and matter can be created from nothing.

This passage presents a logical argument central to Ghazali's critique of the philosophers. He begins with a conditional: if something were necessary, it would be immutable in size. Since the world can be larger or smaller, it is contingent, implying that matter can be created or annihilated. This leads to the possibility of creation ex nihilo. Ghazali then turns the philosophers' own reasoning against them, showing that their claim of impossibility is unfounded.

Guided Path

The conditional is key: necessity implies immutability in size.
2

Notice that the world is not necessary, so it must be capable of being larger or smaller. This establishes the world's contingency.
Contingency means the world depends on something else for its existence and properties.
3
Draw the conclusion
The possibility of change in size involves annihilation or creation of matter, which implies creation ex nihilo. Reflect on how Ghazali uses this to refute the philosophers' claim of impossibility.
The conclusion directly challenges the philosophers' notion that creation from nothing is impossible.

Eternal Possibility Does Not Imply Eternal Existence
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' third proof for the world's eternity by denying that eternal possibility entails eternal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali’s refutation of the philosophers’ third argument for the eternity of the world, specifically why eternal possibility does not necessitate eternal existence.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not force eternal existence.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a key philosophical argument that claims the world is eternal because its possibility is eternal. He counters by pointing out that possibility, no matter how eternal, does not compel actual existence. This is a crucial move in the debate over the world's origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the structure: the philosophers' claim, Ghazali's response, and the analogy.
The passage is short but dense. Read carefully.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice Ghazali's comparison: the possibility of an infinite increase in the size of the world does not entail the existence of an infinite number of beings. Understand how this analogy supports his point.
The word 'ملاء' likely refers to angels or a host of beings.
3
Understand the logical point
Reflect on why possibility does not entail actuality. Possibility is a modal concept; actual existence requires additional conditions. Eternal possibility of the world does not make the world eternal.
This refutes the philosophers' inference from possibility to necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث للفلاسفة على قدم العالم بحث في أزلية الإمكان * * ويرى الغزالى أن الإمكان الأزلي لا يستلزم الوجود الأزلى ، مثلما لم يستلزم إمكان زيادة لا نهاية لها في حجم العالم ، إمكان وجود ملاء لا نهاية له
A third proof of the philosophers for the eternity of the world: a discussion on the eternity of possibility. And al-Ghazali holds that eternal possibility does not entail eternal existence, just as the possibility of an infinite increase in the size of the world does not entail the possibility of the existence of an infinite [host/angels].
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage introduces the third philosophical proof for the world's eternity, which argues from the eternity of possibility. Ghazali's response is presented: eternal possibility does not imply eternal actual existence. He uses an analogy: the possibility of an infinite increase in the world's size does not prove that an infinite number of beings (likely angels) actually exist. The possibility of something does not force its existence.

Key Terms
إمكان أزلي
الوجود الأزلي
لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal possibility necessitates eternal existence.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny that possibility can be eternal; he denies that it necessitates actual existence. Possibility and actuality are distinct categories.

Checkpoint

A) The possibility of a different size world does not imply actual change.
B) The possibility of infinite increase in size does not imply an actual infinite multitude.
C) The possibility of creation before time does not imply eternal matter.
D) The possibility of the world being larger does not make it necessary.

Key Takeaway
Eternal possibility does not force anything into existence; possibility and actuality are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ghazali's First Argument: Impossibility Would Also Need a Subject
Ghazali argues that if possibility requires a subject, impossibility would too.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if possibility required a subject, impossibility would also require one, undermining the philosophers' claim.

Core Idea
If possibility needs a subject, so does impossibility.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' assumption that possibility must inhere in an eternal subject. He uses a parallel argument: if that were true, impossibility would also need a subject, which is absurd. This lesson examines that first counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp Ghazali's point. Notice how he draws a parallel between possibility and impossibility.
The argument is simple but powerful.
2
Identify the logical structure
Ghazali uses a conditional: if possibility requires a subject, then the same applies to impossibility. Consider why this is problematic for the philosophers.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this argument means for the philosophers' position. If impossibility would need a subject, it shows that their reasoning is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويضيف الغزالي : أنه لو استدعى الإمكان شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه و إن البياض والسواد ممكنان قبل وجودهما ، فإن كان الإمكان وصفاً الذي يطرآن عليه ، كان إمكاناً للجسم لا لهما ، للجسم والمفروض أنهما الممكنان و إن نفوس الآدميين عند ابن سينا حادة ومجردة عن المادة ، فبماذا قام إمكانها قبل وجودها ؟.
And al-Ghazali adds: If possibility required an existent thing to be attributed to it, then impossibility would also require an existent thing to be attributed to it. And white and black are possible before their existence; if possibility is an attribute that inheres in the subject they occur upon, it would be a possibility of the body, not of them, for the body while it is assumed that they are the possibles. And the human souls according to Ibn Sina are created and immaterial, so in what did their possibility subsist before their existence?
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents three additional arguments by Ghazali against the philosophers' claim that possibility requires an eternal subject. First, if possibility needed a subject, then impossibility (الامتناع) would also need a subject, which is absurd. Second, consider accidents like whiteness and blackness: before they exist, they are possible. If possibility were an attribute of a subject, it would belong to the body in which they inhere, not to the accidents themselves, yet we say the accidents are possible. Third, Ibn Sina holds that human souls are created (حادثة) and immaterial (مجردة عن المادة). If possibility required a material subject, what would support the possibility of souls before their existence? This challenges the necessity of an eternal material subject.

Key Terms
الامتناع (impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only possibility requires a subject.
Correction: If the principle were true, it would apply to both possibility and impossibility, leading to an absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence Ghazali draws from the philosophers' claim that possibility needs a subject?
A) Impossibility also needs a subject.
B) Possibility is impossible.
C) Impossibility does not need a subject.
D) The subject must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first argument shows that the philosophers' principle leads to the absurd conclusion that impossibility would need a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or His attributes without critically examining the logical foundation of that belief? What assumption are you holding onto that you have not tested against clear reasoning like Ghazali does here?
?
This argument shows that a seemingly reasonable principle can lead to an absurdity. Is there any area of your faith where you cling to an idea because it 'feels' true, even when it doesn't hold up to scrutiny? Will you commit to examining it today?
?
If the philosophers' flawed reasoning could have misled them about the existence of a creator, could your own unexamined assumptions be pulling you away from a correct understanding of Allah? What step will you take this week to strengthen your convictions?

Ghazali's Second Argument: Accidents' Possibility Belongs to Bodies
Ghazali argues that accidents' possibility would belong to bodies, not to the accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if possibility were an attribute of a subject, then accidents' possibility would belong to bodies, not to the accidents themselves.

Core Idea
Accidents' possibility actually belongs to the body, not the accident.

Ghazali uses the example of whiteness and blackness to show a contradiction in the philosophers' view. If possibility is an attribute that inheres in a subject, then before accidents exist, their possibility would belong to the body they occur on, not to the accidents themselves. But we say the accidents are possible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path

This shows a contradiction.
3

First, if possibility needed a subject, then impossibility (الامتناع) would also need a subject, which is absurd. Second, consider accidents like whiteness and blackness: before they exist, they are possible. If possibility were an attribute of a subject, it would belong to the body in which they inhere, not to the accidents themselves, yet we say the accidents are possible. Third, Ibn Sina holds that human souls are created (حادثة) and immaterial (مجردة عن المادة). If possibility required a material subject, what would support the possibility of souls before their existence? This challenges the necessity of an eternal material subject.

Key Terms
العرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents' possibility is independent of the body.
Correction: If possibility required a subject, it would be the body's possibility, not the accident's, contradicting our language.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, if possibility is an attribute of the subject, whose possibility is it?
A) The accident's
B) The body's
C) The subject's
D) Impossible

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second argument shows that accidents' possibility would belong to bodies, not to the accidents, contradicting the philosophers' assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'It is possible that Allah will forgive me,' where does that possibility reside—in your expectation or in Allah’s will? Ghazali shows that philosophical categories can mislead us about reality. Are there places in your faith where you have accepted a human system of thought instead of the simple truth of the Quran and Sunnah? What will you do to untangle that?
?
Ghazali exposed a flaw in the philosophers' reasoning using the everyday example of whiteness and blackness. Think of one area of your life—your worship, your relationships, your understanding of God—where you have overcomplicated things. Are you willing to set aside that complexity and return to the simple, clear guidance of revelation? If so, what is your first step today?

Ghazali's Third Argument: Immaterial Souls' Possibility
Ghazali questions how immaterial souls' possibility could subsist without a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that immaterial souls, according to Ibn Sina, would have no subject for their possibility, challenging the need for an eternal material subject.

Core Idea
Immaterial souls have no material subject for their possibility.

Ghazali targets the philosophers' own view on human souls: Ibn Sina holds that souls are created and immaterial. If possibility requires a subject, what subject would support the possibility of souls before they exist? This lesson examines that dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that according to Ibn Sina, souls are immaterial and created (حادثة).
The word 'حادة' is likely a misspelling of 'حادثة' (created).
2
Identify the contradiction
If souls are immaterial, they cannot have a material subject. So the philosophers' claim that possibility needs a subject fails for souls.
3
Reflect on the outcome
This argument uses the philosophers' own doctrines against them. If they accept immaterial souls, they must accept that possibility can exist without a material subject.

Ghazali's Refutation of the Philosophers on Annihilation
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument that self-subsistent beings cannot be annihilated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the analogy of creation to argue that annihilation is an act of God, refuting the philosophers' claim that self-subsistent beings cannot cease to exist.

Core Idea
Annihilation is an act of the Powerful Chooser, just like creation.

The philosophers argue that the world is eternal because a self-subsistent being cannot be annihilated. Ghazali responds with a simple yet powerful analogy: just as God brings things into existence by His act, He can also annihilate them. Since we observe annihilation in the world, it is validly attributed to God. This passage is a key moment in the Tahafut where Ghazali defends the possibility of the world's end against philosophical necessitarianism.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
First, read the philosophers' argument that every self-subsistent being cannot cease to exist. Notice how they use this to assert the world's eternity.
The philosophers rely on the concept of 'self-subsistent' meaning not inhering in a subject.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Read Ghazali's rebuttal. He draws an analogy between bringing into existence (ījād) and annihilating (i'dām). Reflect on why this is effective.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Consider how Ghazali attributes annihilation to the 'powerful chooser' (al-qādir al-mukhtār). This emphasizes that God's actions are volitional, not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : إذا بطلت جميع الآراء التي تذهب إلى القول بفناء العالم ، ثبت أنه أبدي . ومفاد مذهبهم : أن كل قائم بنفسه لا في محل لا يتصور انعدامه بعد وجوده . ويجيب الغزالي عن هذا قائلا : برغم أنه في وسعنا أن نذب عن كل هذه المذاهب ، إلا أننا لا نطيل بذكر ذلك ونكتفي بأن نقول : إنه كما أن الإيجاد صيرورة الشيء موجوداً ، فكذلك الإعدام صيرورة الشيء معدوماً ، ولا شك أن الإعدام واقع ، فإنا نشاهد انعدام بعض الأشياء ، وما يقع يصح نسبته إلى القادر المختار.
The philosophers said: 'When all the views that go to say that the world perishes are invalidated, it is established that it is eternal. And the purport of their doctrine is: that every self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsihi) not in a subject does not admit of annihilation after its existence.' Ghazali responds: 'Despite that it is within our power to defend against all these doctrines, we do not prolong the mention of that and suffice ourselves by saying: just as bringing into existence (ījād) is the making of a thing existent, so too annihilation (i'dām) is the making of a thing non-existent. And there is no doubt that annihilation occurs, for we witness the annihilation of some things. And whatever occurs is validly attributed to the powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār).'
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that every self-subsistent being (not inhering in a subject) cannot cease to exist, so the world must be eternal. Ghazali refutes this by drawing an analogy: just as God's act of bringing into existence makes a thing existent, His act of annihilating makes it non-existent. Since annihilation is observed in the world, it can be attributed to the omnipotent agent who acts by choice. Ghazali insists that non-existence is an act just as existence is, and God is capable of both.

Key Terms
إيجاد (ījād)
إعدام (i'dām)
قائم بنفسه (qā'im bi-nafsihi)
قادر مختار (qādir mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A self-subsistent being (not in a subject) cannot cease to exist; therefore the world is eternal.
Correction: Ghazali shows that annihilation is an act of God just like creation, and it is observed in some things, so it is possible even for self-subsistent beings.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' main argument for the eternity of the world according to this passage?
A) The world cannot be annihilated because accidents are renewed.
B) Every self-subsistent being cannot cease to exist after existing.
C) Non-existence is not an act.
D) The world is eternal because it has no beginning.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that annihilation is an act of God, just as creation is, and since we observe annihilation, it is attributable to the Powerful Chooser, refuting the claim that every self-subsistent being is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is sustained by God's will every moment, and He can annihilate you as easily as He created you, how does that reshape your sense of security and dependence today?
?
You witness annihilation around you daily—people, things, moments. How does recognizing that each annihilation is a deliberate act of the Powerful Chooser change the way you view loss or death?
?
Ghazali says annihilation is an act of God just like creation. If both your coming into being and your passing away are intentional acts of God, what does that demand of you in the time between them?

The True Agent Requires Will and Choice
Ghazali defines true agency and refutes necessary emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of a true agent and why the philosophers' concept of necessary emanation does not qualify as genuine agency.

Core Idea
True agency means acting with will, not by necessity.

Ghazali opens his first objection by defining what it means to be an agent. He argues that the philosophers' claim that God is the agent of the world is invalid because their model of necessary emanation lacks will and choice. In this passage, he presents his definition and a powerful rebuttal based on the nature of lifeless objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage slowly
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Ghazali begins by stating the philosophers' view, then immediately declares it inconceivable. Focus on his definition of the agent.
The definition includes three elements: will, choice, knowledge.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Contrast Ghazali's definition (agent with will) with the philosophers' view (necessary emanation like cause to effect). Understand why Ghazali says their view makes God like a lifeless cause.
3
Analyze the jamād argument
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own principle that lifeless things have no act. If they call God an agent without will, they are using language metaphorically. See how this forces the conclusion that true agency requires will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفقت الفلاسفة على أن للعالم صانعاً وأن العالم فعل الله وصنعته. قال الغزالى : وهذا غير متصور على مذهبهم من ثلاثة أوجه. الوجه الأول : من جهة الفاعل؛ فإن الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل، مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار، ومع العلم بالمراد. وعندهم أن العالم صدر من الله تعالى صدور المعلول من العلة، ولزم منه لزوماً ضرورياً لا يتصور من الله دفعه. قال الفلاسفة : إن الصدور بالطبع فعل وصنع. قال الغزالي : هذا فاسد؛ لما صح من قولهم : الجماد لا فعل له وإنما الفعل للحيوان، وذلك من الكليات المشهورة الصادقة؛ فإن سمى الجماد فاعلا فبالاستعارة؛ وهذا يقتضى دخول الاختيار في مفهوم معنى الفاعل. قالت الفلاسفة : إن تسمية الفاعل فاعلا تعرف من اللغة، وإلا فقد ظهر فى العقل أن ما يكون سبباً للشيء ينقسم إلى ما يكون مريداً، وإلى ما لا يكون، ووقع النزاع فى أن اسم الفاعل على كلا القسمين حقيقة، أم لا؟ ... والعرب تقول : النار تحرق، والسيف يقطع، والثلج يبرد. إلخ. قال الغزالي : إن قولهم : النار تحرق، والسيف يقطع، تعبيرات مجازية، وإنما الفعل الحقيقى ما يكون بالإرادة. قالت الفلاسفة : نحن نعنى يكون الله فاعلا أنه سبب لكل موجود سواه، ولولا وجوده لما تصور وجود العالم، فهذا ما نعنى بكونه تعالى فاعلا. فإن كان الخصم يأبى أن يسمى هذا فاعلا، فلا مشاحة في الأسامي، بعد ظهور المعنى. قال الغزالى : إن غرضنا أن هذا المعنى لا يسمى فعلا وصنعاً، وإنما المعنى بالصنع والفعل ما يصدر عن الإرادة حقيقة، وقد نفيتم حقيقة معنى الفعل، ونطقتم بلفظه تجملا بالإسلاميين، ولا يتم الدين بإطلاق الألفاظ الفارغة عن المعاني. والمقصود من هذه المسألة الكشف عن هذا التلبيس.
The philosophers agreed that the world has a maker and that the world is the act of God and His making. Al-Ghazali said: This is inconceivable according to their doctrine from three aspects. The first aspect: from the perspective of the agent; for the agent is an expression for one from whom the act originates, with will for the act by way of choice, and with knowledge of the object of will. According to them, the world originated from God, Exalted, as the effect originates from the cause, and it followed necessarily in a way that it is inconceivable for God to repel. The philosophers said: Origination by nature is act and making. Al-Ghazali said: This is corrupt; for it is established from their statement: lifeless objects have no act, rather the act belongs to the living, and this is among the famous true universals; for if a lifeless object is called an agent, it is metaphorical; and this requires the inclusion of choice in the concept of the meaning of agent. The philosophers said: The naming of the agent as 'agent' is known from language; otherwise, it has become clear in the intellect that what is a cause for a thing is divided into what is willing and what is not, and the dispute occurred about whether the name 'agent' is true upon both divisions or not? ... The Arabs say: Fire burns, the sword cuts, and snow cools, etc. Al-Ghazali said: Their saying: 'Fire burns' and 'the sword cuts' are metaphorical expressions, and the true act is what is by will. The philosophers said: We mean by God being an agent that He is the cause for every existent other than Him, and if not for His existence, the existence of the world would not be conceivable; this is what we mean by His being an agent. If the opponent refuses to call this 'agent', there is no dispute over names after the meaning is clear. Al-Ghazali said: Our purpose is that this meaning is not called act and making; rather, the meaning of making and act is what originates from will truly, and you have negated the true meaning of act and uttered its word as flattery towards the Muslims, and the religion is not established by uttering words empty of meanings. The purpose of this discussion is to expose this deceit.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's first objection against the philosophers' claim that God is the agent and maker of the world, arguing that their concept is metaphorical and not literal. Ghazali begins by noting that the philosophers agree God is the maker of the world, but then states this is inconceivable on their own principles from three aspects, beginning with the aspect of the agent (fāʿil). He defines a true agent as one from whom an act proceeds with will (irāda) and choice (ikhtiyār) and knowledge of the object. The philosophers, however, hold that the world emanates from God necessarily, like an effect from a cause, without will. The philosophers respond by saying that 'origination by nature' (al-ṣudūr bi al-ṭabʿ) is also called act and making. Ghazali rebuts by invoking their own principle that lifeless objects (jamād) have no act; act belongs only to living beings, a self-evident universal. If a lifeless thing is called an agent, it is metaphorical, which implies that the true meaning of 'agent' includes choice. The philosophers then argue that the term 'agent' is determined by language, and in language a cause can be either willing or not; the dispute is whether the name applies truly to both. They cite Arabic idioms: fire burns, sword cuts, snow cools. Ghazali counters that these are metaphorical; the true act is by will. The philosophers then clarify their intention: they mean by 'God is an agent' that He is the cause of all other existence; without Him, nothing would exist. They say if the opponent refuses to call this agent, there is no quarrel over names once the meaning is clear. Ghazali rejects this: their purpose is that this meaning should not be called act or making; the real meaning of act is what originates from will. They have negated the true meaning of act and merely use the word to flatter Muslims. Religion is not established by empty words. This entire discussion aims to expose their deception.

Key Terms
فاعل
فعل
إرادة
اختيار
صدور
علة
جماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the term 'agent' can be applied to any cause, whether willing or not, without distinction.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the term 'agent' is properly applied only to acts that proceed from will and choice; otherwise, it is metaphorical. The philosophers' necessary emanation does not qualify as literal agency.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what three elements are essential for an act to be considered a true act of an agent?
A) Will, choice, and knowledge
B) Power, desire, and intention
C) Necessity, causality, and effect
D) Life, speech, and hearing

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that true agency requires will and choice, and the philosophers' model of necessary emanation is at best a metaphorical use of the term 'agent'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God as a distant, impersonal cause rather than a willing, choosing Lord? How does this lesson reveal the poverty of that view? Will you begin to address God as the truly active Agent who acts with will and choice? Commit to a specific change in your relationship with Him today.
?
Do you sometimes treat God as a necessary force—expecting certain outcomes without recognizing His absolute freedom? When you pray, do you pray to a God who can truly choose? Reflect on your last prayer: did you acknowledge His will? Resolve to pray tonight with deep awareness of God's free agency.
?
If calling a lifeless thing an agent is metaphorical, what does it mean to say God is your Agent? Have you reduced God to a predictable mechanism? Take a moment to repent and ask God to help you know Him as the true Agent who acts with perfect will and choice.

The Language Dispute: Metaphor or Reality?
Philosophers argue from language; Ghazali counters by distinguishing literal from metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' appeal to language and Ghazali's rebuttal that common expressions like 'fire burns' are metaphorical, not literal acts.

Core Idea
Everyday metaphors do not prove literal agency without will.

The philosophers respond to Ghazali by appealing to ordinary language: we say 'fire burns' and 'the sword cuts', yet fire and sword are not living agents. They argue that the term 'agent' can apply to both willing and non-willing causes. Ghazali counters that such expressions are metaphorical; the true act is only by will. This lesson explores that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Notice how they distinguish between willing and non-willing causes and claim that language allows calling both 'agent'. They cite common Arabic idioms as evidence.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Ghazali outright calls these expressions metaphorical. He asserts that the true meaning of 'act' (fiʿl) is what originates from will. This is a crucial point.
3
Reflect on the stakes
Why is this more than a semantic quibble? For Ghazali, the reality of God's agency and the religious concept of creation are at stake. If agency is merely metaphorical, then God is not truly a creator in the Islamic sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The philosophers said: We mean by God being an agent that He is the cause for every existent other than Him, and if not for His existence, the existence of the world would not be conceivable; this is what we mean by His being an agent. If the opponent refuses to call this 'agent', there is no dispute over names after the meaning is clear. Al-Ghazali said: Our purpose is that this meaning is not called act and making; rather, the meaning of making and act is what originates from will truly, and you have negated the true meaning of act and uttered its word as flattery towards the Muslims, and the religion is not established by uttering words empty of meanings. The purpose of this discussion is to expose this deceit.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's first objection against the philosophers' claim that God is the agent and maker of the world, arguing that their concept is metaphorical and not literal. Ghazali begins by noting that the philosophers agree God is the maker of the world, but then states this is inconceivable on their own principles from three aspects, beginning with the aspect of the agent (fāʿil). He defines a true agent as one from whom an act proceeds with will (irāda) and choice (ikhtiyār) and knowledge of the object. The philosophers, however, hold that the world emanates from God necessarily, like an effect from a cause, without will. The philosophers respond by saying that 'origination by nature' (al-ṣudūr bi al-ṭabʿ) is also called act and making. Ghazali rebuts by invoking their own principle that lifeless objects (jamād) have no act; act belongs only to living beings, a self-evident universal. If a lifeless thing is called an agent, it is metaphorical, which implies that the true meaning of 'agent' includes choice. The philosophers then argue that the term 'agent' is determined by language, and in language a cause can be either willing or not; the dispute is whether the name applies truly to both. They cite Arabic idioms: fire burns, sword cuts, snow cools. Ghazali counters that these are metaphorical; the true act is by will. The philosophers then clarify their intention: they mean by 'God is an agent' that He is the cause of all other existence; without Him, nothing would exist. They say if the opponent refuses to call this agent, there is no quarrel over names once the meaning is clear. Ghazali rejects this: their purpose is that this meaning should not be called act or making; the real meaning of act is what originates from will. They have negated the true meaning of act and merely use the word to flatter Muslims. Religion is not established by empty words. This entire discussion aims to expose their deception.

Key Terms
فاعل
حقيقة
مجاز
لغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because we say 'fire burns' in everyday speech, fire is literally an agent and has an act.
Correction: Ghazali argues such expressions are metaphorical; true agency requires will and life. Fire does not have an act in the literal sense.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali describe expressions like 'fire burns' and 'the sword cuts'?
A) Literal descriptions of cause and effect
B) Metaphorical expressions
C) Proof that fire is a living agent
D) Logical axioms

Key Takeaway
Common linguistic usage does not establish that non-willing causes are true agents; such expressions are metaphorical, not literal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you naturally say 'the sun rose' or 'the medicine healed me', are you aware that these are metaphors? How does this awareness change your reliance on Allah versus secondary causes?
?
The philosophers used everyday language to challenge a core belief about Allah's exclusive agency. What common phrases do you use that might subtly imply independent causes, and how can you rephrase them to reflect tawhid?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah's will creates real effects, why do you still feel fear or hope from created things? What would it take for your heart to align with this lesson's truth?

The Purpose of the Dispute: Exposing Deception
Philosophers redefine agency as mere causality; Ghazali charges them with deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' final clarification and Ghazali's conclusive charge that their use of the term 'agent' is a deceptive flattery that empties the word of its true meaning.

Core Idea
Words without true meaning cannot sustain religion.

In the final exchange, the philosophers clarify that by 'agent' they simply mean 'cause of existence'. They argue that if one rejects this naming, it is just a semantic disagreement. Ghazali responds sharply: the issue is not the name, but the reality. He accuses them of preserving the word 'agent' while stripping it of its true meaning, thereby deceiving Muslims. This lesson concludes Ghazali's first objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' redefinition
The philosophers state that by 'God is an agent' they mean He is the cause of all other existence. They dismiss disagreement over names as trivial once the meaning is clarified.
2
Analyze Ghazali's counter
Ghazali insists that this meaning does not count as 'act' or 'making' (ṣunʿ). True act must originate from will. He accuses them of using the word as flattery while denying its true meaning.
Note the strong language: 'uttering words empty of meanings' and 'expose this deceit'.
3
Understand the broader purpose
Ghazali states clearly that the entire discussion aims to expose the deception (talbīs). This is not a mere semantic dispute but a defense of the Islamic understanding of God's creative action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفقت الفلاسفة على أن للعالم صانعاً وأن العالم فعل الله وصنعته. قال الغزالى : وهذا غير متصور على مذهبهم من ثلاثة أوجه. الوجه الأول : من جهة الفاعل؛ فإن الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل، مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار، ومع العلم بالمراد. وعندهم أن العالم صدر من الله تعالى صدور المعلول من العلة، ولزم منه لزوماً ضرورياً لا يتصور من الله دفعه. قال الفلاسفة : إن الصدور بالطبع فعل وصنع. قال الغزالي : هذا فاسد؛ لما صح من قولهم : الجماد لا فعل له وإنما الفعل للحيوان، وذلك من الكليات المشهورة الصادقة؛ فإن سمى الجماد فاعلا فبالاستعارة؛ وهذا يقتضى دخول الاختيار في مفهوم معنى الفاعل. قالت الفلاسفة : إن تسمية الفاعل فاعلا تعرف من اللغة، وإلا فقد ظهر فى العقل أن ما يكون سبباً للشيء ينقسم إلى ما يكون مريداً، وإلى ما لا يكون، ووقع النزاع فى أن اسم الفاعل على كلا القسمين حقيقة، أم لا؟ ... والعرب تقول : النار تحرق، والسيف يقطع، والثلج يبرد. إلخ. قال الغزالي : إن قولهم : النار تحرق، والسيف يقطع، تعبيرات مجازية، وإنما الفعل الحقيقى ما يكون بالإرادة. قالت الفلاسفة : نحن نعنى يكون الله فاعلا أنه سبب لكل موجود سواه، ولولا وجوده لما تصور وجود العالم، فهذا ما نعنى بكونه تعالى فاعلا. فإن كان الخصم يأبى أن يسمى هذا فاعلا، فلا مشاحة في الأسامي، بعد ظهور المعنى. قال الغزالى : إن غرضنا أن هذا المعنى لا يسمى فعلا وصنعاً، وإنما المعنى بالصنع والفعل ما يصدر عن الإرادة حقيقة، وقد نفيتم حقيقة معنى الفعل، ونطقتم بلفظه تجملا بالإسلاميين، ولا يتم الدين بإطلاق الألفاظ الفارغة عن المعاني. والمقصود من هذه المسألة الكشف عن هذا التلبيس.
The philosophers agreed that the world has a maker and that the world is the act of God and His making. Al-Ghazali said: This is inconceivable according to their doctrine from three aspects. The first aspect: from the perspective of the agent; for the agent is an expression for one from whom the act originates, with will for the act by way of choice, and with knowledge of the object of will. According to them, the world originated from God, Exalted, as the effect originates from the cause, and it followed necessarily in a way that it is inconceivable for God to repel. The philosophers said: Origination by nature is act and making. Al-Ghazali said: This is corrupt; for it is established from their statement: lifeless objects have no act, rather the act belongs to the living, and this is among the famous true universals; for if a lifeless object is called an agent, it is metaphorical; and this requires the inclusion of choice in the concept of the meaning of agent. The philosophers said: The naming of the agent as 'agent' is known from language; otherwise, it has become clear in the intellect that what is a cause for a thing is divided into what is willing and what is not, and the dispute occurred about whether the name 'agent' is true upon both divisions or not? ... The Arabs say: Fire burns, the sword cuts, and snow cools, etc. Al-Ghazali said: Their saying: 'Fire burns' and 'the sword cuts' are metaphorical expressions, and the true act is what is by will. The philosophers said: We mean by God being an agent that He is the cause for every existent other than Him, and if not for His existence, the existence of the world would not be conceivable; this is what we mean by His being an agent. If the opponent refuses to call this 'agent', there is no dispute over names after the meaning is clear. Al-Ghazali said: Our purpose is that this meaning is not called act and making; rather, the meaning of making and act is what originates from will truly, and you have negated the true meaning of act and uttered its word as flattery towards the Muslims, and the religion is not established by uttering words empty of meanings. The purpose of this discussion is to expose this deceit.

5 min
Act Means Origination
Ghazali defines act as origination and applies it to the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ghazali, the term 'act' (fiʿl) necessarily implies bringing something from non-existence into existence, and therefore cannot be applied to an eternal world.

Core Idea
If the world is eternal, it was never originated, so it cannot be called an 'act'.

In this passage, Ghazali begins his second objection by focusing on the meaning of 'act' itself. He insists that 'act' is synonymous with origination—bringing something into being from nothing. Since the philosophers hold the world to be eternal and unoriginated, they cannot legitimately describe God as its agent. This lesson unpacks Ghazali's definition and the immediate implication for the philosophers' position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: فعل, إحداث, حادث, قديم.
The passage is short but dense—each word matters.
2
Identify Ghazali's definition of 'act'
Ghazali states directly: 'الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث' and then expands: 'إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود'. Commit this definition to memory.
3
Understand why the definition conflicts with an eternal world
The philosophers say the world is eternal (قديم), meaning it has no beginning. An act that brings into existence would require the thing not to exist before. Hence an eternal world cannot be the product of an act in Ghazali's sense.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Reflect on the objection
Ask yourself: If Ghazali's definition is correct, can the philosophers escape by redefining 'act'? This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثانى : من أوجه بطلان قولهم : إن الله فاعل العالم وصانعه ، من جهة الفعل نفسه. لأن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث . والعالم عندهم قديم وليس بحادث . . . ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود ، بإحداثه. قالت الفلاسفة : إن معنى الحادث [ الموجود بعد عدم ] ولا أثر للفاعل في العدم السابق ... ولا في مجموع الوجود والعدم ... فبقى أن أثره الوجود . . . ولا مانع أن يكون هذا الأثر قديما ولا يستشكل على هذا بأن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده ؛ ولأنه إن أريد أنه لا يستأنف له وجود ، فصحيح ؛ ولا يدعى الفلاسفة ذلك . وإن أريد أنه فى حال كونه موجوداً لا يكون موجوداً بموجد ، فغير صحيح ؛ لأن الإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً ، وكون المفعول موجوداً ، والموجود الممكن فى كل آنات وجوده محتاج إلى من يمسك عليه الوجود. قال الغزالي : إن الفاعل يتعلق بالفعل عندنا في حال الحدوث فقط ، لا فى ثانى حال الحدوث.
The second aspect of the invalidity of their statement that God is the agent and maker of the world: from the perspective of the act itself. Because the act is an expression for origination (īḥdāth). And the world according to them is eternal (qadīm) and not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of act is bringing a thing out of non-existence into existence, by originating it. The philosophers said: The meaning of ḥādith is [the existent after non-existence], and there is no effect of the agent in the prior non-existence ... nor in the combination of existence and non-existence ... so it remains that His effect is existence ... and there is no prevention that this effect be eternal. And do not object to this by saying that the existent cannot be brought into existence; because if it is meant that its existence is not originated anew, that is correct; and the philosophers do not claim that. And if it is meant that in the state of its being existent it is not existent by an existentiator (mūjid), that is not correct; because bringing into existence (ījād) is concurrent with the agent being an existentiator and the effect being existent, and the possible existent in every moment of its existence is in need of one who sustains existence for it. Al-Ghazali said: The agent relates to the act according to us only in the moment of origination (ḥudūth), not in the second moment after origination.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second objection, this time focusing on the act (fiʿl) itself rather than the agent. Ghazali defines an act as origination (īḥdāth), which means bringing something from non-existence to existence. But the philosophers hold the world to be eternal (qadīm), not originated (ḥādith). Therefore, calling God's eternal relation to the world an 'act' is invalid because there is no true origination. The philosophers respond by redefining the term: they say the effect (athar) of the agent is existence itself, not the transition from non-existence. This existence can be eternal; there is no need for a prior non-existence. They anticipate an objection: an existent cannot be brought into existence because that would mean it already exists. They clarify: if by 'cannot be brought into existence' one means that it does not have an origination of existence anew, that is correct and they do not claim that. But if one means that in its state of being existent it does not need an existentiator (mūjid), that is false. Rather, the possible existent at every moment of its existence requires a sustainer (mumsik) who holds existence for it. Ghazali counters by restricting the agent's relation to the act to the moment of origination only, not thereafter. This implies that if the world is eternal and has no moment of origination, the agent's act cannot relate to it, so the term 'act' is inappropriate. Thus the philosophers' conception fails even on their own grounds of continuous sustenance.

Key Terms
فعل
إحداث
حادث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'act' can refer to any kind of causing, even eternal causing.
Correction: Ghazali insists that 'act' (fiʿl) in its proper sense means origination (īḥdāth), i.e., bringing into being from non-existence. An eternal cause that does not produce something new is not called an 'act' in this technical sense.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the term 'act' (fiʿl) mean?
A) Sustaining something in existence.
B) Bringing something from non-existence to existence, i.e., origination.
C) Moving something from one place to another.
D) Producing an effect by nature.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection hinges on a strict definition: act = origination, so an eternal world cannot be an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the very meaning of 'act' implies bringing something from non-existence. Does your daily life reflect a deep awareness that you and everything around you were once nothing? How would that change your gratitude to God today?
?
If you truly believed that God's act of creation is not just a one-time event but that He continuously originates existence, how would that affect the way you see the world around you right now? Would you still take things for granted?
?
The philosophers argued that an eternal world cannot be an act. But you live in a world that is constantly changing — things begin and end every moment. How does that observation affirm that the world is God's act? What does that demand from you?

Eternal Effect and the Need for a Sustainer
Philosophers' response and Ghazali's final counter on the act's temporal restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rejoinder that existence itself is the effect of the agent and can be eternal, and Ghazali's counter that the agent's act relates only to origination, not to continued existence.

Core Idea
Even if the effect is eternal, the possible existent needs a sustainer at every moment.

Having defined act as origination, Ghazali anticipates how the philosophers might respond: they could say that the effect of the agent is simply existence, which may be eternal. Ghazali then addresses a potential objection about bringing an existent into existence, and delivers his final argument: the relation of the agent to the act is only at the moment of origination. Since the philosophers deny origination, they cannot speak of divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' response
Read from 'قالت الفلاسفة' to 'لا مانع أن يكون هذا الأثر قديما'. The philosophers argue that the agent's effect is existence itself, not the transition from non-existence. This existence can be eternal.
They are redefining the effect, not the act.
2
Understand the clarification about the existent
Read the part 'لا يستشكل على هذا ... محتاج إلى من يمسك عليه الوجود'. The philosophers clarify that an existent does not need to be brought into existence again, but it always needs a sustainer.
This is a key nuance.
3
Study Ghazali's counter
Read the final line: 'إن الفاعل يتعلق بالفعل عندنا في حال الحدوث فقط'. Ghazali restricts the agent's act to the moment of origination. If the world never originated, there is no act.
This settles the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثانى : من أوجه بطلان قولهم : إن الله فاعل العالم وصانعه ، من جهة الفعل نفسه. لأن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث . والعالم عندهم قديم وليس بحادث . . . ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود ، بإحداثه. قالت الفلاسفة : إن معنى الحادث [ الموجود بعد عدم ] ولا أثر للفاعل في العدم السابق ... ولا في مجموع الوجود والعدم ... فبقى أن أثره الوجود . . . ولا مانع أن يكون هذا الأثر قديما ولا يستشكل على هذا بأن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده ؛ ولأنه إن أريد أنه لا يستأنف له وجود ، فصحيح ؛ ولا يدعى الفلاسفة ذلك . وإن أريد أنه فى حال كونه موجوداً لا يكون موجوداً بموجد ، فغير صحيح ؛ لأن الإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً ، وكون المفعول موجوداً ، والموجود الممكن فى كل آنات وجوده محتاج إلى من يمسك عليه الوجود. قال الغزالي : إن الفاعل يتعلق بالفعل عندنا في حال الحدوث فقط ، لا فى ثانى حال الحدوث.
The second aspect of the invalidity of their statement that God is the agent and maker of the world: from the perspective of the act itself. Because the act is an expression for origination (īḥdāth). And the world according to them is eternal (qadīm) and not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of act is bringing a thing out of non-existence into existence, by originating it. And if it is meant that in the state of its being existent it is not existent by an existentiator (mūjid), that is not correct; because bringing into existence (ījād) is concurrent with the agent being an existentiator and the effect being existent, and the possible existent in every moment of its existence is in need of one who sustains existence for it. Al-Ghazali said: The agent relates to the act according to us only in the moment of origination (ḥudūth), not in the second moment after origination.

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second objection, this time focusing on the act (fiʿl) itself rather than the agent. Ghazali defines an act as origination (īḥdāth), which means bringing something from non-existence to existence. But the philosophers hold the world to be eternal (qadīm), not originated (ḥādith). Therefore, calling God's eternal relation to the world an 'act' is invalid because there is no true origination. Thus the philosophers' conception fails even on their own grounds of continuous sustenance.

Misconception: Some might think that if the world is eternal, it does not need any cause because it always existed.
Correction: The philosophers hold that the possible existent (the world) needs a sustainer at every moment, even if it is eternal. So they do affirm a constant causal dependence.

Objection on the Identity of Possibility and Existence in the First
Ghazali objects to identifying possibility and existence in the First.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical objection to the philosophers' claim that the First's necessary existence is identical to its essence.

Core Idea
If possibility and existence come from different sources, they cannot be identical.

Ghazali here attacks a core tenet of the philosophers: that the First Principle's necessary existence is identical to its essence. By asking whether possibility and existence can be one if they derive from different origins, he exposes a potential contradiction. This short but powerful objection forces the philosophers to defend their identification of essence and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection, hypothetical response, counter-question.
2
Understand the logical structure
The objection hinges on the distinction between 'from essence' and 'from another'. Possibility is said to be from essence, existence from another. If so, how can they be identical?
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Consider the counter-question
Ghazali turns the tables: the philosophers claim necessary existence is identical to existence. But if the First's existence is from itself, why isn't its existence necessary?
The counter-question exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ومداخل الاعتراض على مثلها لا تنحصر منها : كونه ممكن الوجود ، عين وجوده ، أم غيره ؟ . . فإن قيل : إمكان الوجود له من ذاته ، ووجوده من غيره ، فكيف يكون ماله من ذاته، وماله من غيره واحداً . . ؟ قلنا : فكيف قلتم : إن وجوب الوجود ، هو عين الوجود ؟
Then he said: And the avenues of objection to such things are not limited, among them: whether it (the First) is possible of existence, identical to its existence or other than it? If it is said: possibility of existence belongs to it from its essence, and its existence from other than it, then how can that which is from its essence and that which is from other than it be one? We said: Then how do you say that necessary existence is identical to existence?
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the philosophers' view regarding the First Principle. The objection questions the relationship between possibility and existence in the First. The philosophers claim that the First's necessary existence is identical to existence itself. The objector argues: If possibility of existence belongs to the First from its own essence, while its existence comes from another (i.e., a cause), then how can these two aspects—one from essence and one from another—be one and the same? The response is a counter-question: 'Then how do you say that necessary existence is identical to existence?' This highlights an inconsistency in the philosophers' position.

Key Terms
ممكن الوجود
وجوب الوجود
عين الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies God's existence.
Correction: The objection questions the logical consistency of the philosophers' identification of essence and existence, not God's existence.

Checkpoint
What is the central issue in this objection?
A) Whether the First is possible or necessary
B) Whether the First's possibility and existence can be identical if they have different sources
C) Whether the world is created
D) Whether the First has intellect

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection shows that if possibility and existence arise from distinct origins, they cannot be identical, undermining the philosophers' claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to claim that God's existence is simply 'necessary,' how would you respond to Ghazali's objection that possibility and existence come from different sources? Can you defend the philosophers' position without contradiction? What does this reveal about your own understanding of God's nature?
?
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own language to expose an inconsistency. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that, when examined closely, contain internal contradictions? Are you willing to have your assumptions challenged as Ghazali challenges the philosophers?
?
This passage forces us to think about whether God's attributes can be identical to His essence. Does it matter to you whether God's existence is 'from Himself' in a way that is distinct from His attributes? How does this affect your worship and reliance on Him?

The Philosophers' Definition of the First Cause
Philosophers define the First Cause as an uncaused cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers define the First Cause as an uncaused cause, in contrast to a free agent.

Core Idea
The First Cause is not a chooser but the source of all existence.

This passage clarifies what the philosophers mean by 'Maker' or 'First Cause'. They reject the usual human analogy of a free agent who begins actions at will. Instead, they conceive of a necessary cause that has no cause for its own existence but is the cause of everything else.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the contrast with 'free agent'.
The philosophers begin by stating what they do not mean.
2
Contrast with free agent
Compare the philosophers' concept to the human example of a tailor or weaver. Why is that example insufficient?
They emphasize that God is not a temporal agent.
3
Grasp the concept of uncaused cause
Understand the phrase 'no cause for His existence, and He is the cause for the existence of other than Him' as the core definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : نحن لم نعن بالصانع فاعلا مختاراً، يفعل بعد أن لم يكن يفعل ، كما يشاهد في أصناف الفاعلين من الخياط والنساج ، بل نعنى به علة العالم ، على معنى أنه لا علة لوجوده ، وهو علة لوجود غيره وثبوت موجود لا علة لوجوده يقوم عليه البرهان القطعى ؛ فإنا نقول : موجودات العالم إما أن يكون لها علة ، أو لاعلة لها . فإن كان لها علة ، فتلك العلة لها علة أم لا ؟ وكذا القول في علة العلة : فإما أن يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية ، وهو محال . وإن كان العالم موجوداً لا علة له ، فقد ثبت مبدأ لا علة له . وإما أن تنتهى إلى طرف، فالأخير علة أولى لا علة لوجودها . فنسميها بالمبدأ الأول .
The philosophers said: We do not mean by the Maker a free agent, who acts after not having acted, as is seen in the kinds of agents such as the tailor and the weaver. Rather, we mean by Him the cause of the world, in the sense that there is no cause for His existence, and He is the cause for the existence of other than Him. The establishment of an existent with no cause for its existence is proven by decisive demonstration. For we say: The existents of the world either have a cause or have no cause. If they have a cause, does that cause itself have a cause or not? And similarly for the cause of the cause. Either the chain goes on to infinity, which is impossible. Or the world is an existent with no cause—then a principle with no cause has been established. Or the chain terminates at an extreme, and that last is a first cause with no cause for its existence. We call it the First Principle.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage states the philosophers' definition of the First Cause and their classic argument from infinite regress. They first clarify that they do not conceive of God as a freely choosing agent who begins to act after non-action, like human artisans. Instead, they define Him as the cause of the world in the sense that He has no cause for His own existence, while everything else derives existence from Him. They claim that a decisive proof establishes the existence of such an uncaused entity. The proof proceeds by examining all existents: they either have a cause or do not. If they have a cause, that cause itself must be examined—does it have a cause? The chain cannot go on infinitely, for infinite regress is impossible. Therefore, either we stop at a first uncaused cause (the First Principle), or we accept that the world itself is uncaused (which also yields a first principle). The argument is presented as a disjunctive syllogism leading necessarily to the existence of a first uncaused cause.

Key Terms
علة
المبدأ الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny God's will and power.
Correction: They do not deny; they define God's will differently—as a necessary cause rather than contingent choice.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers mean by the First Cause?
A) A free agent who acts after non-action
B) An uncaused cause of the world
C) A necessary being with attributes
D) The first mover

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define the First Cause as an uncaused cause, not a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is merely an uncaused cause and not a free agent who chooses to act, does that change how you relate to Him? Would you still pray to a cause that does not choose? How does this definition affect your personal understanding of God's relationship to you?
?
The philosophers reject the idea of God as a free agent like a tailor or weaver. Have you ever subconsciously imagined God as a cosmic craftsman who decides to create? After reading this passage, can you identify any assumption you held about God that might actually align more with the philosophers' view than with Islamic teachings? What do you need to correct?
?
The passage says God has no cause for His existence and is the cause for everything else. If everything depends on Him, but He depends on nothing, what does that mean for your own sense of dependency on other people or things? Are there created causes you rely on more than the First Cause?

The Infinite Regress Proof for a First Cause
Philosophers present the infinite regress proof for a first cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that all existents must lead to a first uncaused cause through the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible, so a first cause must exist.

Now we see the proof that establishes the existence of an uncaused cause. The philosophers present a disjunctive syllogism: every existent either has a cause or not; if it has a cause, that cause either has a cause, ad infinitum; since infinite regress is impossible, we must end at a first cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical flow: either cause or no cause, and the chain of causes.
The proof is a disjunctive syllogism.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find: (a) infinite regress, (b) the world itself is uncaused, (c) the chain stops at a first cause.
Only one possibility is accepted: a first cause.
3
Understand why infinite regress is rejected
The philosophers state that infinite regress is impossible (محال). Ask: why is it impossible? The passage assumes it as a premise.
This is a key point in their argument.
4
See the conclusion
The proof concludes that a first cause (First Principle) exists, which is uncaused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : نحن لم نعن بالصانع فاعلا مختاراً، يفعل بعد أن لم يكن يفعل ، كما يشاهد في أصناف الفاعلين من الخياط والنساج ، بل نعنى به علة العالم ، على معنى أنه لا علة لوجوده ، وهو علة لوجود غيره وثبوت موجود لا علة لوجوده يقوم عليه البرهان القطعى ؛ فإنا نقول : موجودات العالم إما أن يكون لها علة ، أو لاعلة لها . فإن كان لها علة ، فتلك العلة لها علة أم لا ؟ وكذا القول في علة العلة : فإما أن يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية ، وهو محال . وإن كان العالم موجوداً لا علة له ، فقد ثبت مبدأ لا علة له . وإما أن تنتهى إلى طرف، فالأخير علة أولى لا علة لوجودها . فنسميها بالمبدأ الأول .
The philosophers said: We do not mean by the Maker a free agent, who acts after not having acted, as is seen in the kinds of agents such as the tailor and the weaver. Rather, we mean by Him the cause of the world, in the sense that there is no cause for His existence, and He is the cause for the existence of other than Him. The establishment of an existent with no cause for its existence is proven by decisive demonstration. For we say: The existents of the world either have a cause or have no cause. If they have a cause, does that cause itself have a cause or not? And similarly for the cause of the cause. Either the chain goes on to infinity, which is impossible. Or the world is an existent with no cause—then a principle with no cause has been established. Or the chain terminates at an extreme, and that last is a first cause with no cause for its existence. We call it the First Principle.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage states the philosophers' definition of the First Cause and their classic argument from infinite regress. They first clarify that they do not conceive of God as a freely choosing agent who begins to act after non-action, like human artisans. Instead, they define Him as the cause of the world in the sense that He has no cause for His own existence, while everything else derives existence from Him. They claim that a decisive proof establishes the existence of such an uncaused entity. The proof proceeds by examining all existents: they either have a cause or do not. If they have a cause, that cause itself must be examined—does it have a cause? The chain cannot go on infinitely, for infinite regress is impossible. Therefore, either we stop at a first uncaused cause (the First Principle), or we accept that the world itself is uncaused (which also yields a first principle). The argument is presented as a disjunctive syllogism leading necessarily to the existence of a first uncaused cause.

Key Terms
علة
المبدأ الأول
التسلسل
البرهان القطعى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can't the chain of causes go on infinitely?
A) Because the universe is finite
B) Because infinite regress is impossible
C) Because God must exist
D) Because causes must have a beginning

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress proof concludes that a first uncaused cause, the First Principle, must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your daily life reflect a reliance on the First Cause, or do you act as if your existence is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly lived in the awareness that everything depends on a Cause that has no cause?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible. Where in your own thinking—about purpose, meaning, or accountability—have you been assuming an infinite chain of explanations without anchoring it in the First Principle? How will you stop that regress today?
?
If the First Principle has no cause, then your entire existence is contingent upon Him. Does that thought make you uncomfortable or bring you peace? What commitment does this demand from you tonight?

The Philosophers' Alternative Proof from Possibility and Necessity
The philosophers present a proof that does not rely on infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' alternative argument for a necessary being that avoids relying on infinite regress.

Core Idea
Either a cause is necessary in itself, or the chain of possibles demands a necessary external cause.

This passage presents a second proof from the philosophers, one that does not depend on the problematic concept of infinite regress. They propose a simple disjunction: every cause in the chain is either necessary in itself or possible. The conclusion is that a necessary being must exist, either as one of the causes or as the external cause of the whole chain. Let's examine the logic carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: if... then...
2
Identify the two possibilities
The philosophers consider two cases for each cause: necessary in itself (واجب) or possible (ممكن). Understand the distinction.
3
Trace the conclusion
If a cause is necessary, no further cause is needed. If all are possible, the whole needs an external cause, which must be necessary.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this argument avoids infinite regress. It doesn't require a chain; it just classifies each cause.
Ghazali will criticize this argument later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالت الفلاسفة : لا حاجة بنا إلى استعمال التسلسل في إثبات الصانع ؛ فإن في وسعنا أن نقول : كل واحدة من آحاد العلل ممكنة في نفسها ؟ أو واجبة ؟ فإن كانت واجبة فهى بغير حاجة إلى علة . وإن كانت ممكنة فالكل موصوف بالإمكان ، وكل ممكن فهو بحاجة إلى علة خارجة عن ذاته ، والموجود الخارج عن ذات الممكن ليس إلا الواجب
If the philosophers say: We have no need to employ infinite regress in proving the Maker; for we can say: Each one of the individual causes is either possible in itself or necessary? If it is necessary, then it is without need of a cause. If it is possible, then the whole is described as possible, and everything possible is in need of a cause external to its essence, and the existent external to the essence of the possible is nothing but the necessary.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage introduces an alternative proof from the philosophers that bypasses the issue of infinite regress. They propose to examine each cause in the chain individually: either it is necessary in itself (واجب) or possible in itself (ممكن). If any cause is necessary, it requires no external cause. If all causes are possible, then the entire set of causes is described as possible, and everything possible needs a cause outside its own essence. The only thing that can exist outside the essences of all possible beings is the Necessary Being (الواجب). Thus, the proof concludes that a necessary being must exist to account for the chain of possible causes.

Key Terms
واجب
ممكن
علة خارجية
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires an infinite regress of causes to work.
Correction: The philosophers specifically avoid infinite regress by examining each cause's nature individually.

Checkpoint
In the philosophers' alternative proof, what is the status of a cause that is necessary in itself?
A) It needs a cause
B) It is part of a regress
C) It has no need of a cause
D) It is possible

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof uses a disjunction to argue that a necessary being must exist, either as a self-sufficient cause or as the external cause of all possible causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the philosophers’ proof is valid, then your very existence depends on a necessary being outside yourself. Does your life reflect that dependency in every moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
Consider the possibility that you are a 'possible being'—one that could have not existed. How does that shape your sense of purpose and your relationship with the One who alone is necessary?
?
The proof ends with the conclusion that the Necessary Being must exist. But do you actually live as though that Being is present and aware of you? What would change today if you truly believed that He is the only necessary reality?
